THE RED Page
THE CIRCUMNAVIGATOR.
. . . The ruder Italy laid bare
By that keen Searcher of the Seas
Whose tempest-battling, never-baffled keel
Left half our planet little to reveal;
But restless roaming everywhere,
Zig-zagged the vast Pacific, as he prest.
With god-like patience, his benignant quest;
True hero-god, who realised the notion
Its races feign of mythic Maui still,
And plucked up, with a giant might of will,
A hundred islands from Oblivion's ocean !
Sea-king and sage—staunch huntsman of pure
Fame,
Beating the waste of waters for his game,
Untrodden shores or tribes without a name ;
That nothing in an island’s shape,
Mist-muffled peak or faint cloud-cape,
Might his determined thoughtful glance escape;
No virgin lands be left unknown,
Where future Englands might be sown.
And nations noble as his own !'
ALFRED DOMETT.
CAPTAIN COOK
IT happily occurred to Arthur Kitson, the
author of the latest life of Captain
James Cook (Murray, per Dymock’s
Library, Sydney), to discard the various
biographies and go direct to the Admiralty
fecords for his information. The result is a
valuable study of the great discoverer that
sheds new light upon his character and his
career. In Kitson’s view, Cook has two claims
to fame ; in addition to his rank as discoverer,
he was the first successfully to combat that
dread enemy of the sailor in those days of
long voyages—the scurvy. Prior to Cook’s
triumphant demonstration of his ability td
keep his crew in health, scurvy made
tremendous ravages among seamen.
Cook’s method was to avail himself
of every possible opportunity to vary
the food of his crew with fresh vegetables ;
at every country he came to his first care
was to search the place for “ wild celery,”
“scurvy grass," or any possible substitute.
In Maoriland he brewed a spruce-beer that
seems to have been a valuable preventative
of the disease. This care was but one aspect
of Cook’s incessant attention to detail.
During the whole of the first voyage, with
the exception of the terrible outbreak of
malaria, responsible for almost universal
sickness and a number of deaths, at Batavia,
where Cook had to put in to repair his
damaged ship, the “Endeavour” had had
only five cases of scurvy—“ a report such as
had never before been made by one of His
Majesty’s ships after a voyage of any con-
siderable length. This was most un-
doubtedly due to the careful forethought and
constant attention of her commander to
everything, no matter how trivial, which he
thought would tend to "prevent the horrible
scourge from getting a foothold on board.”
On his three voyages, during which he re-
mained away for years, only two deaths
occurred from this disease; while it was a
usual occurrence for the ships of the navy to
be put out of service owing to losses from
this disease even on a voyage across the
Atlantic.
Working his way up from apprentice in
the mercantile marine to the position of
mate m a Whitby collier, Cook volunteered for
the navy as A.B. He knew that experienced
seamen, despite the efforts of the “press,”
were difficult to obtain, and that men of a
certain amount of nautical knowledge and
good character could soon raise themselves
above the rank of ordinary seamen. He was
then 27 years old. A month after his entry
into the navy he was rated as master’s mate,
and two years later became master. And
Cook had a varied service in the navy before
he became an explorer. He was present at
the siege of Quebec, and rendered such note-
worthy service as a surveyor that the
Admiralty thereafter marked him out for
chart-making. So it was that when it was
decided to send an expedition to the South
Seas to observe the transit of Venus Cook
was the obvious man to take command. Of
the exactitude and the thoroughness of the
charts he made as the result of his circum-
navigation of the two islands of Maoriland
subsequent explorers speak in the highest
terms. Many of Cook’s charts are even now
the basis of those actually in use at the
Admiralty, and in these there is much that
has been found to require no alteration. And
these charts were made under the most un-
favorable circumstances. An instance of the
difficulties encountered in the navigation of
the Great Barrier Reef is given by Cook in
his journal :
We sailed upon this coast near 400 leagues by
the lead, with sometimes one, sometimes two and
three boats ahead to direct us, and yet, with all
this precaution, we were often obliged to anchor
with all sails standing to prevent running ashore.
<252?
Cook made no claim to being the dis-
coverer of Australia. His journal expressly
states that on leaving Maorilandhe resolved
to sail westward “until he fell in with the
east coast of New Holland, and then to
follow that coast to the northward or what
other direction it might take, until we arrive
at its northern extremity ; and if it shoulc
be found impracticable, then to endeavor to
fall in with the lands or islands discoverec
by Quiros.” His only claim, and a very jus;
one, is that he discovered and traced the
course of the eastern coast. Cook took
possession of this eastern coast “by the
name of ‘New Wales’,” though Admira
Wharton says that in the King’s copy of
Cook’s Journal and in the Admiralty copy
the ( name is given as “New South Wales.’
Subsequently, in a letter, he terms it New
South Wales. Kitson observes with refer-
ence to the elaim of De Quiros to the dis-
covery of Australia :
Every information available points to De
Quiros’s discovery being the Island of Santo, anc
not Australia, and it is now universally accepted
that that island is where he turned, back, owing
to a mutiny on board his ship.
Kitson treats in detail Cook’s vain effort
to find the alleged North-West passage from
the Pacific to the Atlantic, and rightly lays
stress upon the valuable explorations made
by Cook in the Arctic and Antarctic regions.
The navigator appears from this biography
as a brave, plain, conscientious man, with a
great command of detail and a remarkable
pertinacity in verifying his conclusions. Of
the personality of the man we know
little save that he was esteemed alike by his
crew and the scientific world. The extra-
ordinary estimation in which he was held
was shown by the safe conducts that Spain,
the United States and France, then all at
war with Britain, issued in favor of the great
discoverer. “In consideration of his services
to mankind,” instructions were given by
these powers to their fleets to regard him as
a neutral.
On the point of the ultimate fate of the
“Endeavour,” Kitson would do well to
accept guidance from Robert McNab’s valu-
able conclusions in his recent work, Muri-
hiku; but otherwise the biography is one
that, abridged, should be of value in our
schools.
FOR AUSTRALASIAN PRESSMEN
■^ rom John L. Given’s Making a Newspaper
(G. Bell, London, per Sydney Book Club) it
appears to be an excellent thing not to be an
American reporter. Also, the heaviest chunk of
luck that can be hurled at anybody is not to be a
New York editor. Also, also, it is quite worth while
for the Australasian pressman to stay at home.
J. L. Given’s picture of the American paper in
the making is elaborate and—to Australian eyes—•
misguidedly enthusiastic. He has a touching
faith that the American paper is somehow worth
while.
In the pursuit, capture and prompt exhibition
of news, it is true the American press can giye
points to the world, though it has a large and in-
definite idea as to what constitutes news. In
New York news is systematically watched for at
recognised news-centres, of which police-stations
and law-courts are merely the most obvious of a
long list, by a co-operative association which
maintains a staff of sixty reporters whose business
is to furnish news jointly to all the members of
the concern. Ihe “stories” are then re-investi-
gated or re-written by each paper’s separate staff.
And not only is news so systematised, but certain
kinds of news can be confidently expected at cer-
tain times. For example, Monday morning is the
recognised time for the receipt of a crop of suicide
“stories.” After a “drunk ”on Saturday night,
the laborer, appalled at the prospect of another
week’s work, usually cuts his throat. In summer,
suicides are always expected after a particu-
larly hot night, while oh especially cold morn-
ings extra space is reserved for fires. On a
big “story,” where a number of reporters from
competing papers are assembled, they co-operate
by exchanging news, and the reporter who plays
false with other news-gatherers, accepts their
gleanings and remains silent about his own, is
doomed. He becomes a marked man, and everv
other reporter will strive to the utmost to land
him in a series of defeats ; and with so many
against the one the defeats are sure to come.
Then he makes his paper ridiculous and loses his
job. Incidentally, it may be noted that Given’s
book is really an elaborate recital of the innumer-
able ways in which an American reporter can lose
his job. His is a precarious profession. But
though apparently no particle of disgrace attaches
to a reporter who has lost his job, it must fre-
quently be confusing to a reporter to remember at
any moment what particular paper he is working
for.
OS?
“There is no reporter who is not aware that
every time he goes out on an assignment, he runs
a risk of putting himself in disfavor with the
editor by whose grace he keeps his place, and no
editor who does not realise that each article that
passes through his hands may prove his undoing.
Once a year city newspapers have a house-clean-
mg, when all the reporters who have failed to
prove their worth are dismissed. . . . Having
been in an office three years, a man is pretty safe,
as long as he keeps up his general standard, and
avoids a bad defeat. . . . The newspaper business
is one wherein young men shine, and, having
faded to fight their way well towards the front,
the middle-aged find small demand for their serl
vices. The fittest survive only until they are
unfit, and sentiment is not allowed to interfere
with the process of exclusion. Further, there are
in newspaper offices no easy berths for men of
long service; advancement brings, not only
heavier responsibilities, but harder work and
often longer hours as well.”
And it isn’t much of a job even when the
reporter keeps it.. He is not allowed much time
to dawdle. On an evening paper, the main force
of reporters begin work at 8 a.m., and as fast as a
man finishes one assignment he gets another. The
city editor’s schedule makes no allowance for
lunch. The “star” reporter is not left idle on
days when there is no news of importance. “The
war-correspondent in times of peace is glad to go
to fires, and he gets his full share of school-board
meetings.” On the sensational press, the reporter
must provide sensations. “Told to look into a
suicide, it does not occur to him to get the facts
in plain sight, or where all is not clear to let the
evidence lead to a dull verdict. First, he endea-
vors to squeeze the suicide into a semblance
of a murder. Failing in this, or the facts
absolutely prohibiting the attempt, he tries to
find something that will make the causes
leading to the suicide a mystery. Not meeting
with success in this direction, he asks whether it
is not true that some other members of the
suicide’s family have killed themselves. Still
failing to score, he goes over a list of recent self-
inflicted deaths in an effort to discover the exist-
ence of a ‘suicide club,’ and, unsuccessful here,
nerves himself, and perhaps lands an even more
fanciful tale.” But, you will say, this is merely
the yellow press. Given points out that the
sedate press is compelled to join in the chorus
when the yellow journals shriek. “ The reporters
and city editors of the sedate publications must
make concessions unless they can present counter-
attractions, for the managing editors and
will not forever have their demands for explana-*
tions set aside by the words ‘ yellow fake.’ At
the end of the month they remember only that
they have had to call the city editor and those
under him to account many, many times. Because
of this the reporters of the sedate papers are under
constant temptation.” And it may be added that
the American reporter usually falls.
And the rewards are, after all, few and mean.
For no journalist can ever hope to save enough by
newspaper work to start a paper of his own in a
large city. The capital required is quite prohibi-
tive. In all the United States, Given estimates
that not more than three hundred editors receive
£lOOO a year or over. An experienced war cor-
respondent who has a reputation receives about
£2O a week and his expenses while at the front.
When he is at the back, he reports street accidents.
The inexperienced reporter starts at £3 a week,
and after twelve months he gets £4 a week. The
experienced reporter on a New York daily gets
from £5 to £7 a week. City editors’ salaries range
from £BOO to £l5OO a year, and editorial writers
average £lOOO, art critics £4OO, and dramatic
critics £6OO per annum. A good illustrator gets
£8 a week. So, to Australasian views, taking into
account the lower purchasing value of money in
the United States, the nerve-worry, the soulless
business methods, the swift and ever-present
liability to lose his job, the callous weeding-out of
the middle-aged men, it hardly seems worth
while.
The morals of the American reporter are as
expeditious as his methods. “Getting a man to
sav something which, there is reason to believe,
he might later wish to have left unsaid, a reporter’s
cue is to retire before the change of mind comes ;
but it does not do for him to make a dash for the
door, for this would not unlikely lead the object
of his questions to retract words or declare that
he does not wish to be quoted. Instead, the
reporter should change the subject, play the
admirer himself, and make his exit gracefully,
leaving the other man to wake up when he seeB
what he has said staring at him in print.”
Given tells many stories which incidentally shed
light upon American newspaper ethics. A news-
gatherer, sent to report a wedding, had his sus-
picions aroused by the anxious manner in which
the relatives of the wealthy bridegroom insisted
that the reporters should occupy front seats. He
slipped out and watched the doors, and found
they were guarded by a corps of private
detectives, who turned away a young woman who
tried to enter. “ Arpund the corner, the reporter
got her story, and, While it was scandal, it wa3
news of the kind his paper wanted, and he scored
a beat that caused much comment.” On another
occasion, a woman reporter was sent to spy upon a
woman who was under suspicion as the murderess
of a man. As it was discovered that the alleged
murderess intended to be present at the man’s
funeral, the reporter garbed herself as a mourner
and rode in the same carriage with the suspected
woman. Given does not say what use she made
of her interview. America seems a pleasant place
to live out of.
The newspaper “ story ” differs strikingly from
the short-story. A pure news story begins with
the climax, and works backward. It begins
always with the most important fact, and the
various incidents are then taken up in the order
of their importance. The inessentials come last.
“ A story constructed on this plan has two strong
points.. It can be cut off almost anywhere, if
space is at a premium ; and, appearing in the
paper, it does not hold the reader in suspense and
demand a complete reading.” A news “story”
must explain as it goes—ip case either it or its
reader doesn’t go very far. Style isn’t necessary.
“ On some papers there is a rule that no sentence
should be printed which occupies more than seven
lines of type.”
Given has unwittingly done the Australian
pressman good service in stating these disconcert-
ing facts.
♦
It appears that the Hairy Dysoxylon is missing.
In the latest issue (Yol. 8, Part 61 of The Forest
Flora of New South Wales, -T. H. Maiden asks for
more information about this rare tree, and hopes
that it will be rediscovered. Its lurking place is
likely to be about the Northern Rivers of N.S.W.
There is no reward offered, except the satisfac-
tion of running the Hairy Dysoxylon —alias
Dysoxvlon Becklerianum—to earth, and adding
to our knowledge of the flora of Australia. The
same part of a highly useful national work gives
much interesting information about the mulga.
It was so called from the mulga, or long, narrow
shield made by the aborigines out of acacia wood.
“It is a matter for regret that so valuable a
fodder plant should require to be cut for timber
at all. Mulga should never be cut down —it
should only be. pollarded or lopped. One reason
why the tree is becoming extinct is because the
seedlings are eaten out by stock. Mulga is likely to
become an important factor in the settlement of
the interior, as it forms good fodder for camels.
It appears to be the most universally distributed
fodder tree in the arid parts of Australia. In
former years mulgas have been destroyed by
thousands because the precaution to leave at least
one leading branch uncut was not observed. If
mulga is cut off at the stump it will not throw out
new growth. In this respect it differs from nearly
every other Western fodder-producing tree.” < A
commendable feature of this scientific description
of our forest flora is the large number of photo-
graphs of the trees in situ that are now supplied,
though photographs of branches and blossoms (in
addition to the lithographed drawings of leaves
and seeds) would add further to the interest of
the Australian who wishes to know his bush.
It seems about time to call kindly attention to
the Jujube Advertiser upon this page. What
relation Jujubes have to Book Worms isn’t very
clear; but the J ujube Advertiser pays in advance—
about a century in advance—and he evidently
doesn’t mean to be dislodged from his valuable
corner. However, the “Red Page’’ appeals to
him to get his weird map of our internal regions
re-drawn and brought up to date—or if
it’s a cabbage or a bunion or a bunyip,
we merely ask him to make it a little
more artistic cabbage or a bunion or a bunyip.
Merely planting it occasionally on end doesn’t
satisfy our artistic cravings. Sometimes —after a
good dinner—that diagram almost makes us want
a Jujube—which is probably the deep design of
the Jujube Advertiser. By the way, though this
advertisement hasn’t been asked for, the Jujubes
are very] good Jujubes. They might, conceivably,
do a Critic good, and Poets have been known to
thrive on them.
VERSES OF THE SLUMS
THE LODGING-HOUSE FIRE.
My birthday—yesterday,
Its hours were twenty-four ;
Four hours I lived lukewarm,
And killed a score.
I woke eight chimes and rose,
Came to our fire below,
Then sat four hours and watched
Its sullen glow.
Then out four hours I walked,
The lukewarm four I live,
And felt no other joy
Than air can give.
My mind did know no thought,
It knew my life too well:
’Twas hell before, behind,
And round me hell.
'Back to that fire again,
Ten hours I watch it now,
And take to bed dim eyes
And fever’s brow.
Ten hours I give to sleep,
More than my need I know ;
But I escape my mind
And that fire’s glow.
For listen : it is death
To watch .that fire’s glow ;
For, as it burns more red,
Men paler grow,
O better in foul room
That’s warm, make life away,
Than homeless out of doors,
Cold night and day. ...
I close my eyes and swear
It shall not wield its power ;
No use. I wake to find
A murdered hour. . . .
I count us thirty men.
Huddled from Winter’s blow, N
Helpless to move'away
From that fire’s glow. . , .
But all my day.is waste,
I live in lukewarm four
And make a red coke fire
Poison a score.
SATURDAY NIGHT IN THE SLUMS.
Why do I stare at faces, why,
Nor watch the happy children more ?
Since Age he hath a blackened eye,
And that grey hair is stained with gore.
For an old woman passed, and she
Would hide her face when I did stare;
But when she turns that lace from me,
There’s clotted blood in her grey hair.
Aye, here was hell last night to play.
The scream of children, murder cries;
When I came forth at early day,
I saw old Age with blackened eyes.
Why do I stare at people so,
Nor watch the little children more,
If one such brutal passions show,
And joy is all the other’s store 1
O for the shot in some fierce land,
A sword or dagger firmly held:
No brutal kick, no mauling hand,
No horrors of the partly killed.. .
I will give children my best hours,
And of their simple ways will sing:
Just as a bird heeds less old flowers.
And sings his best to buds in spring.
(From “The Soul's Destroyer ” and “ New
Poems,” by W. H. Davies.)

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Mother.
Last spring, when paddocks were scorched and
sere
Like a desert land, with hurried pace
He came to conquer the red Bush fire
That raged and spread round the stricken place.
’Twas deep in the heart of the Old Man Drought
When first I saw the stranger’s face.
But when the rains filled pond and tank,
“Good-bye ” he waved at the Sweetheart’s Gate
And rode, and the grey went galloping fast
Over the hills to his greener State.
In the twilight lone for a man and horse
Now I watch and watch and I wait and wait.
I know when wild birds build their nests
The weak must wait, though the strong may
stray;
And lovers are restless, they wander far ;
But they all come back some day, some day.
Oh ! He will come back, though others doubt:
“He loved,” they laugh, “and he rode away.”
’Tis Autumn now. The teeming earth
Yields fruit and flowers and yellow grain.
Ah, God ! And I, I too bear fruit,
But crushed, blood red, with a heart of pain.
“Love,” once he said, “ made all things grow
As innocent as the sun and rain.”
The season’s ripe, and many a tree
With heavy fruit the orchards claim.
Deep in my heart they’re ripening still,
His kisses of fire and his words of flame.
But baby, baby at my breast,
Your birth alone brings sin and shame,
vio.
LOUIS ESSON.
Returning.
Hey, for the land o’ the sun once more !
And hey, for the unfenced run once more I
For the western ways,
And the long, bright days,
And the crack of the hunter’s gun once more !
It’s back to the old life soon we’ll go ;
To the nights of the full round moon we’ll go ;
To the camp-fires red,
And the shearing-shed,
To the huts by the dark lagoon we’ll go.
It’s hey, for the woolpacks high again !
And it’s hey, for the purple sky again ;
For the creaking wains,
On the salt-bush plains,
And the granite hill-slopes dry again !
, A horse, and the rough bush tracks once more,
And a brush with the thin-shanked blacks once
more !
The rush and din
Of the yarding-in,
On the half-tamed brumbies’ backs once more !
A horse, and a dog, and a gun for me !
The stars, and the moon, and sun for me !
The ewes and lambs,
And the deep-voiced rams,
And the open plains of the run for me !
It’s a long road out by the West again,
Till we come to the brown hill-crest again—
With book and pack
We are making back—
Then hey, for the life that’s best again !
vio.
C. G. A. COLLES.
The Midnight Train.
I awoke to a sound that seemed
To have died away in the rain.
I told myself I had dreamed,
And had turned to slumber again,
When an engine-whistle blew,
Faint as an infant’s cry,
And I waited, as sleepers do,
To hear the train rush by.
A moan as of wind in trees
Sullenly swathed the gloom
Merging by slow degrees
Into a hollow Boom !
As down the line she swept,
She sent a call through the dark ;
And the drowsing echoes leapt
To life and shouted “ Hark /”
Nearer she came a-roar,
Her headlight’s wicked glare
Lighting the road before
With hard, insistent stare.
Our little cottage rocked
In the cataclysmic roll
Of harsh sounds interlocked
In one discordant whole.
Clatter! and Clash 1 and Clang /
Rolling and thudding blows,
Steel on iron rang
Like meeting of galloping foes.
All the world seemed filled
By sound that silenced the rain—
Thunderous sounds that thrilled,
And, circling, crashed again.
A child awoke and sobbed,
In terror of the sound,
As the long train leaped and throbbed,
Shaking the solid ground.
Again the whistle blew,
Warning the world a-dream
The midnight train was due—
Goliath of steel and steam.
The tail-lights passed, and we heard
Tap-tap! on the distant rail;
Afar a lonely bird
Sent through the night a wail.
The weather outside was wild,
Heavy with wind and rain,
And the mother soothed her child,
“ It was only the midnight train.”
UAOBILAND.
WILL LAWSON.

THE LATEST GRIEVANCE.
Whilst concessions were being asked for it was a matter for regret that South Australia had seized the opportunity of imposing regulations against New
South Wales fruit of a distinctly harassing nature. If they showed more conciliation and feeling i n regard to New South Wales trade they would probably
receive more consideration from the people of this State.— N.S.W. Premier Wade in his Legislative Assembly, moaning because Australian States refuse to take
infected fruit from N.S.W. orchards.
AH WADE: “WHAFFOR? YOU WELLY BAD. DIDN’T I CALL YOU LIAR THIFF? YET YOU NO LIKE ME! NOW YOU
WON’T TAKE MY DIRTY FLUIT. OH, YOU WELLY BAD!”

How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
bxd to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Sjteciul Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right, to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing xvith Australian subjects, and not exceeding
hoc columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
Tiie Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed Bat no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it 'will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —ivill be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions ivill be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 7, 1907.
THIS BOOM AND THE OTHERS.
Last week, as Treasurer in the Wade
Thomas Waddell delivered the N. S. Wales
Budget speech—that Thomas Waddell who,
less than four years ago, was declared to be an
utterly hopeless and incompetent Treasurer, and
was thrown out of office by the very men who
have since called him back to his old office. Yet
probably neither Waddell, nor Wade, nor
Wade’s other Ministers have changed their
spots. Australian conditions have, however,
changed ; and that, and the game of party politics,
account for all the remarkable double-somer-
saulting. Waddell happened to be Treasurer at
a time when it was almost impossible for an
honest man to put a good face on things. He had
served through the droughty years of 1902 and
1903 ; he had come into the 1 remiership in
1904, which was even droughtier than 1903.
In those three years the average annual
value of the wool-clip of N. S. Wales
was £8,407,081. Agriculture had got clown to the
bed-rock of disaster ; and mining also languished
on account of a spell of low prices. The railways
were the true reflector, as usual, of the general
depression. In those three years, after the work-
ing expenses had been paid, the earnings of that
department fell £750,531 short of the total of the
interest bill; and Cohen, who knows nothing of
seasons, except to be keener in pursuit of the
interest when they are bad, required that the
shortage should be made up somehow. Except
by borrowing from a second Cohen to pay the
first Cohen, there was only one way to make up
the shortage—by taxing a people who, on account
of bad times, looked hard at every twopence
and grudged parting with it. After those
three years Carruthers arrived in the latter
part of 1904. Already a season of full and
plenty was practically assured ; and the_ country
realised the assurance, and a bonus into the
bargain. From an average of £8,407,081 for the
previous three years, the value of the wool clip
sprang to £12,344,444. The fat year was followed
by two that were fatter still. The N. S. Wales
railways, which in three years had called for a
subsidy of £750,531 out of the Treasury, restored
no less than £1,051,095 in the next three years ;
so that, as to this one item of railways, Waddell,
the man who sat at the Treasury in the lean years,
was £1,801,626 worse off than Carruthers, the
political fox who scented easily-got surpluses on
the turn of the seasons, sprang into the chair, and
took all the glory of the fat years as his booty,
and then sprang out again when the country
correspondent began to mention drought.
* * * »
Under the best of circumstances the party
system of government is a fool of a system,
which can only give the best possible
results by a miracle; and miracles are
much too unreliable for general purposes. But
in Australia it is a worse fool of a system than
usual, because Australia, depending so much on
“the great natural industries,” is above all others
the plaything of the seasons; and the seasons can
make or mar a Government when there is an
Opposition on hand prepared to turn a national
misfortune to its own advantage. Carruthers,
and Ashton, and Wade, who threw Waddell
out with such scorn and violence, knew perfectly
well that Waddell, if not a brilliant financier and
administrator, was at least a reasonably careful
one; they showed their true feelings in the matter
when, later on, having got into office, they were
quite prepared, in return for a poor few votes, to
Eut him back into the very place for which they
ad furiously declared he was a hopeless misfit.
Then, just as Waddell had been proclaimed
a failure because it didn’t rain much while
he was Treasurer, so Carruthers was after-
wards exalted as a magnificent success and a
Heaven-sent genius because it did rain, and be-
cause prices for wool and wheat and lead were
good, for reasons with which Carruthers had no
more to do than the most completely-dead
mummy in Egypt. On the mining fields it has
passed into a proverb that a good mine makes a
good manager ; and it is quite as true that a good
season can make a good reputation for a very poor
Australian State Treasurer. So the artful dodger
of politics who happens to be out of office is given
a magnificent chance. He can pile up a reputa-
tion while the fickle seasons smile, and when they
frown Australian Parliaments and people are
easily enough led to join him in heaving a brick
at the man who has failed at a time when success
was well-nigh impossible.
• * * *
The fact is that, under present Australian
conditions, very little notice ought to be
taken of one single year’s figures, at the
Treasury or anywhere else. The three great in-
dustries of Australia, so far, are the pastoral, the
agicultural, and mining; and allof them are subject
to extraordinary variations. The wheat yield of
N. S. Wales has roamed between a minimum of
1‘24 bushels, and a maximum of 17'51 bushels to
the acre. The State has pastured 61,831,416
sheep, and a few years later the flocks had been
reduced to 26,649,424—they had been 41,857,099
within the previous 12 months. The copper
market climbed up to £ll2 less than nine months
ago ;it has since been below £56. In 1904 lead
was down to £11; within this present year it has
been up to £22 ss. Every Cup time they tell us
that the Australians are a nation of gamblers. In
one respect, however, they are nothing of the sort.
The gambler—the professional gambler, anyhow
—doesn’t expect to win all the time. He
expects losses, and provides for them. What he
looks for is a balance on the right side. But—
outside “ sport,” at any rate—Australia doesn’t
“ make its book ”on that rule. When there is a
boom it won’t recognise that it is a boom and
nothing else. And when the other thing happens,
it is, as a consequence, pretty often unprepared
to see the gloom through.
# 4 1 #
Still, in this respect things might lately have
been a great deal worse. Australian banks, at
any rate, have not lost their heads, so far as can
be j udged at present, during the recent remark-
able seasons. The president of the Bankers’ In-
stitute said a true thing at its annual meeting,
the other day: “Had we enjoyed similar
good seasons a few years back, a spirit
of unhealthy speculation or booming would
have arisen in our midst.” The banker said
that we didn’t have the “unhealthy speeu-
tion” because of “the severe lessons we learnt
some 15 years ago.” That is part, at lea,st, of the
truth ; the other part probably is that, instead of
being able to get vast quantities of cheap money
from London, the Australian banks have been
able to place large amounts in London at
phenomenal interest. That put temptation out
of their way, even if they had been inclined to be
tempted. So even if Australia has had a boom,
its after effects won’t bear any comparison with
those of the boom of the early ’nineties. Our
foreign debt has been actually reduced, and that
is the vast difference between this boom and
every other boom that Australia has had for these
last 50 years. This time, anyhow, we shall have
no overnight drunken I O U coming home to mock
at our sorrow in the morning.
THE INDISPENSABLE IRON.
\y ILLIAM Sandford, Ltd., the Lithgow iron-
works people, appealed to the public a few weeks
agQ £ or more capital, and the appeal failed badly,
q u tbe j ssued prospectus nothing else seemed
pro bable. A r o statement of profit (or loss) was
p . but the public saw that the co. owed
£142,000 to the principal, who apparently owed
most of it to the bank and that the co. itself had
achieved an overdraft of more than £55,000.
Overdrafts don’t necessarily mean distress, of
verara \ ® invest ors had an uneasy feeling that
in this case the public was to get'in. and the
bank was to get out, and in the absence of
specific profit and loss statements, investors natu-
r&ly let” the piopositi™ pus. Failing «. S ««
money from the public, the State has now been
applied to for aid—“by way of an advance, to be
paid by instalments on the certificate of a Govern-
ment officer as plant is erected.”
* « * *
The case recalls the situation in which Maori-
land found itself in 1894, when the Bank of New
Zealand arrived at the Treasury and announced
that Government cash or guarantees to the extent
of £2,000,000 must be put up, or the bank would
close its doors. The State put up what was
wanted, and the results justified the action over
and over again. Not only was the bank saved,
but the State was saved too. Maoriland
was just then adopting a reformed policy, and it
set out on its new way unhampered by the
wreckage of a ruined bank. It nursed the concern
back to prosperity and vigor ; and to-day, under
slightly altered conditions, the State stands
owner of £500,000 worth of the stock in
one of the most profitable businesses in Austral-
asia. If the Bank of New Zealand was necessary
to Maoriland, an iron and steel industry is much
more necessary to Australia. The failure of the
bank might, it is true, have for the moment
caused keener sufferings; but the failure of
the effort to establish the iron and steel
business will almost inevitably have much more
lasting and wider and deeper consequences. Our
very national existence may depend on it. In
the last resort two things seem indispensable—
men and iron. It is hard, then, to conceive of a
price too great to pay for the firm establishment of
a business which will give us the iron.
* * * *
The failure of the Lithgow works—assuming
for the moment that it is a failure—may reason-
ably deter the private investor, but it should
have the very opposite effect on the State.
The first railways in Australia had just the
same experiences as the Sandford people
have had ; but that failure simply translated
itself into a demand that what the private
capitalist had not the strength to undertake, the
State should carry through; and the State did
carry it through. The path of the pioneer is not
often carpeted with roses. Rarely a man of large
means—the man of large means comes in later—
he spends his years and his money in marking out
the track. Presently it may cost only £lO,OOO to
provide an effective plant to do certain work ; but
it may easily cost the pioneer £lOO,OOO to find the
effective machine. For over 30 years William
Sandford and others have been finding out
things at Lithgow and thereabouts. It is
simply inconceivable that if these works fail,
others will not, sooner or later, be attempted
elsewhere in Australia. But these Lithgow works
have started and they ought to be kept
going. They at any rate are certain, and
all the rest are uncertain. And, above all, here
the experimental stage has passed. Were the
iron and steel industry at Lithgow allowed to
collapse, other works might materialise— and
after five, 10, 20 years might very possibly find
themselves in the same position as the Lithgow
works to-day. Australia is littered with smelting
works built by men who should have known their
business; but experience proved their calcula-
tions wrong. From Maryborough, in Queensland,
right round the coast to Fremantle, in Westralia,
there are crumbling furnace stacks. The State
cannot therefore afford to let the industry at
Lithgow perish on the assumption that somebody
else will build works elsewhere. All the pioneer-
ing has been done, and it has been far too costly
and it is far too valuable to waste.
* * * *
There is no reason, however, why the assist-
ance should be given by way of an advance. If
the State is going to put money into the business,
ic ought to have more control over it than
the mere holding of an IO U will give. Nor,
looking at the matter in the cold, commercial
way, is there any reason why, after the private
investor has refused to pull the co. out of the fire,
the State should risk its money without any
possibility of ever getting more than perhaps
3| per cent. If the State saves the undertaking
now, it will treat the present co far better than
the world of commerce would treat it if Sand-
FORD’s pound is allowed to carry the same power
in the concern as the State’s pound. The way of
the world would be for someone to build on the
old co.’s ashes ; but the State at any rate should
not treat the pioneer in that brutal fashion. Still,
from the public point of view it isn’t the
Sandford Co. that calls for anxiety; it is
Australia —the Australia that must have iron and
steel, and that looked like getting what it must
have, but now awakes to find the essential in-
dustry in peril. And the Stats of N. S. Wales—
blessed, happily, with a full Treasury at the
moment —has a chance of averting the peril, and
doing for Australia a service that will blot out
the unpleasant memories of many other things
that it has done lately in its relations with the
Commonwealth.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Fruit and Vegetable Shylock.
The Cow man is often a Tory and an individualist
in politics, but in his business affairs he knows
better than to practise his creed. Consider, his
position regarding the co-operative butter-fac-
tories. There are nearly 500 of them in N.S.W.
alone, to say nothing of the hundreds in Banana-
land and Bent’s country. In the early days of
the industry, Private Enterprise took up the
treatment of milk and cream, and mixed
up so much robbery with the business
as to practically force on co - operation.
The dairyman, finding co-operation a good thing,
carried it further, and lately co-operated in fight-
ing the Shipping Trust, and offered successful
threats to cease supplying the Sydney milk dis-
tributors if they did not pay him a share of the
increased selling price. In Queensland the saw-
millers put up the price of hoop pine for butter
boxes nearly 50 per cent., so the co-operative
factories decided to run a sawmill of their
own. In Victoria, a year ago, the fac-
tories found that the Cold Country middleman
was sucking with great energy, and a few decided
to send their own representative to London, and
in consequence they recently divided £BOOO which
they would not otherwise have seen. Why the
other farmers cannot do what the cow farmers
have done to rid themselves of their own special
ShyIOCKS is a mystery. The fruit and vegetable
growers especially need mutual aid. They are
robbed north, south, east, and west. It may
occasionally be their own fault, through careless
packing or neglect, that they are landed actually
in debt on their consignments, but it is not
so always. There is no check on Sussex-
street. Sussex-street just says so, and that
ends it. Last year one grower sent in 148 boxes
of tomatoes, which were supposed to have fetched
£l5 11s. ; but one single Shylock’s charges came
to £4 18s. lid. At one time the grower used to
buy boxes at 51sd. each, but he never got any re-
turns. Then he accepted the offer of Shylock to
hire them at 3d. a time. Now he always gets the
boxes back—the same lot 12 to 15 times during
the season. The saving in the charges under a
system of co-operation would pay the expenses
of a co-operative society many times over. But
the co-operative system would need to be strength-
ened by a compact amongst co-operating growers
not to consign elsewhere.
Bent’s Indeterminate Sentences Bill.
Premier Bent’s Indeterminate Sentences Bill,
just out, goes a shade further than anything in
that line yet legalised, in that it proposes to apply
the uncertain sentence to “ first-timers ” as well
as to habitual criminals. Culprits of both such
classes may be ordered to a “ reformatory prison ”
on the expiration of their set term of imprison-
ment, or without any such set term, by a Supreme
or County Court Judge, there to remain during
the Governor’s pleasure. The common honor-
arium is, fortunately, not trusted with this power,
but may order a convicted person to be sent
to a Judge to be dealt with under the
Act. This idea of empowering a Judge to sentence
where he didn’t hear the evidence is distinctly
quaint, not to say Bent. Prisoners may be trans-
ferred from gaol to reformatory before their set
sentences have expired if the Inspector-General
and the Indeterminate Sentences Board think it
desirable, but if such prisoners don’t behave them-
selves in the reformatory they may be sent back
to gaol for the rest of their terra, and the
time spent in the reformatory won’t be
counted—which ought to be a powerful in-
ducement to good conduct or a plausible imita-
tion thereof. Every prisoner in a reformatory
is to be kept at remunerative work, a
proportion of the pay going to the prisoner, a
proportion to his wife and family or any person
dependent on him, and a proportion to the State.
Prisoners may also be released on probation and
must then report themselves to the police once in
every three months during the term of probation.
Einally, an Indeterminate Sentences Board is to
be appointed to “make careful inquiry” as to
whether prisoners are sufficiently reformed to be
passed out of gaol and into a reformatory, and
afterwards whether they are fit to be released
on probation. Probation officers may be appointed
to visit, befriend, or assist, or try to find work
for probationers, and generally keep a fatherly
or motherly eye on them. It is a Bill that can t
leave things much worse, and may leave them a
good deal better than they are at present; but it
practically rests on two supports—(l) the consti-
tution, capacity and general behavior of the I.S.
Board and the probation officers, and (2) the
provision of enough remunerative employment
for “reformers.” When a prisoner is once made
to grasp the pounds, shillings and pence aspect of
remunerative work, and the advantage, to him, of
doing that work outside instead of inside a house
of detention, his “ reform ” won’t be long delayed.
But the plenteousness of work inside such places
pre-demands the plenteousness of it outside ; and
that is a matter that does not lie entirely with the
State authorities.

Practical Patriots.
“ Breathes there a man with soul so dead,’ a
romantic poet has asked, “who never to himself
hath said, ‘This is my own, my native land?
Had he lived in these days in Australia he would
have recognised a large class with souls not only
dead but so far progressed in the unpleasant stages
of decay as to say, “Yes, this is my own, my
native land. But it’s a poor sort of place and
paltry. The things it can do are few and mean ;
the tilings it cannot do are so many as to cover
almost all the dignified processes of civibration.
Yes, if you must know, we are Australians; but
would prefer to keep that fact concealed.” Then
follow statements as to what Australians cannot
do. They cannot defend their own country, but
must pay tribute to others to do that for them.
They cannot build ships or make engines, manu-
facture textiles or furniture, or shoes, or machines.
They must import all their big officials. In o
only resources of the country are those primitive
industries which produce raw materials from the
soil, and primitive laborers. In view of the
“Leaders in Handcuffs.”
If a man would fain be leader in the Ultra-Labor
van
He must be a man of grit and wit and mettle;
He must feel the pulse of Demos right from
Beersheba to Dan ;
He must keep himself in everlasting fettle ;
He must win the shrill fanatic, who is shrieking
out “ The Cause,”
He must hold the Moderatist close beside him,
He must bear the sneers of Boodledom, the fury of
the claws
Of the plutocratic journals that deride him.
He must be endowed with wisdom, and he must
not want in tact;
He must always rule his crowd with gentle
humor ;
He should know the time for silence, he should
know the time to act,
And be deaf to bitter sneer and rabid rumor ;
In debate he should be eloquent and vigorous and
bold,
His brain should be as quiver to the bowmen,
His counsel to his party should be blessing
manifold
And a source of endless cursing to his foemen.
And whene’er the Ultra party gets a leader thus
endowed,
Does it follow where his skilful counsels lead
them?
Does it hail its brainy Gracchus with a greeting
high and proud,
And his warnings and his chidings—does it heed
them ?
Nay. Whene’er his men he marshals up in trucu-
lent array,
They show him that his leadership’s a fiction,
Bor he finds they’ve other leaders in the country
far away,
And he’s only— just a general —with restriction.
So with hobbles on his ankles and with fetters
on his wrist
He must cut his way to victory and glory ;
Must fight a bitter foeman who is mighty with
the fist,
And make his party proud to tell the story ;
He must plan a cunning campaign, and discover
to his cost
He has masters and not soldiers—when he needs
them ;
While the rank and file who hail him as the leader
of the host
Will do everything but follow where he leads
them.

POLITICOS.


existence in Australia, and especially in
Sydney, of these unpleasantly decayed souls,
there is a good deal of comfort to be
derived from such an assembly as that
which has just broken up—of the manufacturers
of Australia, the practical patriots of the Common-
wealth. They are the men who, whatever their
motives, or their methods, at least give the lie to
the slander that the Australian is a Gibeonite
among the peoples—a mere hewer of wood and
drawer of water. These manufacturers are
making the necessary things of civilisation—hats,
clothing, machinery, musical instruments,
pottery, furniture —with Australian labor in
Australian factories, and proving false some of
the lies of the anti-nationalist, and throwing
grave doubts on other of the lies, for if
it is alleged that the Australian can’t make a
piano and he does make one it is a fair
inference of the falseness of the charge that he
can’t make a rifle. As a matter of truth, the
Australian has shown (not so much in his own
country as in foreign lands) that for inventiveness,
resource, and mechanical skill he is abreast with
the highest of the older peoples. But he can only
fully prove liis capacity to constitute a nation by
making on his own soil, and with his own brains
and hands, all things necessary to fully-organised
existence. That is the ideal, and is yet to come.
Meanwhile the existing manufacturers, who bring
us a stage towards that ideal, are the pioneers in
practical patriotism.
Sydney’s Water Supply.
Just when a coastal drought holds out the possi-
bility of Sydney being put on very short commons
for water, another row between the Board of
Water Supply and Sewerage and the Government
is announced. The new subject of contention is
the Waterfall resumption. The Board wants the
land around Waterfall reserved to augment the
catchment area of the Sydney water supply. The
Government insists on devoting the area to
the use of a consumptives’ hospital, and
“for other schemes of water supply.” So far as
a lay mind can tell on a subject which
needs an expert engineer’s judgment, the Board
of Water Supply and Sewerage is usually in the
right in its controversies with the Government,
but is made the victim of the cantankerousness of
Public Works Department officials. There seems
to be an hereditary feud in that department
against the Board. Anything the Board proposes
is wrong because the Board proposes it, if for no
other reason. It is deplorable that this friction
should exist between two great public bodies,
more than deplorable when the matter of con-
troversy is such a vital thing as the water supply.
Already Sydney is suffering from a contaminated
supply because of the Government’s desire to
annoy the Board by keeping a lot of rotten vege-
table matter in the Cataract dam. Now, the
Board is to be further aggravated by the planting
of a consumptives’ hospital on a spot which it
wants for water collection. Doubtless it is a very
fine game for the Government, which, having the
power, can annoy and thwart the Board to an
almost unlimited extent. But it is poor fun for
the Sydney public, which sees its city threatened
with actual disaster, so that a few Jacks-in-office
can feed some ridiculous grudge or jealousy.
Little White Slaves.
Victoria has just discovered another unpleasant
feature about its cocky. That misguided State
hires out little orphans to the farmers for Is. per
week and their clothes, and the department in
charge of the children has been somewhat stag-
gered of late at the enormous increase in the
number of returns. Also, someone more observ-
ant than usual has noticed that the children are
usually returned in exactly the same clothes as
they wore when they were sent to the farmers,
even if they were sent a long time before. The
explanation is simple. The cocky is largely
what the rack-renting landlord has made him,
and the landlord s handiwork is very dreadful.
When the State child, who is usually an orphan,
arrives on the farm he is made to hand over his
clothes, which are folded away. Then he is
clothed in shirts and pants made out of
old flour bags, or some other valueless and
superannuated material. While on the farm,
long hours and bad food, often punctuated
with beatings and abuse, are the orphan’s lot.
And if, in spite of these things, he persists in
growing, the cocky keeps an anxious eye on his
progress, and calculates how long he will be able
to keep him before it will be necessary to supply
(b®w clothes for his very rare public appearances.
When it comes to the last chance of getting that
kid into, those clothes, he is bundled back to the
“home” as unsatisfactory, dull, untruthful, or
something of the sort; and another is sent along
in his place, to be also returned when he comes to
the same crisis as his predecessor. This boarding-
out system, which is really another name for child
slavery, is practised solely on account of its
economy, and in the belief that it eases
the burden on the State. If the State
would only take the trouble to investigate, it
would probably discover that what it saves on the
State child it loses over and over again on the
adult. These starved, ill-treated little waifs grow
up physically weak, and mentally feeble, and
with blurred ideas of right and wrong. Then the
State has to supply them with hospitals, gaols, and
occasionally with rope and free burial. It is
hardly worth while burdening the State with an
enormous outlay on hospitals, asylums for the
insane, and burials for the impecunious dead in
order to supply the cocky with cheap child labor.
Humanity does not seem to appeal to the Govern-
ment of Bent in this matter, but there is a cash
aspect of the case. Surely Bent will consider
the cash aspect of the case.
Agriculture and the Importing Disease.
The Herald is still suffering from the importing
craze. It quotes Railway Commissioner Johnson
as justification for the Government going abroad
to secure an officer to man the new Agricul-
tural Department. The cases are not parallel,
even were the importation of Johnson justified.
The railways are based upon a common-place
system, pretty complete, thanks to Oliver, so
that the staff could manage affairs if Johnson left
for a two years’ trip to Antarctica. In fact,
Johnson has been content to follow the
old plans and methods very closely. But
the Department of Agriculture is a vastly
different concern. Local knowledge is absolutely
necessary, and is the main qualification, provided
the man handling the ropes has the required
administrative capacity. A man from Europe or
America must be perfectly useless infilling the
post as it should be filled until he became
acquainted with the peculiarities of Australian
soils and climate. N. S. Wales, pastorally,
easily stands first in the world ; and in agriculture
she is well forward, considering the popula-
tion and the effects of the old Freetrade
tariff, which sterilised experiment and handcuffed
the secondary industries which would, if encour-
aged, have given the man on the land his chance to
embark on new ventures. As it is there are farms
in the State equal for equipment and progressive-
ness to any in the world. The owners have bred
wheats which are adaptable to the climate ; have
raised stock equal to the best herds for pro-
ductivity anywhere ; produced sheep which chal-
lenge comparison ; and made butter and cheese of
the highest quality—the best proof of talent and
adaptability. As regards the Department of
Agriculture there is room for reform, but no
necessity for importing any officials. The staff is
good enough, taking it all round, for it contains
officers of the best type. But red-tape, or some
other sort of manacle, cob-webs them at every turn.
During Carruthers’ time the Estimates, which
were never high, were cut down further and
further, until there was not enough voted
to keep the State farm hands going, and
many had to be dismissed. Director Camp-
bell may not be the best man procurable,
but he has never had a chance, for Carruthers,
who insisted upon telling Sammy Moore that he
was merely a cipher, used to consistently shelve
every scheme or plan that was hatched. Let the
Department under the new regime have a better
scope under its present head and see the results.
Already great progress has been made, and N. S.
Wales is not all in the state of despicable back-
wardness mentioned by some ; to accelerate the
rate of that progress wiser political administra-
tion alone seems to be needed. The farmers are
all right, the Department’s experts are all right.
The last thing needed is an imported head who
would have to learn the A B C of Australia
before he was worth anything.

The Commonwealth’s New Protection.
The Victorian Employers’ Federation has written
to Victorian Premier Bent, stating that it is not
burning with the desire to serve two masters, and
that it wants to serve Bent alone. Its grievance
is that “ the Commonwealth Arbitration Court,
sitting in connection with the Excise Act, is using
legal machinery to decide the minimum
wage in the agricultural-implement industry
irrespective of the Wages Board legislation
of the State.” This, it is contended, is “tan-
tamount to setting up two separate authorities,
and the Commonwealth Government taking
charge of the State’s industrial conditions.” The
grievance is plausible, but fictitious. The Com-
monwealth decides that, in return for payingdecent
wages, Australian manufacturers will be exempted
from the payment of certain Excise duties, and
to charge Excise duties and to remit Excise duties
are among the functions of the Commonwealth.
If the Victorian Employers’ Federation members
decide that they will not pay the wages considered
fair by the Commonwealth Arbitration Court,
they need not. They are free to run their indus-
tries as they choose—but they cannot dodge the
Excise duty. Of course, the effect of that is to
give the Commonwealth, by a roundabout way,
control of the wage-rates paid. And that is bene-
ficial to the decent employer, as it guards him
against unfair competition from other States.
The manufacturer in N. S. Wales, where there is
an Arbitration Act, or Victoria, where there is
a Wages Boards system, used to suffer from the
disadvantage that he might be undercut by the
manufacturer in, say, Tasmania, where there is
no industrial legislation, and against whose pro-
duct he has no Protection such as he has against
cheap labor products from outside the Common-
wealth. The New Protection Excise system tends
to prevent that, and put all the States
on equal terms. The device is a clumsy
one, certainly : it would be far better
to put the whole question of industrial
legislation in the hands of the Commonwealth and
allow it to directly dictate fair-wage rates through-
out. But the States won’t agree to that so far.
The Commonwealth, therefore, does its best in a
roundabout way ; and no decent employer has any
call to grumble—rather otherwise.

The Health of the Kids.
President Roosevelt’s advice to this country
(that heroic and industrious orator is fond
of giving advice) was to “keep the cradles
full.” The next step is to see that they
are full, not of sickly kids, but of healthy
ones. A means to this would be the regu-
lar medical inspection of children at schools.
According to an enthusiastic letter, written by
Victorian Education-director Tate from the Inter-
national Congress on School Hygiene in London,
this duty of inspection, which awakens a shudder
in Australia, on the ground that it is “ Social-
istic,” is already undertaken by State or
local authorities in Austria, Sweden, Argentina,
Hungary, Norway, Switzerland, France, Germany,
and parts of the United States. (He might have
added Russia, Roumania, Servia, Chili, and even
Egypt.) By means of this system, the youngster
with tuberculosis may be noted, segregated, and
cured ; and all sorts of weaknesses may be dis-
covered and remedied. The supporters of school
medical inspection include all the leading educa-
tionalists and most of the leading medical men in
Europe. Little Tasmania (though it has not yet
got a full system of free education) has appointed a
Medical Inspector, and is at least pottering with
the subject. A Bill to provide for the inspection of
English schools is to be presented to Parliament.
Probably a logical corollary to the inspection is an
arrangement whereby, when parents are poor,
the State will provide some sort of medical
and hospital attendance for the sick boy or girl.
And the State will find it cheaper to cure the sick
person while he is young than to, support him
when he grows older.

For Unity.
Of late years certain peaceable British and foreign
“ middle-class ” families have begun a system of
exchange whereby a British youth or maiden goes
to a Continental family and learns something of the
country in the home circle there ; while, in return,
the hope and pride of the Continental house
temporarily fills the place of the exported British
product. This sensible practice tends to remove the
idea from a certain number of British and foreign
minds that all the people of each and either coun-
try are hankering to get at the other fellow’s
throat and scratch for hlood. In Australia, and
especially in N. S. Wales, the daily press seems to
deliberately employ itself in work having exactly the
opposite effect. Under its baneful influence a clan
of youthful Victoriaphobes threatens to grow up in
N.S.W., suffering from a firm conviction that every
inhabitant of Bent’s country is a predatory and
malignant villain, and one whose only design in
life is to stamp out and reduce to utter ruin and
anarchy the amiable and suffering old Ma State.
According to The Bulletin’s way of thinking,
the fostering of such sentiments as the above
is not merely lamentable and foolish, but
dangerous. Australia doesn’t possess so many men
capable of handling guns that it can afford to
have large sections of its populace sooled at each
other like so many dogs. On the day when the
invasion comes it will be somewhat late for the
Sydney Telegraph and S.M.H. to explain
hurriedly that they never meant a thing by their
reference to Victorian “ treacheries ” and “out-
rages, and “ robberies,” and the like ; and that,
on the contrary, they would as soon have two or
three regiments of Victorians to aid in checking
the enemy s advance on Sydney as any other two or
three regiments of their acquaintance. But these
insane Jingo, importing rags won’t find out that
till ci foe is actually hammering at their officedoors.
In the meantime, whatever damage they can do
as daily papers in the way of strife-creation
between State and State they are diligently
doing. One method of counteracting the effect of
this constant stream of State Rights slander and
dissension-breeding would be the adoption by
those Australian-minded parents who could afford
it of the European plan aforementioned. The
N.S.W. youngster who had spent a year in Vic-
toria would almost certainly return to his town-
ship with a refreshing contempt for talk of wrongs
“destined to be wiped out in blood,’’and other
such balderdash—a contempt, moreover, likely to
be healthily contagious. It may seem a fantastic
proposition as between people of the same blood
and language ; and yet the Sydney Yellow press is
toiling its hardest to render something of the
sort very needful. Its theory that all virtue,
honesty, decency and such-like end at the
Murray, or (where there isn’t any Murray) at a
peg on the road between Delegate and Bruthen,
has created, in the minds of a few fools, a
more frenzied hatred against an adjacent State
with which N.S.W. has never had a shred of real
disagreement than exists to-day between France
and Germany with a thousand years of war to
look back upon ; and even a few such combustible
fools may be dangerous in a community.
It is a long while since a more cheerful report was
made by directors to shareholders than one
issued recently from the Jumbunna Coal Co.’s
office. It wasn’t meant to be cheerful, but it was.
The co.—a leading member of the Gippsland
(Vic.) group—complained that it had lately been
making no progress, because it couldn’t either
keep the miners it had or get new ones. When
they think it over, those directors may remember
something about curses coming home to roost.
In this case they themselves are the roost.
Five years ago they treated their employees
shamefully. To get even on a reduction from
13s. fid. to 11s. 3d. per ton paid for coal
by ,be Victorian railways, this and other
cos. ti ed to cut down the miners’ pay from
3s. 6d. to 2s. fid. per ton. When the men said that
the cut was too deep, and asked—finally begged—
the cos. to arbitrate, the directors’ reply was
to fill the mines with police-protected non-
unionists, who were housed in a stable and fed
like beasts. The Jumbunna Co. “broke” the
strike, but in the mind of almost every decent
mine-workman in Australia a black cross was put
against the name of the Gippsland colliery pro-
prietors. So long as labor was plentiful these
mines could get workers; but when fresh open-
ings offered they were promptly taken, and Jum-
bunna was left to its losses and its lamentations
at the very time when trade was brisk and
the chances of profit good. Other employers
with high-handed ideas may note the fact with
quite as much benefit to themselves as to their
workmen.

The “pastoral” on Sabbath desecration, just
issued by the Rev. Alexander Stewart, Scotch-
byterian Moderator of Victoria and pastor of a
suburban tabernacle, is another illustration of the
folly of the cobbler who doesn’t stick to wax. In
pursuing the Sabbath desecrator, the Rev. Alex.,
who can no doubt split a theological hair with
unerring certainty, wanders off the beat he knows,
and discusses Cow and the way she should go.
Dairymen he urges to use all possible mechanical
means for lessening Sunday work, “such as home
separators.” Now the home separator, instead of
decreasing the Sunday work, actually increases it.
In those districts which send whole milk to the
factories and creameries, all that is necessary,
once the milking is over, is for someone to drive
to the factory. With a separator, the milk has to
be dealt with at once, and the engine and separa-
tor have to be kept going. The cream and skim
milk must be put into cans and taken care of;
and, when the separating is done, the separator has
to be taken to pieces and cleaned. There is easily
twice as much work on a home separating farm at
milking time as there is on an ordinary dairy.
So in the name of Sabbath observance, the subur-
ban Moderator really asks the dairy farmer to make
Sunday more of a working day than ever.
Secondly (this being by way of a sermon), butter
from home-separated cream is not of the highest
grade. The Victorian Government experts are
all agreed in advocating the taking of the whole
milk to the factory, instead of the cream. And,
thirdly, the home-separator plays into the hands
of the Melbourne agents, who buy cream, and
try to burst up the co-operative factories. Lastly,
dearly beloved brethren, Mr. Stewart’s pastoral
is a deplorable mistake, and his church should
see that in future no Moderator discusses Cow
until he has learned Cow.

Professor Biles, who teaches Naval Architec-
ture at Glasgow University, will take away with
him one thing at least, a knowledge of the pro-
cesses by which the anti-Australian press cramps
and stifles the industrial development of the Com-
monwealth. He talked to the papers in Mel-
bourne on the subject he knows most about, with
special reference to the building of torpedo-boat
destroyers in Australia. He said—what Australia
was quite prepared to admit—that the Common-
wealth doesn’t know all there is to know about
the building of destroyers, and that if it wants to
get the best results it must not depend wholly
upon its own knowledge. Gn perfectly fair
and reasonable statement the antVAustralian news-
papers built the usual structure of misrepresenta-
tion and vilification of Australia. The Argus led
off withaheading—“Cannot be built in Australia.”
But the quoted authority said nothing of the kind.
To tell a man that he will never learn to swim
unless he bathes, is not the same as telling him
that he will never be able to swim. There are no
special circumstances about the matter. It is
simply the old, horribly familiar, degrading “We
can’t ” attitude, based on the one presumption
that Australia is a miserable worm or toad, and
must never try to be anything else but a worm or
toad.

Flour is “ up,” and bread is “ up,” and milk and
cheese are “ up,” and now in Sydney the sixpenny
hash-house has decided to be a sevenpennv temple
of indigestion. Of course the threatening drought
(which in some places has got past threatening) is
responsible : still, when anybody writes to the
Sydney News and says it’s all on account of “our
Melbourne rulers,” the News prints the rubbish,
and lets it soak in without a jeer. One such
correspondent, after the usual anti-tariff imbe-
cility, burst out the other evening—“ O for the
days when butter could be bought for fid. alb.,
bread two loaves for 3Jd., and good meat for Id.!”
Now, when did those days happen ? The “Official
Year Book of N. S. Wales,” just published,
prints “average retail prices of eight standard
commodities ” for each year since 1870. And that
official list shows that, in all the bright Freetrade
years from 1870 to 1892, bread never in any single
year averaged less than threepence, and often was
fourpence, whereas in the three years 1904-5-6 it
didn’t once average more than 2fd. ; and even in
these latter distressing days that move “Con-
stant Reader ” to his lamentations, bread can be
had for 3d.—which, as mentioned just now, was
the lowest average for all the glorious Freetrade
years between 1870 and 1892. As for fresh beef,
in the good old days of 30 years ago (1876) it
averaged s|d., and sugar averaged 4d. (it is 2|d.
now, since the Kanaka was sent back to his island
home, where we undertook all along to send him),
and butter averaged as much as Is. 7d. in 1874,
when Parkes was Premier. “Father of Four-
teen” and “ Constant Breather ” and other press
correspondents have got into the bad habit of
referring to the good old times in this vague way
—of exclaiming “O for the days when bread was
2d. a dozen ! ” and things of that sort—and it is
quite up to someone to ask them to put their
finger on the almanack and say exactly when
those days were.

“ Turtle ” drifts from worms on to a large sub
ject
The Jap invasion of the North is already something
considerable. In regard to pearling-, the whites are
merely living on the rents of boats, etc., giving divers all
pearls and £BO per ton for shell. Further, the beche-
de-mer industry is now practically controlled and run
by Japanese, who, not being allowed to own boats, evade
the law by employing white agents, who hold the
licenses. Yet there is good money to be made by white
men running- the boats themselves, and utilising New
Guinea men and our own aborigines. Some remarkable
catches have been made off Bowen and neighborhood,
and at present all Japs engaged in the industry are fish-
ing down there, taking the cash proceeds out of the
country and demoralising- the aborigines.
Further, adding to the danger of Australia, many Japs
are, from the nature of their employment, becoming
more thoroughly acquainted with the Queensland East
Coast than any of our Torres Straits pilots, knowing
passages and channels unsurveyed and uncharted. These
men are practical “pilots,” ready when emergency calls
them in the future. Writer witnessed, at night, in
narrow enclosed waters between dangerous reefs, some of
the lately-visiting Jap war boats manoeuvring into fornia-
Mons, just as though they were in open waters.
~he western entrances of Torres Straits are the key—cr
a key to this continent in the event of trouble. I
suggest the abandonment of the Fort at Thursday
Island, which at any time could be made unten-
able by the later type of battleship shelling from
behind the numerous islands around there, where the
h ort s guns would be blind. Substitute, instead,
a torpedo mine station, modern in every particular,
keeping m touch with improvements, and having- torpedo
boats and destroyers to work with it. Further, in refer-
ence to the passages available for heavy-draught war-
ships coming from the west, and entering Torres Straits,
passing south : First there are the Endeavor Straits,
with many small channels, which, if mined, would prove
death traps to any men-of-war. This channel is impos-
sible save to men with local knowledge. Jap fishermen
possess such knowledge. Then there are channels
through hammond, Friday, Goode, and Thursday
on? are impossible to vessels drawing over
-.oft. This leaves only four other practicable channels to
heavy-draught vessels, viz., Prince of Wales’ Channel,
Dayman Channel, Simpson Channel, and Yule Channel,
averaging one mile and a half at their narrowest,
lhese could be made practically impregnable, if
properly mined. Finally, there are two dangerous
channels (Japs know them thoroughly) between Hawkes-
bury and Banks Island ; and Napoleon Channel, danger-
ous and usually impracticable, but possible, though it
narrows to less than a quarter of a mile in places. So it
narrows defence to about six channels, impreg-
nable if properly mined. This would force any possible
enemy to the eastward of New Guinea, again in narrow
and dangerous waters, where a possible naval holocaust
could be brought about, granting Australia had a decent
navy. Outside of these routes, possible invaders would
have to circle within the Eastern Polynesian groups, or
go west via the Indian Ocean and Cape Leuwin. But the
natural gateway to the Eastern Coast is via Torres
Straits; and that can be made impassable if we like.
Instead of making it impassable, we are permitting the
people who look most like our natural enemies to learn
far more about it than we know ourselves.

From N.S.W: Treasurer Waddell’s financial
statement :
So far as I can estimate, the value of these (transferred)
properties will be about £4,000,000. At the present time,
by mutual arrangement, an officer of the Federal Govern-
ment and two State officers are engaged in valuing these
properties. lam hopeful that before long we shall receive
payment for them. It must be recognised that the Federal
Government has been paying us a larger amount of the
Customs and Excise duties that it was bound to. If it
had paid for those properties before it would have
deducted the interest on the capital value from the sums
to be returned to us, and we would have got less.
Thus does the truth arrive. Before the elec-
tions, when Carruthers was floating hack to
office on a flood of lies against the Federal
Government, he almost wept with wrath as he
explained the villainy of the Commonwealth
Government with regard to those properties.
Now Carruthers’ colleague and successor at the
Treasury officially tells the truth of the matter :
(1) The Commonwealth hasn’t settled up for the trans-
ferred properties because it can’t till it knows their value.
(2) Carruthers’ successor and ex-colleague doesn’t
know their value ; Carruthers’ doesn’t know (seemingly
he didn’t try to find out) ; no one knows, but the Com-
monwealth Government is working its hardest to find
out and settle up.
(3) Anyhow, N.S.W. was getting extra revenue by hav-
ing the matter unsettled, so N.S.W. was all right.

Cyril Blacket on the timber trade and the
tariff :
Messrs. Ketiiel, Fehon and Freeman (N.S.W. Forestry
Commission) have reported that, as the supply of local
seasoned hardwood is frequently not equal to the demand,
the duty on foreign timber in its dressed state should
be taken off, and an export duty put on local timber.
This advice is the natural result of the Foreign-trade line
of thought.
At present the Sydney builder pays 14s. per 100 ft. for
what is known as “seasoned” hardwood—the word
“ seasoned ” being very often only a figure of speech. The
Sydney saw-miller, who buys this in its log state, pays 7s.
per 100 ft., delivered at his Sydney mill, whilst the selector
who originally sold the tree gets from 6d. to Is. per 100 ft.
as it stood. The present market price of Oregon is 15s.
per 100 ft. As Oregon pine comes many thousands of
miles over the ocean, and, before that, is probably floated
down a river from the place where it grew to
the shipping port, it naturally follows that it is more
or less seasoned, and therefore is put on the
market in a marketable state. Local hardwood, on the
other hand, is rarely seasoned, as the miller and the
carpenter find it easier to work when green, so that the
two classes of timber do not meet on equal terms. Yet,
as tests prove that, for joists, rafters, etc., local hardwood
of 25 per cent, smaller section is as strong as Oregon, it
is evident, at present prices, that seasoned hardwood
costing 10s. 6d. will go as far as Oregon costing 155.,
whilst for durability under exposed and trying conditions
Oregon is scarcely worthy, by comparison, of serious con-
sideration.
Whatever the future may bring about, there is not at
present any scarcity of supply, as on the N.S.W. South
Coast, which is the district best known to me, we have
undisturbed forests.
But the selector-owner of the tree gets so small a price
for his timber that it does not pay'him to sell it, and the
bulk of the present supply comes from Crown Lands ; and,
certainly, seasoned hardwood is a scarce commodity.
Had there been during all these years a stiff import
duty on foreign timbers, our local industry would be in a
healthy state, instead of which the original seller is
starved out, and the necessary preparation for market
by seasoning has become an unknown thing.

PRACTICAL TEST OF THE "RYEE AUTOMATIC
RABBIT DESTROYER MADE ON MR. CHARLES
HARVEY’S FARM, CROOKWELL.
Crookwell, 4th November, 1907.
To the Manager, Tyree Limited,
314 George Street, Sydney.
Dear Sir, —I have much pleasure in informing you that
the trial of your famous RABBIT DESTROYER took
place on Friday, November Ist. In all, three tests were
made, two in my paddock and one in Mr. J. Keith’s, and a
large number of rabbits were killed in each test. In No;
1 test, a marked rabbit was placed in the burrow, and on
opening the burrow, it was not found on Friday, as the
whole of the warren was not opened; but on Saturday
morning, when Mr. Desmond and myself visited the farm,
we could not find any trace of one rabbit having
left the warren, and on opening a small pocket a short
distance from the main burrow we found the marked
rabbit, together with fifteen others, quite dead. I am
very well pleased with the result of this trial, and have
no hesitation in stating that the Tit REE RABBIT
DESTROYER is the best means of destroying the pest.
My opinion is, that if the burrows are not interfered with,
not one can escape the deadly poison, and, as they remain
in the burrow, no nuisance is caused, and no other animal
or bird is affected by the poison. I can confidently re-
commend this machine to all those desirous of wholesale
destruction of this pest.—Yours faithfully (Signed)
Charles Harvey.*
