—— ♦
Some hundred or so of English play wrights
have been petitioning Edward R. to cut off the
head of his old pal, the Lord Chamberlain, so far
as the old pal uses it to censor plays. But what
playwrights really need is the abolition of news-
papers, the execution of editors, and the suppres-
sion of popular education. For these things have
made life very hard for the poor playwright. The
public is taught too much ; and familiarity breeds
contempt. So the playwrights have to wipe out
of their universe all the times and places that the
papers talk about, and get back to the vague past
which they can paint in gold and purple, or
to! the distance that lends enchantment to the
view. Julius Knight turns his clock back to
Beaucaire and Robin Hood; Andrew Mack is
Tom Moore; Tittell Brune is Kundry, or some-
thing in the days of Bess; while Waldron,
seeking safety in miles instead of years, hunts up
a squaw in the legendary “West”; Australia’s
Bilanderson explores our own bushranging myths
for his little bit of thrill; and the musical-comedy
writers stick tight to “the East” as the best peg
to hang their trifles on. Whatever Columbus it
was that first discovered how good “the East”
is for this last purpose made a notable find. For
the fabled “ wealth of Ormuz and of Ind ” —the
spicy breezes, and the coral strand, and all the
rest of it that everybody knows who hasn’t been
there —furnishes a reasonable excuse for the gor-
geousness of the frills that must hang about every
musical-comedy performance ; and what couldn’t
be swallowed even with a ton of salt if it were
supposed to happen under a gum or an oak is all
right if there is a property palm in the back-
ground. So “ the East ” has these last few years
been ploughed and harrowed and reaped of a new
crop —Persia, and India, and Ceylon, and Japan,
and now Burmah, the home of “The Blue Moon.”
But what is going to happen soon, Heaven only
knows, for Cochin China is about the only terri-
tory that isn’t now annexed.
* * * •
But' whether it is Persia, or India, or
Ceylon, or Burmah, it is very much the same—
the same English Johnny officers, the same coffee-
colored potentates, the same canoodlings. “The
Blue Moon ” certainly resists the temptation, fatal
to most others of the species, to forget all about
the plot as the show bustles towards its end. It
is quite prepared to mislay the plot as it goes
along, but in the last five minutes it dashes back,
and picks it up, and explains that little Blue
Moon, the strolling Burmah singing-girl, really
isn’t Chandra Nil, but the stolen daughter of Lady
Brabasham; and Pat Bathurst, who is the principal
potentate, offers the lady her choice between him-
self and one of the English soldier Johnnies, and
she chooses the English soldier Johnny; and
that’s the story, except that a gentleman whose
father sold whelks in Whitechapel turns himself
into a Burmese juggler without sufficient excuse,
and, as a sort of godfather to the Blue Moon,
supplies the George Lauri part of the entertain-
ment. Unfortunately when the show opened at
Her Majesty’s (Sydney), on Saturday night, Victor
Gouriet, the man imported for the George Lauri
part, was still nursing an injured ankle, and
Edward Nable wrestled with the fat part. It
was a bit too large for him. But the part is
whimsical, and was quite well enough played to
make it popular. It also owns one of the three or
four catchy musical-bits—“Oh, be careful of the
Crocodile,” the first of “ The Blue Moon ” ditties
that the small boy will whistle as he hangs on the
tram step. The soubrette’s success, “ She Didn’t
Know,” isn’t as good for whistling purposes ; but
five thousand prodigies in pinnies are already
struggling with it.
* * * *
Maorilander Miss Amy Murphy, who is
Chandra Nil (which, being interpreted, is Blue
Moon), is no actress yet, but her voice is her
fortune, and it runs into five figures—a
light, pure soprano fortune, and the owner
handles it with brains. There is nothing more
artistic in all the show than the soprano’s duet
with Reginald Kenneth, the new baritone —who,
too, is no actor, and wasn’t helped by his undress
uniform to cut a gainlv figure. In the way of acting,
the ex-Adelaide boy, Harold Parks, came as the
grand surprise. He had to get his effects by solid
work—he had no picturesque costume-part to
help him—and he got them quietly and most
effectively. Myles Clifton, the Majah, furnished
a good crisp character study, and joined Kenneth
and a rapidly-improved J. R. Hogue in the popu-
lar “ Majah and Minah ” trio. The work of the
chorus is brisk, but this part of the co. has no
very striking work to do. The scenic artists
(W. R. Coleman and John Gordon) have much
larger opportuities, and they deserved the calls
they didn’t answer.

Brewster continues the struggle to dissi-
pate thousands in order to acquire millions at the
Theatre Royal; and the quaintness of the idea

evidently didn’t live in a place where the Sunday
papers give mining tips.

When he found the Whiteleys in Europe,
prospector Rickards discovered a mine that will
go 3oz. to the ton, with something over in the
tailings—the tailings being the people who
couldn’t get into the Tivoli on Saturday, and will
have to come up for re-treatment later on. These
Whiteleys— four of them—juggle with themselves,
sometimes on the stage, sometimes on the slack
wire; and the lady of the co. is so nimble and
dainty that she could probably turn somersaults
on a cobweb if she tried. Another newcomer is
Henri Alexander, who earns his living standing
on his shoulders and cutting antics with his feet.
The two turns take the present Tivoli bill right
up into the front row.

“J.A.C.”: The “ early-door ” extortion
received a severe set-back at the Criterion on
Saturday last. The whole of the area, with the
exception of three seats at the back, was turned
into what they term orchestral stalls. These
three rows were speedily filled, but the “ early-
door” charge still urged on its wild career, and
the greater part of those who paid the extra
money had to content themselves with standing
incommodation. Now, the alleged reason for the
existence of the “early-door” is that people, by
paying a little more for their tickets, may secure
a seat. The “early-door” is rather a trick, any-
way ; but it is a malpractice when door curtains
are kept closely drawn that people may not see
what they are being deluded into. (I do not
blame Mr; Anderson any more than other man-
agers—they are pretty well all tarred with
the same brush.) Those who had paid the
“early-doOr” assessment, for the “privilege” of
standing, did not like it ; they grumbled,
but they bore it meekly until the advent of a
sturdy old lady. When she found she could not
get a seat, she demanded her money back. Oh,
dear, no ! they could not do that! She said she
could not, and would not, stand throughout the
performance. Well, she could pay another
shilling and go into the stalls. “Oh, no!” she
said; “ I’ll show you another way of arranging
matters.” And she walked up to the barricade,
assaulted it, razed it to the ground, and took her
seat in front, where there was plenty of room.
She was immediately followed by all the other
standing members of the audience, and the entire
staff of the front of the house, including the pea-
nut boys, could not shift them, for they had the
sympathy of the house with them. I honor that
plucky old lady. By the way, is there not some
vague police ordinance regarding people standing
in passages of theatres '!
(From THE BULLETIN 15/2/’05.)
LINDSAY’S GREAT PICTURE just acquired by the Victorian National Gallery.
“POLLICE VERSO"—a black-and-white by Norman Lindsay, exhibited by the Sydney Art Society, which set
many tongues clacking and pens scratching in Australia. Hailed enthusiastically as a masterpiece of
black-and-white work by some critics, it was condemned with even greater fervor by others as “immoral”
and “blasphemous.” The Bulletin has printed much of this controversy. Now, at the suggestion of
readers who had not a chance of seeing the original, it prints a reproduction of the picture. Justification
for the praise it won will be found promptly. Reason for the blame may seem, too, at first apparent. But
the work calls for explanation rather than apology. There is ho intention to represent the Crucifixion. The
crucified figure is the symbol of Asceticism; the rout of revellers of Epicureanism. A crucifixion might have
been used as a symbol by a Pagan painter long before the Christian era, and may be used to-day without
raising the question whether, in the honest expression of opinion, an artist is entitled to use a picture as
Gibbon did a solemn sneer, Heine an epigram, and Huxley a scientific argument. That question is not
raised by this picture, and need;not be argued. But—a point which the uneasy moralists have overlooked —
the picture presents the challenge of Pleasure to Asceticism, but not necessarily Pleasure’s victory. The
joy of life is set in bright light (such bright light—truly wonderful in technique!) against the pangs of
death. Yet, for all that is said by the work to the contrary, the palm may be with that stubborn figure in
the gloom.
Miss Eugenie Duggan ceases to be a
Female Swindler after to-morrow (Friday) night,
and resumes her old role of honest woman. For
on Saturday “ A Life’s Revenge ” will be staged
at Sydney Criterion, and in that well-known
drama Miss Duggan is her persecuted, stainless
self again.
*
A Lady Godiva act is billed for next Wed-
nesday at Wonderland City. The Godiva craze
first broke out in Coventry (Eng.) years and
years ago. Since then it has raged with
intermittent fury, ever growing more and
more innocuous as the centuries sped. The
original Godiva did her horsebacking in so
marked a state of evening dress that—even in that
gay old epoch—the solitary man who gazed upon
her was reckoned a shameful fellow. But the
Wonderland City people plight their troths that
the Godiva they propose presenting won’t bring
the blush of shame to the cheek of a Wesleyan
cleric—and yet she will allure immensely.
** * *
Unless there is an astounding amount of
money to be squandered on shows in Melbourne
this spring, there should be many singed fingers in
the theatrical and allied professions. Just now
every second establishment seems to hold a show
of some kind. Shops have been converted into
picture entertainments of one sort or another ;
all the theatres are in full blast; so are all
the halls. The Women’s Work Exhibition is
in need of a great crowd nightly to make ends
meet. Prince’s Court is whooping for pat-
ronage ; so is Wirths’ Olympia. The Waxworks
hangs its banners on the outer wall, and Cole’s
Hippodrome wants patrons. Clara Butt rolls on
like the mighty deep at the Town Hall ; the
Athenaeum has a topical cinematograph; a Maori
co. is open to business; the Cyclorama runs fights
and varieties; St. Kilda and South Melbourne
Sands, sport, living pictures, concerts, Pierrots,
Figure Eights, and skating rinks ; the Y.R.C. is
running some kind of a side show out at Fleming-
ton ; and Tom Bent is always in action. In short,
visitors to Melbourne are afforded every oppor-
tunity of being tired to death.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal the reign of Meynell
and Gunn started on Saturday with “ The Mid-
night Wedding,” a drama that struck the local
public in its breathing apparatus for the first time.
Up to then there had been a horrible suspicion
that this “Wedding” was a first cousin in art to
the “Fatal” ceremony of yore, and would be
equally open to reproof. Therefore the local
public, in its more intelligent section, was glad to
find that the Wedding, which happens at mid-
night in the cell of an up-to-date Friar
Lawrence, is only one startling incident in a story
of recklessly intense dramatic interest. The
author of this wily production has borrowed some
of the best situations of bygone drama, and when
he couldn’t put his hand on exactly what he
wanted, he probably invented the neces-
sary thrill. There is a fastness and furious-
ness about the incidents. Powerful effects
are led up to in a violent hurry, and
the people in the leading parts get no time for
tiresome lingering over their many pieces of
“fat.” Nothing quite so slick in sensational
melodrama has been previously staged in Mel-
bourne. Moreover, the atmosphere of the action
is new, and the author can be unhesitatingly
credited with the discovery of a new country
where the military system is as full of derange-
ment as a gourmand’s liver.
* * * *
Played by Harcourt Beatty, Gaston Mer-
vale, Miss Congdon, and a new leading lady, the
principal parts go for all they are worth. Miss
Madge Mclntosh, making her first appear-
ance in Melbourne, had the audience with her
from the outset, when she married a strange
gentleman at a moment’s notice to save
herself from the marital attentions of
another man who wanted to marry her for her
own sweet sake, but was vaguely understood to be
villainous. The heroine’s objection to the other
man is based on the purest melodrama principles.
Apart from his sinister smile, he seems a
desirable adorer, and although he presently
obtrudes himself into the lady’s bedroom with
a view to compromising her into lawful matri-
mony, his tactics merely go to show the
sincerity of his suit. All is fair in love and war
except for an alleged villain of the piece. This by
the way. Miss Mclntosh held the audience in all
her scenes, and every time Harcourt Beatty
held Miss M. the audience felt doubly embraced.
Beatty’s part puts even the heroism of Julius
Knight into the shade. His charms are deadly with
the wonienkind, and his sword lays every rival
low. Gaston Mervale closes a career of pic-
turesque usefulness by falling before the hero’s
fatal thrust. And there is the comic relief pro-
vided by Cannan and two other members of the
local army, who jointly and individually pursue
an attractive Kathie, a monotonous proceeding,
which is not too long-drawn out to delight the
gallery. Judging from the applause on Saturday,
“ The Midnight Wedding ” will carry its comic
relief for some weeks.

Wirfchs’ Olympia, which offers seating
accommodation for 5000 worshippers of the
Almighty Animal, is just now providing a Cup
Week programme of suitable horsiness. The
trained gees and the bounding jockey actors are
supported as before by the gentleman who dances
on a rope, and the Herberts, who fly through
space. Also the Lion is lying down with the
Goat as usual. The programme, in a Biblical
manner, asserts that the king of beasts is lying
down with the lamb, but if the sheep’s offspring
was visible in the first instance, it is now safely
concealed in the lion’s interior.

At the Opera House a first part of normal
quality, and a second part of exceeding magnitude
are presented to overflowing crowds of punters,
to whom the name of Rickards is a lode-
stone at all times, but particularly when they
have suffered a bad day at the races, and want to
forget their sorrow. It is at such times that the
misguided punter seeks his only consolation in a
comic song.
At the Bijou a wise management is
running “ Rogues of the Turf ” for a second week,
the moral of that amazing melodrama being like
unto the seed which fell on stony ground, and
made no difference.
** * *
The Princess’s has its German and Grand
Opera back again, apparently quite a smooth,
sanely-working company. Melbourne had ex-
pected a shattered remnant of a company-
ragged tatters, and chasms, and gaps. It finds
the original bevy of members in toto (with the
exception of Herr Greder and Fraulein
Waller, who beam peaceably from “in front,”
and show signs of agreeably re-joining),
“Lohengrin” was staged for Saturday night,
and there was merely one change in the adver-
tised cast. Fraulein Sara Anderson found the
rough Loongana trip upsetting, so little Mia
Barcow played her Elsa. She looked the part
very sweetly, and was in excellent voice ; she only
sang flat once or twice. That little woman wants
a Svengali to keep her voice on the track ; then she
would make something of a grand opera artiste.
Tenor Studemund, who was the Man of
the Chook, suffers from the same painful fail-
ing—his voice rang gloriously at times through
the big performance, but there was always a dread
uncertainty about the trueness of top notes and
other passages. The clarion-voiced Rupp made
the most of his Herald-ship. There is no doubt
that “Lohengrin” is the opera of Riinger and
Fraulein Heinze—they sing magnificently to-
gether, and Heinze did brilliant work in his
second act. Mohwinkel’s fine voice strikes one
with increased emphasis after his absence, and
his Henry the Fowler is a noted performance at
anytime. There was rather an erratic first-night,
not-rehearsed-enough feeling about the chorus
and orchestra, but nothing more than a getting-
in-stride. On Thursday night “Carmen” will be
staged, followed by “Tannhauser” on Friday and
“ The Flying Dutchman ” on Saturday.
* sc-
Bland Holt, who leaves for Europe shortly,
had a gorgeous send-off at the Theatre Royal on
the last night of the last show he will stage here
for some time. Bland was cheered madly by a'
crowded house; when he appeared, flowers,
wreaths, and laurel crowns were handed up ; then
the co. massed together and sang “ Auld Lang
Syne.” The limit was reached when Bland stepped
forward and returned thanks in home-hewn verse.
It was great. Strong programme distributors
broke down, and the very ushers (and a more
impersonal profession than that of ushering does
not exist) found it hard to control their emotions.
Seriously, Bland Holt was a good sort, a barracker
for Australia, a generous employer, a fine worker
in his calling. He will be much missed.

“ Robbery Under Arms ” showed its
lengthy biographical film at the Athenaeum on
Saturday night, for the first time. It’s a bully set ,
of pictures, and they run smoothly without
flicker. One fails to see how Captain Starlight
and his gang manage to get away with the victims
of their depredations, If the loot were all in horses
there might be some hope l —but the cattle they
bang is heart-stirring; 999 shots are fired during
the performance (counted them, of course), and
they are intended to hit the bull’s-eye of the
public for many a night to come.

The Glideaway opened its rollicking
rolling Rink at St. Kilda this week, and a great
crowd bumped their noses —and other parts —on
its granolithic surface without much damage to
either it or them. The glissando movement of
the Glideaway would strew envy in the heart of
any violinist of these parts. The open-air Glide-
away rink promises to be a success.

Julius Knight has had a good week at
Adelaide Royal in “Raffles,” after an absence of
some two years. The feminine interest written
into the play is not very strong (it was entirely
absent in the book), so the absence of Miss Beat-
rice Irwin from the cast has not mattered much.
Miss Elbertorton (her name is now officially
printed all in one piece, without even a hyphen)
has better chances in “Robin Hood,” the rather
trivial prettiness of which has just replaced the
modernity of the Cracksman. Hubert Willis is a
new-comer, who has become popular at once, and
Plimmer and Leslie Victor are always reliable.
The “Besses” have come back for some abso-
lutely last concerts. The Tivoli and Hippodrome
stand where they did, and a new and illusive
moving-picture show has opened all on its own;
it sets its patrons down in a railway-carriage, and
bids them imagine they are “doing ” Switzerland,
or Kamschatka, or other ’popular resorts.

The second half-year begins auspiciously with
the November number, now on sale.
Press criticisms throughout the whole Common-
wealth repeat the same note—that The Lone Hand
improves with every issue. That was the policy
decided upon from the first—to begin tentatively,
and to improve generously, as experience showed
the requirements of the public and the possibilities
of Australian Art and Letters.

Australian matter. No better Christinas present

John Quick, another sick politician
voyaging for his health’s sake, has reached
Westralia. He was tackled there on the duties
on mining machinery, which it is alleged will
seriously affect the. Bull-owned gold mines of the
West. Quick defended the duties, declaring that
there was no reason why the machinery for the
mines could not be made in the Commonwealth,
provided that the industry was properly pro-
tected. In reply to a protest that “natural”
protection ought to be sufficient, Quick retorted
that freight from Melbourne to Fremantle was
255. a ton, while from New York to Fremantle it
was lGs.
One of the incidents that make Arbitration
Courts want to see books instead of relying upon
vague statements has happened in Maoriland. The
Auckland stonemasons claimed higher wages,
better hours, more overtime pay, and preference
to unionists. One builder, representing the em-
ployers, opposed all the demands. He declared it
was impossible to pay higher wages ; the existing
scale of payment was so high that work had to be
refused. As for preference to unionists, the
employers were unanimous that this was nothing
short of brigandage. After some talk like that,
all showing the iniquitous greed of the working
stonemason, the minutes of the Einplovers’ Associ-
ation were called for, and there the Court read a
resolution agreeing, without the least reserve, to
the whole of the men’s demands !
“ Nulla” : There was an accident out our
way recently, when a juggermotornaut did the
usual deed. As my tram approached the spot
marked X in the daily papers, the small man on
the front seat gave me the impression of a person
sitting on an ant-heap. Suddenly he speared the
atmosphere with a crooked forefinger. “There
it is,” he cried in triumph. “Y’ can see th’ blood
yet; there by the v’randah post.” There was a
patch on the roadway which might have been
blood or mud, or the tears of a rich importer who
had wept over the woes of the working classes.
But it absolutely fascinated the small man.
“Blood!” he shrieked. Then he was silent a
space. “But you should ha’seen it yesterday!
There was pools of it yesterd’y.” And not till
we turned the corner did he resume his quiet.
Maoriland “ wind-jammers ” in the timber
trade from Kaipara to Commonwealth ports are
developing a habit of running short of tucker.
Only a week or two since, the Speculant put into
Eden (N.S.W.) short of provisions, whilst on a
voyage from Kaipara to Warrnambool (Vic.); yet
she was then only 34 days out. Such bad judges of
appetites are some owners and agents, that a year
or so ago the Maoriland Government found it
necessary to insist on at least 50 days’ tucker
being on board every sailing vessel leaving the
Dominion for the Commonwealth. But, it seems,
miscalculations still happen. The officer of
Customs at Kaipara states that, when clearing,
the master presented a list showing that he had a
full supply on board ; and if that is so, it looks as
though it may be necessary for the Customs
people in future to spurn a proffered list and
make a personal inspection of the biscuit and the
salt pork.
In addition to the Senate bungle, S. Aus.
has, more or less, tangled itself up over the
Adelaide street-pavement question. A few years
ago F. H. Snow attempted to grab the city’s
tramway rights. The City Council made a
move to get in first with road levels, which
meant “grappling with the pavement problem,” as
the local papers majestically put it. However,
Snow failed to pull off his scheme, so the Council
went to sleep again. Then the Labor Govern-
ment, after several Tory Administrations had
failed, put the tramways on a workable basis, and
provided for electrification in one breath.
Thereupon the City, Council woke with a start,
and made a violent rush to “do something” to
the streets. A Melbourne tender was accepted
for paving King William, Bundle and Hindley
streets with jarrah blocks at a cost of £35,000,
subject to the confirmation of a special rate.
Fat potted the special rate at the poll. The City
Council thereupon casually accepted a second
tender from the same contractor to woodblock
King William-street for £15,000. Fat was flabber-
gasted, and an injunction was immediately hurled
at the Council to restrain it from proceeding with
the work. The Council snapped up J. H. Symon,
and is now saying “Come on”; and there is no
particular rush to “ come.”

Mr. Arthur Leplastrier, of Sydney, after having
toured the world, says he has not seen or heard of a
better Steam Engine than the “ Robey,” and that means
a range from Portable or Traction Engines to the most
scientific Drop Valve Triple Expansion Engines used in
the numerous enormous electrical installations. The
“ Robey ” Engine is now admitted to be the “Niagara” of
the steam world. Australians, although ahead in most
things, have not yet quite grasped the value of the higher
class of Wood-Working Machinery, and the firm with the
widest reputation in the Old World is A. Ransome and
Co. Ltd., of Newark-on-Trent, England, whose distribu-
tion in the past has been so great that it was useless
extending their propaganda to Australia. Mr. Leplastrier
has, however, now completed arrangements whereby
every person interested in saw-milling can obtain full
information about these high-class Wood-Workin«-
Machines.*

The mariner goes to the play, and then writes this:—
“ I don’t believe Capt. Stirling Whyte, of ‘ Brewster’s
Millions ’ (present port, Theatre Royal), has a master’s
certificate. If he had, he wouldn’t put the starboard
light on the port side of liis yacht, and, when signalling
‘ distress,’ he wouldn’t use flags on a pitch dark night.
Then again, it’s just as well to furl sails when your boat
is struck by a gale.”
Miss Lenore White, “ The Californian Venus,” writes to
this paper in bitter protest against other poseuses who
are not genuine Venuses “imitating her business.” It
seems the lady is thus caused much pain of mind and she
blushes to think of the shameless imitators who disrobe
on false pretences. The Bullktin has a good deal of
sympathy with Miss White. It, once in the long ago, saw
a posing Venus in a theatre who was about six feet seven
inches high and had three ample chins. Her pretty face
was like a Paphian dove roosting on billows of flesh, and
in this paper’s opinion no lady in the artistic disrobing
line should have more than two chins.
J.A.O. : S. M. Herald (26/10/’O7) writes apropos a
young Australian actor, who recently went to London •
He was in ‘The Judgment of Pharaoh’ at the Scala
Theatre London which entirely failed after arduous
(unpaid) rehearsal, a fact which opened the wanderer’s
eyes to the fact that actors on this side are exempt from
such calamities.” The comment is misleading. The
calamities ” are as frequent here as elsewhere. Actors
are not paid for rehearsal, and failures are not unknown.
I may cite One instance out of many I know : A company
with which the late Martin Simonsen was connected'
rehearsed an opera at Melbourne Theatre Royal for six
weeks. The ppera was played seven nights, and
then went sky-high. The performers were “ balanced,”
and- the “divvy” was 3s. 6d.—6d. a performance,
or, counting rehearsals, 6d. a week. One of the compan v
made a present of this wealth to the management, with
the remark that such a hard cheek might want moisten-
ing. Payment for rehearsals ”is one of the planks of a
new Vaudeville society, not long ago established in Lon-
don, but that end has not yet been attained. There is
something to be said for and against payment for re-
hearsals. Take a case. A revival of an old, well-known
opera is contemplated, and a chorus is engaged. Among
them will be many who know the opera backwards, as
the saying goes, and many will be novices. It would be
manifestly unfair to the management to pay these for
learning what they have to do, and, on the other hand
it is unfair to those who do know the work to have
to attend day after day, simply that they mav be of
assistance to the others. I think it would be a fair thing
to pay half-salary during rehearsals to those who could
do without them. lam told that Mr. Allan Hamilton is
now paying half-salaries for rehearsals, when the per-
former is not already a member of the company and in
receipt of full salary.

The curtain rises discovering- the co. in various attitudes
of shrinking* modesty. It falls and rises again on a
diminished number of shrinkers. Again it falls, and,
rising for the last time, reveals Julius just about to depart
from the stage. With the slightest start he notices that
something is up (it is the curtain, in point of fact), and
with just a tinge of reluctance he turns and lowers his
eyelids to the homage of the audience.
“S.” : “Li Sylphe,” the American dancer at Melb.
Opera House, has not been the success she deserved to
be. Her dancing is superlatively good. She is one of the
very few experts who can high-kick with elegance.
Thousands can kick high, but few ean do it with anything
of the poetry of motion. The high kick is usually a
spasmodic jolt, that seems to rick all the joints of the
kicker, and threatens to shoot her mechanical teeth into
the orchestra, but with La Sylphe it is a
flame-like rhythmical flow of the limh, as graceful
as the uplifting of a lily before the spring zephyr. This
slim and pretty Yankee has had no superior in Australia
as a poetic dancer, but her turn is a little too quiet, a
trifle too refined for Australian vaudeville. A spice more
devil is necessary. Above all, La Sylphe should tear
down the absurd setting in which she is placed. The
spangled fairy-dell sort of trellis-work background, which
looks as if it had been filched from a pantomime, gets
mixed up with the dancer’s evolutions, and is ruinous to
the effect. A plain black or white cloth setting (accord-
ing to costume) is what La Sjdphe’s turn demands.

Road Liuffe, a mortally-wounded Frontiersman, loq. to
“ 8 mend:—“ I told you of a supper I had one night at
the Savoy—do you remember? There was a young
Indian prince in England then. He dined in high places
and supped at the Savoy with the coryphees ; and both
in the high places and among the corvphCes his jewels
had made him welcome. This is the truth lam telling
you. Well, after supper that night, he threw a girl down
the stairs. Never mind what she was—she was of the
white ruling race—she was of the race that rules India.”
Vhich resurrects an actual happening of a few years
back, the assaulter being a polo-playing nigger, of im-
mense popularity in Court and sporting circles. The lady
who went down stairs without touching a step was his
mistress, and a musical-comedy star of wide fame. The
episode didn’t damage either party. A well-to-do nigger
can do most things in the amazing old Cold Country
without losing his halo, and', so far as the lady was con-
cerned, the prevailing feeling in her clan was that to be
slung downstairs by a potentate of color constitutes a
distinct tribute and a token of regard.

Hamer”: Governors have their uses. Once when
writer was attached to the management of a company
that struck a state capital in desperate circumstances,
he took the clothes he wore when he was a lord or some
similar circumstance from his property box, burnished
his cheek, and hired a cab with his last half-crown. He
aidn t expect to see the Governor, but he tried and he
aid see him ; and, moreover, he secured the Governor’s
attendance on the opening night. The Governor’s
attendance paid some back salaries, several bailiffs, and
a mortgage on the scenery; there were packed houses
ana enthusiastic press notices. If there had been no
Governor, Cie show, being merely an Australian one,
under Australian management, would have had empty
houses as far as the D.O. was concerned, and the papers
would have dealt out their notices with sarcastic super-
ciliousness. 1
The Countess of Rosslyn, who used to be Miss Anna
Robinson, till she picked up with the amazing nobleman
whom she divorced the other day, is taking to the boards
again and will “lead”at the Apollo Theatre, in “The
iiducatnon of Elizabeth,” by Horniman. Competent
statisticians hold that a divorce is worth to an actress in
_ogland at least five years’ training ; if she is not only a
divorceuse but a countess as well, she steps, without
further parley, into the Bernhardt class.
The new Drury Lane success, “The Sins of Society”
played five hours at rehearsal, and had to be blue-
penc'lled savagely to maintain any semblance of a plot,
while leaving in the three big sensation scenes (Long-
champs racecourse, a Thames boating scene, with a dive
over a real water weir, and the sinking of a troopship).
Out went the whole of a freak banquet, with the pluto-
crats neglecting their babies (if any) for their dogs • and
out went a church scene. This last was no real loss, for
the militant clergyman simply turned up at the ladies’
club, and gave his anti-Bridg - e sermon there.

Allan Hamilton, who is spending a few months in
Hobart, has been collecting- reasons why the Slowbartians
do not more liberally patronise music and the drama.
Result, so far, of his investigation :
(1) Because the theatre is opposite the morgue.
(2) Because it’s too near the end of the month.
(3) Because the theatre street is badiv lighted.
(4) Because a lot of people go to Sydney every Easter.
(5) Because it’s no use trying- to get in—the theatre is
so small.
(6) Because I saw 6. V. Brooke there 40 years ago, and
don’t want to forget that pleasant evening-.
(7) Because the management hasn’t sent “ passes.”
(8) Because they charge 6d. for early doors.
(9) Because they charge only 3d. to‘see a lantern show
while the theatre charges 35., 25., and Is.
(10) Because there will be a new theatre soon—in about
90 years—when we shall get a better seat.
(11) Because entertainments provided by the gramo-
phone shops are free.
Hall Caine, author of “ The Boomster ’’—the person who
aims to produce a facial effect somewhere midway
between the Redeemer and W. Shakspeare, but who
only succeeds in the ultimate resort in looking like a
dejected ram in a top-hat—has broken forth anew. He
gave about 300 parsons free seats, t’other night, for his
new play—a sexual hair-raiser, by all accounts, that puts
“ The Sign of the Cross ” in the icebox for all time. Of
course, they came, and were as edified as parsons mostly
are at this kind of slush. And then, framed in a circle of
admiring and foolish clerics, Hall the Awful played
his trump ad. His play, he explained, had a moral pur-
pose. That was the sole reason he wrote it. He (Hall)
had made a close study of females lost, stolen, strayed
and otherwise undone and mislaid, or left chargeable on
the parish. He wanted a mighty mansion built for them
by the millionaires whom he desired should come to see
his play. This mansion was to be a combination of a
Jew palace in Park Lane and a bad dream out of the
Apocalypse. And the man in charge “ must be without
a stain on his escutcheon, well born, well taught, with a
heart like a lion and the soul of a god ; a nobleman, if
possible, that no lady of rank may be unwilling to serve
Under his banner ; a gentleman, that no poor girl may be
afraid to come to him in her trouble ; a martyr, that no
failure can frighten, no ingratitude dishearten, and no
temptation gainsay.” In short, he was to be ’All Caine
or some other god of the sort.
Criminal is the dilatoriness of the alder-
man. In Murwillumbah, which had its business
end badly charred recently (largely through
lack of a water supply and effective fire brigade);
the principal business of a Municipal Council
meeting was to be the consideration of a water'
supply for fire-fighting purposes, and Widening the
main street to lessen the risk of fire crossing it, as
occurred during the recent- flare. And the
council meeting lapsed for want of a quorum.
The inquiry as to the value of the vast
Peel River estate at Tamworth, which the
N.S.W. Government intends to resume under the
compulsory section of the Closer Settlement Act,
has now begun. An expensive Bar has been en-
gaged, and since the inquiry will last some
weeks, Costs already begin to loot the future
settlers. Further, under the Act, the Govern-
ment must resume the whole of the estate (some
hundreds of thousands of acres, including the
homestead and all improvements). As these are
not required for closer settlement, their purchase
will materially add to the price per acre
which will have to be paid by the settlers.
This provision in the O.S. Act requiring the re-
sumption of the whole of any estate resumed
under the compulsory clauses, has been found to
operate against the interests of the Government,
and W ade proposes to bring in a Bill to amend
the Act in this particular. Seeing this, it would
have been as well to have delayed resuming
Peel River until the amending Act was law.
But a graduated land tax would save all the bother
and expense.
“Boll,” from a place that might be
further from Melbourne than it is :
Some women lead bandit’s lives. Struck a little girl
lately who earns an honest crust doing sewing. She
lived for a time rent free in a lonely, out-of-the-way,
God-forsaken district, in return for her services as care-
taker of the Mechanics’ Institute—a weird, ghostly, rat-
haunted barn. She was supposed to guard a few standard
mouldy works provided by a long-forgotten town coun-
cillor, who had intended them for the improvement of
the minds of the surrounding farmers and their families.
The surrounding farmer, as a rule, doesn’t pine to read
David Copperfield ” or “ Bleak House,” and Miss
Siirroundinr Parmer prefers the Family Herald, where
duke sports at large. The patrons of this poor
little soul paid her in kind—that is, when she sent in her
near, 1 tile account for the making of a dress, etc., they
would i?nd along a bag of onions, or a few dozen
eggs, Or half-a-side of bacon, or the offer of the
loan of a plough, or any old thing that hap-
pened to be lying around, and she either had to
trade with the grocer—who was himself getting pretty
sour on the “in kind” system of doing business—or
subsist on bacon without bread, or onions without bacon,
until the next job came along. There came a time when
nothing came along for a few weeks, and the little dress-
maker’s dietary scale got down to black tea and an unholy
concoction she called bread. Then she developed
“ nerves,” and took to sleeping with a loaded revolver on
one side and a tomahawk on the other. At this point
the local schoolmaster and his wife took a hand and broke
in to the rescue. They were received with firearms and
hysterical cries for help; but, notwithstanding, they
gathered up the half-starved dressmaker-caretaker and
bore her off. The standard works are now left to the
care of the rats and spiders.

THE MAN OUTSIDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The actor-manager bestrode
With mighty feet the quaking stage,
And with his insults strove to goad
The good young man to blinder rage.
He’d snarl, and grin, and growl, and nod,
And scowl just like a heathen god.
The audience with eyes intent,
(There were just twenty-one of it)
All wondered what the villain meant.
Complainants murmured in the pit.
.When someone clamored at the door
The heavy villain ceased to roar,
And at the humble rouseabout
He shook his fist, with anger red.
“What’s that infernal row, you lout?”
“ Please, sir,” the ticket-taker said,
“A man outside, who’s at a crack,
He says he wants his money back ! ”

The more we master the forces of nature, the
more do our chances of accidents multiply, even as the
tamer’s dangers increase in proportion to the number of
wild animals which he “ puts through their tricks ” in
the cage. Formerly, we avoided the contact of these
forces as much as possible ; to-day, they have gained
admittance to our household. And so, notwithstanding
our more prudent and peaceable manners, it happens to us
more often than to our fathers to look pretty closely upon
death. The South British Insurance Company of 12
Bridge Street, Sydney, issues policies covering all classes
of accident and seventeen (17) specified sicknesses for
£2 2s. per annum upwards—special policies for your
special needs.*

colic, or a pain in the stomach.* ,

PENEOLD'S

“ Petramos ” : With me the study of
pants is a favorite pastime. I have made careful
observations of the trousers of Methodist minis-
ters, always with the same result. They never
fit. Never, never, never! Ido not state this
with the object of raising the sectarian issue, but
merely as a cold statement of a sad truth. See
the glossy silk hat of the Methodist parson ! See
your reflection in the shiny surface of his immacu-
late collar ! Gaze on his well-brushed coat !
Then droop your eyes to his trousers. His mysti-
fying trousers ! His frayed-at-the bottom pants!!
His trousers may fit him when they come from the
tailors, but never for long after.

Sydney Harbor foreshores are now
guarded against the picnicker and the visitor
and the artist in an almost ferocious manner.
The landlord is always lying in wait to order
people back to their boats (as was done when
the yacht lolanthe, sheltering from a storm, was
forced to put out by a landlord and had seven of
its occupants, men, women and children, drowned),
and to enforce his orders he has sometimes a
pack of bloodhounds, which he lets loose if the
landing party exhibits any tardiness in obeying
orders to leave.
Last week the Government of N.S.W., in
cold blood and with solemn ritual, broke the
neck of Nicholas Baxter, whom its Courts
had found guilty of the murder of the aged, help-
less Mrs. Macnamara. He was a useless drunkard
and a cowardly murderer, if that is any mitiga-
tion of the Law’s homicide. But Nicholas Baxter
had a wife and three little children, the youngest
of them a baby in arms. They had nothing to do
with his crime, but they have to bear perhaps the
greatest portion of the punishment of it.
Nicholas Baxter’s agony lasted seconds—theirs
will endure for long and bitter years. They are
left dependent on charity, and your hangman’s
rope is a great strangler of charity. Humanity
and Justice demand that where the Law, in the
cause of justice, takes the life of a man or woman,
it should provide for the maintenance of the crim-
inals’s dependents. In so-called barbarous Russia,
when the Law casts a man into gaol, it makes him
work for the benefit of his family, and sees that
the family gets the benefit of the work. The
present law of N.S. W. not only punishes the actual
offender, but, more harshly, his wife and children.
Premier Wade is said to be a humane man, with
an eye specially on the rights of helpless children.
Here is an opportunity to prove it.
* * * *
The efficacy of hanging as a deterrent to
murder was strikingly illustrated in Sydney last
week. On Tuesday morning Baxter was suspended
for the Enmore murder ; the same afternoon, just
about the time when the community should have
been struck rigidly moral by the “awful vindica-
tion of the law,” a man fired three point-blank
revolver shots at a policeman. He didn’t kill the
policeman—but it wasn’t the morning’s business
at Darlinghurst that stopped him.
Civilisation isn’t always properly appre-
ciative of what it owes to the churches. For
example, it is not generally known that it is due
to the careful researches of a clergyman that the
present humane method is adopted of regulating
the length of the drop to the weight of a criminal
about to be hanged. Some time in the ’seventies
there was an execution in Dublin, and owing to
some bungling the man was decapitated, the head
being literally torn from the body. The late Rev.
Samuel Haughton, Fellow of Trinity College,
Dublin, a well-known mathematician and scientist,
joint author of Galbraith and Haughton’s
Manuals, took the subject in hand as a mathe-
matical problem. His position as Dean of the
Faculty of Medicine, in the Medical School of the
University of Dublin, gave him the opportunity
of utilising subjects provided for dissection. Ex-
periments were made, theories propounded, and
tested, the strength and resistance of the various
tissues of the neck investigated, the increase in
velocity of falling bodies from varying heights
calculated. The whole inquiry evolved a com-
plete mathematical formula, culminating in the
demonstration of how to scientifically deprive a
human being of life by breaking the neck without
severing the head from the body. Under the
supervision of the Reverend Professor the next
condemned was carefully weighed, the tonicity and
breaking strain of the neck mathematically esti-
mated, and the length of drop allotted. The pro-
fessor detailed the result at a meeting of the Bio-
logical Society of the University :
I am very happy to inform you that the subject met
with a painless death ; the neck was completely broken,
while none of the soft tissues were lacerated. My com-
panion Fellows and I were hurriedly departing from the
scene of the execution, when my hand was suddenly
seized by someone and most cordially shaken. It was
the hangman, who greeted me':—“Be gorrah, sir, didn’t
we do that well ! ”
“ Watto ” : Our Aunt Mary went down to
Sydney to recuperate. Everything went well till
someone persuaded her to take a sarsaparilla mix-
ture before each meal as a tonic. Aunt Mary, who
takes everything recommended by everybody,
procured some immediately, and started the
treatment at once. The result was fearful:
At afternoon tea she was voluble, but at
dinner she talked with such emphasis, and
gave vent to such startling opinions that
those who heard were amazed. During
the evening she startled the house with
wild bursts of hyaena laughter, and greeted
the appearance of supper with a loud
howl. About midnight the housemaid re-
ported that the old lady in the back bal-
cony room was “ goin’ on somethin’ awful.”
The landlady found Aunt on the bed, waving
her booted feet in the air. The sarsaparilla
tonic was firmly removed, and a respectable
lady, who always votes No-License, quickly
sobered up.
There have been ructions again in the
Sydney Immigration League. Dr. Arthur, its
first founder, has been turned out of the position
of president, and, taking with him some of the
most earnest workers, has founded a new organi-
sation. That Arthur is often tactless must be
admitted, and he has given some occasions for
criticism. Still, after all, he is the Immigration
League, and that body’s action in turning him
down is practically equivalent to cutting off its
own head. A flighty head is sometimes a worry ;
but it’s on the whole better than an amputated
head.

N.S.W. Shearers’ Hut Accommodation Act
finds it difficult to get into its stride. It took
years of Labor agitation to pass the measure, and
when at last it went on the Statute Book, it was
with a condition that it should not be enforced
until it was proclaimed. It remained a dead
letter season after season, year after year ; one
Minister found the drought was so severe that
pastoralists could not be expected to go to expense
over huts ; and another Minister discovered floods
taxed all the squatters’ resources; and still another
Minister instituted inquiries, and either never
received answers or forgot to act upon them.
Lately the Garruthers Government, pushed to the
wall, agreed to enforce the Act and to prosecute
wherever pastoralists were found evading the
provisions which were intended to ensure health
and decency. But, of course, there had to be
evidence of such evasion, and the Government
appointed in most districts the local police to do
the inspection and compile the evidence against
the squatter, or pastoralist company, or banking
corporation. And that doesn’t work satisfactorily.
Now there is a demand to place the inspection on
a different and independent footing.

During a recent visit to Sydney, Queens-
land Registrar-General Weedon made arrange-
ments by which copies of the records of births,
deaths, and marriages for Moreton Bay, Ipswich,
and Darling Downs districts, prior to
the separation of Queensland from
N. S. Wales, are to lie supplied to
the Queensland Government. The
records go back as far as 1829.
But they don’t represent all
the births, deaths, and mar-
riages of the period. Before
State registration arrived
the parson was the only
recorder of these domes-
tic events, and the parson
didn’t pervade the country
in the ’thirties with any-
thing like his present
numerousness. Among the
names of clergymen on
the marriage lines the
most noticeable are those
of Benjamin Glennie (Angli-
can archdeacon), John Han-
ley (Roman Catholic dean), and
William Lambie Nelson (father
of Queensland ex-Premier Nelson). Nelson,
Griffith, Reid, Playford, Higgins, and Way were
notable instances of how the parson’s offspring
takes to politics.
* * * *
As The Bulletin announced more than
six months ago, Dr. Tidswell’s report as to the
introduction to Australia of the Danysz virus for
rabbit-poisoning is altogether condemnatory.
When publicity was given in these columns to the
fact—ascertained on unimpeachable authority—
there were all sorts of threats of “proceedings’’
and so on from interested folk. Now, what was
their object ? They knew as well as we did that
what was published was a statement of the truth.
Was there any idea of passing on the financial
baby in the minds of some of those who had put
money into the experiments ?
* * * *
N.S.W. Government now refuses to hand
over the Cataract reservoir to the Water and
Sewerage Board. Sydney is indebted to the
Government for the fact that the reservoir is
tainted with rotten vegetation—a taint that the
Government insisted on in spite of the Board’s
protests. Presumably now the Government
intends keeping the reservoir under its control,
lest by some mischance it should be allowed to
become clean.

Urging that it was quite safe to let the
Hindu hawker into Australia, Mrs. Molyueux
Parkes told the Anti-Sosh Women’s Conference
in Melbourne: —
The caste prejudice of the Indian is very strong. The
race was inclined to look down upon ours, and- conse-
quently she thought the male would not stoop to mar-
riage with our girls.
That probably explains why the high-caste Hindu
gentlemen, as many as a dozen of them in a
noisome camp on the N.S.W. North Coast, all
refer to their white “housekeeper” as “my
wife.” Not one of them would “stoop to mar-
riage”; they just get along without that. Is it
Aunty-Sosh’s theory that, so long as the white
woman doesn’t go to the marriage registrar with
Ram Chunder, it doesn’t matter where else she
goes with him ?

Miss Robinson’s diary, in the Druce case,
appears to be an unfortunate book. Miss Robin-
son is from Maoriland and a witness in the case.
At one time the lady reported the diary as having
been stolen from her luggage during the voyage,
but, after an exciting hunt, the precious volume
was found in the bottom of a trunk. Now it is
alleged to have been snatched from her in a
London street.
LEST ALGERNON SHOULD LEARN TO SWEAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A convention of the Tasmanian W.C.T.U. has con-
sidered the proposal for compulsory military training: of
the youth of the Commonwealth, and has come to the
conclusion “ that such training-would prove demoralising
to the young men of our State in that they would be
leaving their homes at a most critical time of their lives.”
A memorial setting out this view of the case has been
presented by Mr. Storrer to the House of Representa-
tives.
Australia may be wiped out by the Chow !
But better that than Algernon should swear.
The Jap may over-run our land ; but how
If Willy should get vine leaves in his hair?
Liver, and black and tan, and mud-hues may
Pollute the white man’s fount unto his shame 1
But better that than e’er should come the day
When Samuel knows the wicked two-up game !
A Jap may be the Argus editor 1
The newest bishop’s name may be Ah Cow 1
But better that disgrace, and even more,
Than little Tommy’s “ fall ” should wring our brow !
Better the Commonwealth should fall down dead !
Better good soldiers we should direly lack, or
Not even one white man should raise his head,
Than Willy know the odor of terbacker !
Football is wrong and rough ! Boxing is rude 1
He who goes swimming shall not come to grace !
You shall become a cross ’twixt pimp and prude
An you but listen to Miss Frostiface 1
Australia ! Splendid name 1 He who attaints
Her by neglect but gives her to the alien 1
Down! Down! you weak-kneed, timid, meddling
saints—
The better Christian is the good Australian.
VIC.
MARTIN LUTHER.

Norman Lindsay’s “ Pollice Yerso” has
been sold to Melbourne National Gallery for
150 guineas. It is the biggest price ever
paid in Australia for a black-and-white.
This tribute to a great artist is the more
pleasing because the picture in question was bit-
terly attacked by the ignorant and the jealous
when first shown in Sydney in 1905. The Royal Art
Society was inclined to refuse it a “show ” at all;
when wall space was grudgingly given it was near
the skyline where the flies roost. A suggestion by
one or two critics that the Sydney National
Gallery should purchase an obvious masterpiece
was treated with cold scorn. It is a good thing
for Australia that Melbourne is wiser.
Sydney National Gallery, it may be remarked
again at this juncture (the remark should be made
every week, in the hope that it will get to the dull
perceptions of the mostly inartistic and artless
trustees), has not a single masterpiece of Aus-
tralian black-and-white. Phil May, Hopkins, and
Lindsay have done in that medium work which is
on the top level of world’s achievement. They
all, in a sense, belong to Sydney, as having done
their best work here; but their city’s gallery
knows them not.
* * * *
“ Louis Esson ” on “ Pollice Yerso ” :
. So far, the theme of the drawing attracts more atten-
tion than its art. Its Nietzschean ethic, its “ blasphemy,”
its “candor”—by such accidents are too many people
attracted or repelled. There is a certain section of
dilettanti which considers that whatever is supposed to
be immoral must necessarily be inartistic ; and there is
another _ section which considers that whatever is
immoral is therefore highly artistic. For these reasons
many people have acquired a cheap reputation for
morality by condemning-“ Pollice Yerso ” ; while many
others, _by praising it, have acquired a still cheaper
reputation for immorality. Art has nothing to do with
morals (that is, in the theological sense of that abused
word), though, as Verhaeren maintains, every masterpiece
is part of the conscience of the world. But an artist’s
professed philosophy need never be taken very seriously ;
and it matters little whether Norman Lindsay be a Neo-
Pagan, an Early Christian, oran esoteric Buddhist. Jew,
Christian, or Heathen may all equally delight in this real
masterpiece.
Truly, it is a noble composition. The figure on the
Cross is handled with exquisite skill, and artistic
sympathy ; the landscape is beautiful and mysterious, the
clouds fine in color ; the “ line ” of the passing Pagans, an
heroic group, heroically visioned, sweeps grandly, though
two or three of the foreground figures are, perhaps, some-
what too equal in tone value. The work is rhythmically
decorative, and glows with color. In black-and-white art
Norman Lindsay has no compeers; indeed, few even of
the old masters ever used the pen more richly. Black-and-
white, the Cinderella of the arts, which too often returns
to base uses, he has raised to one of the most serious
media for the expression of modernity.
' * * * *
Nearly £4OOO has been spent this year on
art works in Melbourne, which has made sudden
claim to be an Australian Athens or Florence.
Amateurs like Prof. Spencer and L. Abrahams
have already begun to make private collections,
and other men of affairs seem likely to emulate
these local Medici. The Felton bequest has set
the example. The Melbourne man begins to feel
like an old Athenian, or Florentine, or modern
burgess of Amsterdam or Glasgow—he must
live up to the reputation of his art-loving city.
Every happy purchase—the Rodin, the Pissarro,
the Corot, the Fremiet, the Norman Lindsay—is
a fresh stimulus. Sydney, which was once a home
of the arts, does not develop in the same direction.
It will degenerate into a provincial city if it con-
tinues to turn its thumb down on the things of the
intellect, and actually to take pride in its Philis-
tinism.

The exemption certificates scheme, under
the Sydney Shop Assistants’ Arbitration award,
gives the sweating employer the opportunity to
victimise his employees, and he seems to rush it.
The employees in some Sydney houses are plainly
given to understand that these certificates must
be applied for, or they must accept dismissal.

penal offence is badly wanted on the North Coast
of N.S.W. Picturesque Maclean, on the

alliances between white women
and Kanakas. It was in that
locality thatthenotorious
Jane Davis, now ser-
ving a life sentence
in Darlinghurst for
giving leper
Tommy Abra-
hams a dose of
strychnine, used
to “keep house”
for some half-
dozen Couth
Sea Islanders.
Up the river
from Grafton
there is a white
woman who has for-
saken a white hus-

some ten or a dozen Hin-
dus. Of course, she is not
legally married to them ; the
parson is guiltless, owing to some
defect in the law which does not
allow him to marry one white woman
to several Hindus. But each nigger
always refers to her as “ my wife.”
A nice gentlemanly fellow, who admitted
over 30 prior convictions, was charged at Fitzroy
(Melb.) last week with assaulting a cripple-
woman. The evidence showed that he had
knocked her down, knelt on her chest, bitten
through her cheek, and otherwise brutally ill-
treated her, one constable saying that he thought
a murder had been committed when he entered
the house and found blood spattered over the
walls and floor. A cruel Bench fined the poor
fellow £5 ! There was only one J.P. on the Bench,
which was hard luck for the accused. Had there
been three he might have been discharged with a
caution.
B enley-on-t he-Yarra is a decent spree,
prejudiced in the Australian mind by a deplorable
title. It is saddening to reflect that young Aus-
tralia can’t take his girl out in a decorated punt
and frivol on the river in the liquid gold of his
native sunshine without being reminded of the
capers of an effete aristocracy on a grease-spattered
stream in a cold island on the other side of the
world. In England there is a Henley, and
the name belongs, but the superior Melbournian
hasn’t sufficient self-respect to find a name of
his own and a method of his own when he wishes
to be giddy up the river; so to be intensely
English he names his escapade after a caper in a
far land, and in his efforts narrowly escapes being
miserable. This waterside holiday may easily
become a grand national institution if the people
are permitted to give free play to their own
cheerful Australian inclinations to evolve a
carnival of spring suitable to the spirit of the
time, the climate, and the country. Meanwhile
the name fetters imagination, and limits the
spree to a benighted effort on the part of a few
fashionables to be intensely Hinglish for the
mortification of the local democracy.
* * * #
Tom Bent does not get much respect from
Melbourne crowds, but his touch of megalomania
pulls him through. He was the most barracked
performer at Henley-on-the-Drain, on Saturday
week, and whenever his motor boat drew near
the south bank he was chivvied with a blatant dis-
regard for his high estate and his enormous im-
portance. In fact, he was popularly accepted as
chief clown of the festival. Once when the wash
of his tub interfered with the rowing of
an eight, the stroke ripped out a streak
of abusive language in the face of the
Premier, and passionately reminded Tommy that
he didn’t own the lurid river. Tom always re-
torted on his critics, but in this lattter case the
“eight” was a quarter of a mile off while Tom
was still on his legs, gesticulating, and throwing
remarks into space. He only ceased when a boat-
load of youths collided with his craft, in the hope
of precipitating a valuable politician of vast bulk
headlong into the stream. Even after he had
toppled for two seconds on the awful verge of a
national calamity, and when eager hands dragged
him down into a safe position squat on the bottom
of the boat, Tommy was not disconcerted. You
couldn’t wound Bent’s dignity with a hundred-ton
gun, for the same reason that you couldn’t nip a
guinea-pig’s tail with a Nasmyth hammer.

“Hiphen” tells how Sarah got into
Society :
I am much about that maligned stream, the Yarra.
Among my river acquaintances is a humble barge, The
Giddy Sarah. Daily, with several sisters, she conveys
mud to the sea. She is useful, but unlovely. She ‘is
broad of beam and ungainly of gait. She has a blows.y,
raffish, seen-lmtter-da.vs appearance. She is a mud barge
with a past. Yet her heart is good, though her smell is
bad. . . .
On Henley-on-the-Yarra night we were drifting around
in a skiff, Youth in the prow and Beauty at the helm. (I
wa-steering.) We were admiring the house-boats, the
illuminations, and the fireworks.
We stopped, smell-bound, before one fairy palace, an
electric-lighted bower of roses. From it floated on the
balmy air, etc., soft, love-compelling strains of music, the
silvery laughter of lovely women and the popping of
champagne corks, etc.
I asked myself “Ami in Venice?” I replied to my-
self, “ I am not sure.”
Then something familiar struck me about that gorgeous
house-boat.
I took a closer survey.
I rubbed my eyes.
I hastily enumerated the drinks I had had.
No, I was not dreaming. Alas, I was sober.
It was, it was, The Giddy Sarah.
Under all her paint and powder, her fal-lals and her
finery, I recognised her battered, mud-sodden features.
Poor old mud-barge ! She knew that I knew her.
Never have I seen anything so self-conscious, ashamed
and uncomfortable. In charity I passed on without a
word, leaving her with that fashionable crowd, cham-
pagne stains on her sham decks. . . .
On Monday, after the regatta, The Giddy Sarah was
her disreputable self again. I saw her rollicking con-
tentedly down stream with several of fier dissolute com-
panions, the rich, aromatic river mud oozing over her fat
plebeian sides.

* * * *
MajorP. G. Pennymore, William Sandford’s
manager for his first blast furnace, goes back to
England shortly, having finished his stipulated
job of putting the furnace in running order. At
a valedictory meeting, the retiring manager said
it came as a surprise to him that so few trained
men were required to start the works. So
proficient had the Australian workmen become,
that Pennymore said his successor, now on his
way from England, would have an easy job.
Pennymore also remarked that he was convinced
that Australian workmen could adapt themselves
to anything, and that the progress made
would be a credit to any class of workmen any-
where in the world. Pennymore’s advt. for the
Australian workman should be plastered on the
front gate of the Stinking Fish party, whose
creed is that Australia is incapable of making a
chair leg or bass broom.
THE BURDEN OF SEX.

“ H.G.” : Apropos of this fuss in Sydney
because three suburban mayors wanted people to
wear more clothes when they bathe. Why
should Australians perpetuate the dirty habit of
wearing clothes at all ? A cultured, scientifically-
trained man or woman who had been brought up
without a knowledge of clothing would revolt at
the very idea. The human being respires through
his skin as well as through his lungs, and by wear-
ing clothing he is acting just about as logically as
if he plugged up one of his nostrils in the in-
terests of decency. Nobody who wears clothes can
be properly clean unless he chooses to change his
undergarments at least a dozen times every day.
The wearing of clothes dates from the time when
sundry human beings were driven by more
vigorous races into northern climates, never in-
tended for human occupation. The fugitives—
obviously the inferior race—-were forced to choose
between perishing quickly from cold and being
slowly suffocated by placing obstructions in front
of the pores of their skins. They chose the lesser
evil, and, in the course of generations, their de-
scendants became immune to the deadly effects of
partial suffocation, just as they became im-
mune to the deadly effects of alcohol,
measles, and other pests which, together with
clothes, devastate the races unused to them.
Now the clothes-wearing habit has been im-
ported, with many other objectionable habits,
into Australia, where it is not necessary to live
uncleanly for more than three months of the year.
The decency of the matter is merely a conven-
tion, and, anyhow. Nature has provided a natural
garb for human beings in the tan she places on
their skins. The new surf-bather, with his
white arms and legs, resembling something that
has been turned up under a stone, is certainly
grotesquely and hideously obscene. But see him
six months later. His warm brown skin is a gar-
ment in itself, and he ought to be able to stray
anywhere attired in nothing else without giving
offence or attracting a rude glance.
“ Rosack ” : Have any Bulletin readers
tested pine-apple juice as a strengthener of the
lungs and throat ? Have heard that some doctors
recommend it as a remedy for diphtheria if taken
at the first sign of the disease, but cannot say so of
my own knowledge. But I can vouch for its
curative effect on sore throats and inflamed
bronchial surfaces.
Patrons no longer discharge bouquets at
those theatrical performers who please them, and
it is not now usual to see a plump soprano smitten
violently in the main-spring of her high C with a
hard mass of botanical specimens, hurled by an
admirer in the stalls; but the floral contribution
continues to be a nuisance and an outrage at
theatres. Now-a-nights the sweet pea and the
rhododendron have to be delivered to the astonished
soprano by a long line of assistants and an active
actor man, who gallantly seizes the samples as
they are served up, and hangs them about the
fair lady, propping the surplus against her
feet, while the other actresses stand round, smil-
ing like fiends, and looking so stark and distressed
that every feeling heart in the audience bleeds for
them. The actress who does not receive a bouquet
when half a florist’s stock is being dumped on the
stage looks so like the leading juvenile at a hang-
ing that the giving of flowers should be wholly
suppressed at the instance of the Society for the
Prevention of Cruelty to Actors. In any case it
is unreasonable, inartistic, and absurd to delay a
famished audience, lusting for Chablis and
oysters or scrambled Frankfurters and hot spuds,
while a grimacing female has garlands hung about
her till she resembles the tombstone of a popular
jockey.
A simple little bouquet caused a heap of mis-
chief and considerable screaming in a Melbourne
theatre between acts recently. The floral tribute
was passed up out of the O.P. box by a hireling,
and taken in charge by a decorative actor with a
fluent style of walking, who wound himself into a
beautiful bow, and passed the bunch of daisies to
Miss Sophronia Jones, at which Miss Eglantine
Smith gave a gasp that could be heard in
the next street, and signalled fiercely, claiming
possession of the goods. She was gesticu-
lating with anger, and saying things in a
shrill whisper when the curtain fell, and although
the audience howled to have the rag up again it
couldn’t be done, because Sophronia and Eglantine
were having a spirited cat-fight over the flowers.
Eglantine declared the gift offering was hers;
Sophronia denied it, and hung on. Eglantine
grabbed, and then there was a brisk interchange
of compliments and scratches. The bouquet was
ruined, and the young ladies came out of the wrangle
more or less tattered and torn, and each with her
dear little nose sadly lacerated. The people in the
orchestra stalls heard the last scream of the
baffled Eglantine. “ Yours indeed ! Yours, you
creature!” she cried. “Why, I bought them
myself! ”

N.S.W. Primate Smith notified last week
that in view of the droughty conditions he was
thinking of inviting the four principal churches to
join in a prayer for rain. That leaves close on a
dozen Christian churches, and as many more of
other denominations, out, apparently on the
ground that they have no appreciable influence
with the Almighty. Or, perhaps, they are not
toney enough to associate with. A lady Deform
politician arranging a deputation to a little Pre-
mier a short while back, limited the deputation to
“ladies of good standing.”

“THE DISTURBING ELEMENT.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rev. J. Gray wants Melbourne Presbytery to do some-
thing- final and emphatic, because Williamson’s chorus
girls collected £250 for the hospitals h.v rattling tins in
the local cricket ground, while a band played—all on the
holy “ Sawbath.” “ Such entertainments by theatricals
should be held on week days,” said the parson. “ They
are a disturbing element to our people—especially to our
Sunday schools.” Which amounts to a lamentable con-
fession that when the clergy come into competition with
the chorus, the former gets ignominiously left.
The clergyman will never cease
To pray and preach of gentleness.
He shineth as the man of peace
Whose mission ’tis to soothe and bless
The weary wanderer below.
And still the sinner’s not content,
But leaves the man of peace to go
To some “ disturbing element.”
My saintly brother, rest assured
The case has chronic grown by this,
And man will never more be cured
Of his besetting wickedness.
’Tis all in vain to murmur “ Shame ! ”
This has been man’s peculiar bent
Since first to Eden’s garden came
The same “ disturbing element.”
VIC.
S.S.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
This current year, of which four months have
passed, N. S. Waies is to have a surplus of £850,896,
according to the Estimates tabled last week. The only
unsatisfactory feature about it is that it will be a bogus
surplus. There are two reasons for saying so—(1) Money'
derived from the sale of lands is included as revenue (the
land-sales money'last year amounted to £1,029,439, or
nearly' 50 per cent, more than the entire estimated sur-
plus) ; (2) the old fraud of charging works up to Loan
account is accompanied by'the moderately'new fraud of
charging other works up to the Public Works Fund, and
paying for them out of the bogus surplus. You put your
money in your right-hand trousers pocket, and call it
revenue. But instead of paying for things out of the
right-hand pocket, you shift the money to the left-hand
pofcket and call it surplus, and pay' for the things out of
it. You might just as well pay for the things
out of the right-hand pocket at once ; it would
save wear and tear of pocket. But Australian State
finance is an enormous piece of make-believe, and the
Treasurer who can discover a new trick is pretty sure of
a good run. This was Carruthers’ trick—the Bogus Sur-
plus trick —and Treasurer Waddell took it over with the
rest of that accomplished political fakir’s stock-in-trade.
For the current year Waddell proposes to make
to the Works Fund a statutory contribution of £347,667
out of his alleged surplus, and to also pay a voluntary
£650,000 out of the same alleged surplus and the other
alleged surplus which he inherited. Together, then, he
proposes to pay, in' round figures, £1,000,000 for works.
Now, if the Works Fund were not a sham, this large pay-
ment ought to cut down the works to be paid for out of
loan money to nothing or thereabouts. As a matter
of fact, however, the Wade Government proposes
to spend no less than £2,237,000 of borrowed money
during the year. Last year the total loan ex-
penditure was £1,233,172. Therefore Wade and Waddell,
though they 7 pretend to be paying for £1,000,000 of works
out of surplus (that is, revenue), estimate that their
works will call for nearly twice as much borrowed money
as was spent last year. A Works Fund that doesn’t re-
duce borrowing is a fraud ; and the Wade-Waddell Works
Fund obviously doesn’t reduce borrowing.
No doubt the Works Fund money will be spent
on works—on repairs, and replacements, and culverts,
and buildings that bring in no revenue, and such like.
But those things ought to be paid for out of revenue ; to
abstain from paying for them out of revenue, and to
claim a surplus because they 7 haven’t been paid for in that
way, is mere trickery. And it is serious trickery 7 when
a remission of taxation is proposed on the strength of the
bogus surplus which it produces. In tins case the
Government intends to make £IOJO instead of £3OO the
minimum for income-tax assessment. This does not
mean merely that people earning up to £lOOO a year will
go free ; it means that people earning upwards of £lOOO
will only pay on their earnings in excess of £lOOO. This,
and'the abolition of stamp duties on cheques, etc., and
the reduction of railway rates, will mean “ remitting re-
venue this year to the aggregate amount of £550,000, and
next year to a much larger amount ” ; and the Fat man
gets nearly all the benefit. These things are said to be
not only possible but actually' demanded on the strength
of the surpluses ; and the surpluses are a fake, wherefore
the remissions are a fraud.
Besides pointing to the large contributions to
the bogus VVorks Fund as a justification for these remis-
sions, Waddell points also to his proposed payments in
reduction of the public debt. The fact is that the State
had a succession of deficits while it was collecting' the
very taxes which Wade and Waddell now propose to
remit, and Parliament obliges them to make small pay-
ments to reduce those deficits. And, anyhow, what a
farce it is to talk of reducing the debt when in the same
speech it is announced that a further debt of £2,237,000
is to be incurred during the year!
The most scandalous feature of Waddell’s
Budget speech, however, was his reference to the earning
capacity of the assets on which the borrowed money has
beenspent. “If,”hesaid, “they 7 capitalised their railways,
tramways, Water and Sewerage Board works. Harbor Trust
works and resumptions at 3$ per cent, on their net returns,
they found that these properties were worth £86,507,000.”
(The public debt at June 30 stood at £85,641,733.) What
sort of a Government is it that, in order to prop up a
policy of further heavy borrowing and tax-remissions,
bases its calculations upon what is admittedly the biggest
boom year on record ? Last year the railways showed a
surplus of £650,422 over working expenses and interest.
But in the 10 y r ears, 1885-1894, they 7 showed a deficit of
£1,760,516, and in the next 10 years they showed net
deficits amounting to £422,761. Up to the present time,
including the money advanced to make good worn-out
rolling stock, etc., they show a total deficit of £4,357,257.
And y 7 et, Waddell takes the unexampled surplus of a
boom year as the basis of his calculations, and dis-
regards the past deficits. Moreover, further destro.y-
ing the value of his own absurd and grossly
misleading calculations, Waddell himself mentions
that rates have been cut down, so that the re-
venue will be reduced by £312,000; so on his own
3$ per cent, basis, he ought to have wiped £8,900,000 off
the capital value. It was as flagrantly improper to base
an argument upon the 1906-7 figures alone as it would be
upon the 1902-3 figures alone. In that financial year
1902-3, a calculation made on the same basis as Waddell has
adopted, would show that the public works and other
undertakings into which loan money has been
put, were worth £43,135,800, or less than one
half the sum arrived at on the boom - year
figures'adopted by Waddell; and in the year after chat
they would have been worth £50,800,450 on the same
basis. On the strength, however, of some grotesque
figures arrived at on the boom basis, Wade and Waddell
propose to launch out on a policy 7 of furious borrowing,
and at the same time, while piling up the bill that is to be
passed on to the next generation, they propose to cut
down still further the bill which they themselves pav.

For two years previous to this last year unexplained
additions were made to the reserve fund ; in 1906 the
amount that thus dropped in without mentioning where
it came from was £2390, and in 1905 it was £4580. There
has been no such addition during the past year. When
these additions in 1905 and 1906 are taken intoaccount, the
fall in the.profits for the past year will be less noticeable.
That is to say, the ordinary insurance business has been
less unsatisfactory'in 1907, compared with what happened
in the previous two years, than the table might suggest.
The accounts are, however, a little confusing this year.
Formerly there was a reserve for unadjusted losses, main-
tained at £6OOO for some years past. This year that
reserve has been wiped out, the amount being added to
the re-insurance reserve ; and the unadjusted losses(£627o)
have been charged up direct to profit and loss. The prac-
tical effect of the change is to increase the receipts by £6OOO
and the outgoings by £6270. But the £6ooomuch more than
covered actual outstanding losses as at October 1, 1906 ;
the directors reported at that time—“ The claims out-
standing and in course of settlement do not exceed
£1200.” In addition, then, to the fire and marine losses
actually paid during the year (£46,080), the losses appear
to have been about £5070 (the difference between the
£l2OO mentioned and the present unadjusted losses).
The accounts, however, don’t disclose the actual outstand-
ing losses on September 30, 1906 ; all that can be as-
sumed from them is that the £6OOO provided to meet
such losses actually represented unadjusted losses. And
going on that basis, this comparison is arrived at :

Of recent years the co. has had a remarkably successful
career, and, though its dividends are now something
more than substantial, it deserves -success because for a
long while it kept its dividend at 6 or 8 per cent, though
that meant putting to reserve three times as much as it
distributed. Even now, though the dividend is over 20 per
cent, on the basis of the £lOO,OOO of capital, it is no more
than 9 per cent, on capital and reserves combined.
The present lay-by is, however, much less in propor-
tion to profits than the old lay-by' was. It is
quite possible, no doubt, that in the past year the co. had
particularly heavy marine losses, like most other cos. ;
on the other hand, it is equally possible that a good deal
of the gilt is off the gingerbread in the accident insurance
department. This co. got early’ to work in that very
profitable branch of the underwriting business, and culti-
vated the field very energetically when there were fewer
to reap the harvest than there are to-day.
