During the half-year this co. purchased the Poolman re-
fineries, which had been its only competitors in the
southern States ; and for, that purpose the capital is being
further increased from £2,200,000 to £2,300,000. The pre-
sent accounts do not, however, show this change in the
capital, though the profit has moved up a few thousands
by way of preparation for the increased total that the divi-
dend will call for in six months’ time. For that is the
way with this co. Within limits it can make the dis-
closed profits what it pleases—or what the directors
please. When they wanted to “ mark the jubilee ” by a
special distribution, up sprang the profits £50,000. The
fact, of course, was that the secret reserves, built up out
of profits that had been made and never mentioned, had
grown rather cumbersome, and the special jubilee bonus
was a handy way of reducing the stock of surplus coin.
With refineries, mills, rolling stock, etc., having a book
value of £2,489,743, a few odd thousands can be easily-
adjusted by rather more, or rather less, liberal
writing down than usual. Any one half-year’s
figures are, therefore, not worth much, taken
separately. Precisely . what was paid for the
Poolman properties has not been disclosed; but all that
has been added net to the book value Of all fixed invest-
ments (works, etc.) in the six months is £38,296. Either
the business is much more profitable than it was, or
the fixed investments have been written down very hard,
so that they are probably worth much more than they
stand at in the books ; which of the two it is, is not clear,
but the steady increase in profit earned per cent, of fixed
investments proves that it is one or both. For the past
six months the earnings on the basis of these invest-
ments, without deducting the half-million depreciation
fund which is a fixture, was about £7 19s. per cent, per
annum ; in 1890 it was only £6 7s. 3d. per cent. ; in 1900,
£6 12s. 2d. per cent. Since 1890, by the way, the paid
capital has been more than trebled ; and a co. that can
pay so easy a 10 per cent, on all that new capital has got
hold of something better than a copper-mine.

Assets written up £28,588 ; which amount
was transferred to capital
1907 .. 3978 4,00 272 73,218
Originally the shares were £lO paid. In 1892 the society,
having' at the time £330,000 of debts, was compelled to
suspend. As usual, the size of the smash was under-
estimated, and only £2 10's. per share was wiped off ; but
two years later a further £4 10s. per share went. At
the same time the interest on deposits was reduced from
5 per cent, to 3 per cent. Helped by that, by careful
nursing, and by the complete suspension of dividends, the
society- got into such a condition that a few months ago
it decided to have another thorough overhaul. The re-
sult is shown above. Properties were written up so as
to make the paid capital £6 instead of £3 per share ; but
£4OOO was written off for safety, and the amount was
made good again out of the year’s profits. This process
of writing-up isn’t one to be in general recommended ;
but in this case the purpose is obvious : under a bill
passed last session this society' and others in the same
position are permitted to resume ordinary building-
society business, and when deposits are being sought a
paid capital of £57,175 looks a deal better than one of
£28,588. And, anyhow, in the past 10 years £33,100 has
been written off out of profits (that is independent of
what had been written off out of capital), so that the
writing-up to the extent of £28,588 has more justification
tliaii writing-up generally has. Interest and rent, after
paying repairs and rates, last year yielded £5 14s. Bd.
percent, on the written-up value ; but repairs' cost less
than usual during the year. And where properties have
been nursed for many years the chances are that a large
proportion of them are old, and the rental return is,
therefore, misleading as a basis for estimating their
capital value. The directors “do not say that the pro-
perties could readily be sold at their (new) valuation,
but they are well worth it to the society as income-pro-
ducing assets.” The trouble, however, is that the old
deposits have to be paid off at the rate of about £3750 a
year, so there will have to be some realisation of assets,
or the profits will have to go to pay off the old depositors,
or new deposits will have to be used for the purpose ;
and it would certainly be a wrong policy to have new
deposits represented by assets that could not be sold at
their valuation. However, the society has made a good
recovery, very- largely as a result of its own patience,
and its forbearance in the matter of dividends, and it
deserves the kindly word, if that sort of word will send
business its way.

1900.. 11,544 5164 4737 9,901 1,016

1902.. 12,566 5019 6537 11,556 2,831

This co. supplies insurance mostly of the fourpence-a-
fortnight sort—very necessary insurance, but very
costly. Its tables exhaust most of the alphabet, and
cover accident and children’s assurance, medical benefits,
and undertakers’ bills. It runs an ordinary insurance
department, too ; but even the ordinary policies only
average about £49 each. Under these circumstances ex-
penses are, of course, very heavy. Lumping together all
classes of business, as the co. does in its accounts, the
expenses have borne the following proportion to total
income :
1900 £4l 0 8 per cent. I 1904 £49 16 6 per cent.
1901 48 8 3 „ I 1905 50 14 9 „
1902 52 0 5 „ I 1906 47 18 0 „
1908 52 17 5 „ | 1907 46 9 9 „
Apparently the reduction of the rate during the last few
years is due to a larger proportion of the business being
in the “ordinary insurance” branch. The “loading”
for expenses provided in this branch amounts to an
average of 23'3 per cent, of the premiums ; and it is very
doubtful whether the work can be done for the money.
If it can’t there are only two sources to draw upon : the
industrial branch may be paying some of the “ordinary ”
expenses as well as its own ; or the ordinary branch’s
own reserves may be drawn upon. Actuary E. M. Moors,
however, values the liabilities under policies, and declares
that there is a surplus. And that is quite possible,
seeing that some of the industrial branch “ loading ”
amounts to no less than 55 per cent. —that is to say, in
fixing the premiums, it is calculated that 11s. of the
money will go in expenses and 9s. in benefits. The M.L.A.
policy-holders are going to have their insurance business
done at a cost of Is. 6d. in the £, and then
share in the profits. But these poor little fourpence-a-
fortnight policy-holders are reckoned on to pay Us. in
the £ for their insurance. It is simply another illustra-
tion of the fact that the little man’s penny won’t buy the
same penn’orth as the big man’s. Also, it is another
illustration of the need to organise these things—call it
Socialism or what you like —in order to prevent some of
the present appalling waste.

This co., more often known as “Broughton’s Co.” than

of its reserves consists of a contingent fund to provide
against possible losses on the time-payment bargains.

£72 ; but £154 had to come off the contingent fund, to
provide for losses on former sales, which left the net loss
of £B2 shown above.

* * * *
No Broken Hill mine has lately come into such
strong favor as the South. It got an ore body 370 ft. wide
at its deepest level, and that and its 4s. quarterly divi-
dends dazzled the public. Unfortunately for the public
the dazzling happened when the metal markets were
booming, and when the share markets reflected the boom.
Even those who bought at the top may get their money
back, perhaps with interest; but that is poor consolation
when by holding off for a few months they could have got
exactly the same shares, carrying the same dividends, for
£3 12s. 6d. instead of the £8 3s. reached in December.
Last half-year (to June 30) the South’s profit
and loss account showed a gross profit of £104,526; but
that allows nothing- for wear and tear. The concentrates
contained 11,118 tons lead, so the gross profit equalled
£9 Bs. Od. per ton of lead. Lead averaged £l9 17s. Id. ;
therefore, costs ran to £lO 9s. Id. per ton of lead
plus all the silver. Silver averaged 2s. 9sd. per oz.
Present price is about 2s. 6d. If silver had averaged only
2s. 6d. last half-year, the profit per ton of lead would
have been reduced to £9 os. 9d., leaving the balance
(£lO 16s. 4d.) to represent the costs. In the previous
half-year the costs amounted to £9 14s. Bd. per ton of
lead, with silver at 2s. lOd. Reducing the silver to the
half-crown basis, lead could have been produced in that
half-year for £lO 3s. Id. per ton. As prosperity
increases, so do costs, which explains why big mines like
this can pull through in times of depression. Four years
ago, in the first half of 1903, this co. produced lead for
£9 3s. sd. per ton, plus the value of the silver, and silver
was only 2s. Id. per oz. then. Putting silver up to 2s. 6d.,
the costs at that time, stated per ton of lead, were only
£8 9s. 6d.—very different figures from the present
£lO 16s. 4d., calculated on the same basis. Still, the
comparison is not quite fair. Four years ago, some old
concentrates (which only cost about Is. per ton to deliver
at the mill) were re-treated, and that reduced costs.
With silver at 2s. 6d., then, costs on the most
recent figures of the B. H. South have been between
£lO 3s. Id. and £lO 16s. 4d. per ton of lead, without pro-
viding for wear and tear or any capital expenditure. The
profits for the past half-year also include about £lO,OOO
received for zinc-bearing tailings ; but those receipts are
likely to continue. The output of ore has been 4000 tons
per week ; but the new mill, which will be ready about
next April, was planned to increase the output to 6000
tons per week. (At that rate there is 10 years’ ore de-
veloped above the 970 ft. level.) The trouble is that the
increase in output will not improve the lead market, and
that is the great factor in determining the co.’s profits.
Assuming (1) an output of 6000 tons per week, (2) costs
at £lO 10s. per ton of lead (the average for the past year),
and (3) that the ore remains of the same quality as last
year, and that the same extraction is made, then these
profits would be earned in a year at various metal
prices:—

I he Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on'a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These two plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated
their worth and ability. Both Aerialways were supplied
by W. & J. Lempriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleichert & Co., of London, the largest firm in the world
for the manufacture of transport appliances!*

* * * *
One set of Improved Plunger Pumps, Cornish
type, is at present being constructed for the Berry United
Gold Mines, to the order of Messrs. Bewick, Moreing and
Company. The set will consist of 3 lifts, each of 200 ft.,
the total lift being 600 ft. The Plungers are 26in. in
diameter and 10ft. stroke, and the discharge 2,500,000
gallons per day. The valve boxes are made in separate
pieces, instead of forming part of one H piece, and the
valves are of the compound flap type. The total weight
is 110 tons. The Austral Otis Engineering Company have
this work in hand. This Company has constructed and
erected most of the big volume, deep alluvial plants now
working in Victoria.

* * * *
Now that the Junction North, Broken Hill,
has its new electric pump, work should very soon be
resumed at the 1137 ft. level. Inferentially, the mill should
shortly be working up to its full capacity. There are three
working places on the 1137 ft. where operations can be
started immediately, and if advantage is taken of these,
there is no need for the mill to be short of ore. But as to
whether the mill will be able to deal with that ore when
it comes along, opinions differ. Rhodonite, it is to be
feared, will still form a percentage of the “dirt” sent
up from below, and as this unyielding substance has up
to now broken every breakable part of the Gates
cracker used for pounding the ore before it goes to
the mill bins, it is probable that the mine’s
troubles are not all over yet. Breakages mean a prolific
source of delay in mill work, and consequent irregular
returns. Still the mill shows an improvement of late,
the extraction being consistently better than it was some
time back.
» * * *
Broken Hill British is assured of a good length
of eastern vein in Block 15, although going south from
the 800 ft. crosscut in Blackwood’s section the vein seems
pretty hard to follow in the drive. It has been carried
for a long way on the west side of the north drive which
is being put out to connect Thompson’s with Howell’s
shafts on the 800 ft. level, and though a bit irregular,
appears to carry good ore. This body will probablv per-
sist through Thompson’s section, and, the management
hopes, prove to be the source of the ore tapped by the
diamond drill at various points between Thompson’s
shaft and Marsh’s.
* * TV *
The threat of the directors of the New White
Leads, Broken Hill, to erect a concentrating plant was
not all bluff. The step was actually decided on at a meet-
ing held the other day in Melbourne. But where they
are going to get the ore to keep the mill going for any
length of time hasn’t transpired. What ore bodies have
been proved are mostly mullock, with a large percentage
of zinc and pyrites. The western crosscut recently put
in on the 400 ft. level didn’t prove anything except that the
ore there was about the same as in the east crosscut-
bunchy, with plenty of zinc. If a trench were put
through the surface dump, its average assay wouldn’t be
high enough to pay for concentrating with lead at under
£lB a ton. A more sensible move is the sinking of the
shaft. If they get better ore at the 500 ft. level it will be
plenty of time to think about a mill.
* * * *
Growl Creek-Shuttleton (N.S.W.), which was
knocked out by a demand to pay up £BOO overdrawn on
copper shipments when the market slumped, proposes to
reconstruct as the Mouramba Mines. The scheme is all
right for the men who can play the game of backup-or-
forfeit; but it is mighty awkward for weak little holders.
Of the 150,000 shares in the new co. 75,000 are offered to
present holders at 2s. ; 37,500 bonus shares will be issued
to the applicants ; and for those who can’t, as well as
those who can, put up the 25., there are 37,500 shares—
one for every four now held. But the first-mentioned
75,000 shares are preferential, and must get 2s. in divs.
before the others take a bite. Even if they have to make
some sacrifice, shareholders ought now to hang on.
In spite of the daily-paper yarns of abundance
of ore, Blayney (N.S.W.) smelters, as this paper expected,
have had to shut up for want of something to smelt.
* * * *
“A Pitt-street miner” is accused by the S. M.
Herald of making a rough calculation of Mt. David’s ore
reserves, to the detriment of the show. The tonnage
even including the ore between the winzes below the
level they are stoping, doesn’t amount to much for such
a big co., no matter who makes the estimate. Neverthe-
less, Mt. David certainly never had a better shoot
of ore than this one, and 18dw't. per ton has
left old records miles behind, with the exception
of some small crushings taken out by Crozier
the prospector. It is too optimistic, though, to
expect such a high average yield to continue, and that is
evidently the view the market takes; and the fact that
such a k.rge proportion of the stone that will be required
to supply the mill for, say, a year has yet to be developed
increases the pessimism with which investors naturally
view a mine with such a long record of calls.

Australia’s mineral wealth and production with the help

man who is worth his weight in gold. Leaving gentlemen
Of the weight in the community of G. H. Reid and Thos.
Bent out of the case, and taking instead the average
citizen of 10st. 71b., enough fine gold has been produced
t 0 'lbu an ami -7 of 52 > 000 such men ; and then there
would be enough left over to provide every private of
them with a few ounces of real gold braid.”
* * * *
Some more money has been found to work
Bayley’s, at Coolgardie (W. A.). A new co. with a nomi-
nal capital of £50,000, in 200,000 shares of ss. each, has
been registered in London ; but as the vendor (in this
case a second-hand machinery corporation) takes 80,000
as its cut, only 120,000 rank as contributing. The vendor
corporation has agreed to supply all plant'required, and
to accept payment from the proceeds of gold won, so the
whole of the money subscribed minus flotation expenses
will be available for working capital. Still the mine is
never likely to make money for shareholders, in spite of
the concession. What will probably happen is this : The
capital will be spent in getting a little payable ore, the
proceeds from same will go to the aforesaid corporation
in part, or whole, payment for plant, and then the
shutters will be put up again.
* * * *
Ivanhoe east lode (W. A.) averages 465. per ton
for 26ft. in the crosscut at the 1669 ft. level. As a re-
sult of the westerly slide, the ore has now been carried
about 100 ft. clear of the eastern or Boulder boundary
These slides occur every 150 ft. or so in depth, and an
important feature about them is that they prevent the
lode (which up to date has been the main source of ore
supplies) from going east into Boulder ground.
* * * ■*
Great Boulder No. 1 Co. (W.A.) is getting
ready to instal a mill and other plant on the St. George
leases at Magnet. In March last the ore reserves blocked
out above the 90ft. level were estimated at 28,000 tons of 40s.
grade. Since then operations have been confined to shaft-
sinking, and no more ore has been developed. As already
pointed out, the great width of the lode at 90ft. augurs
well for permanency; but until permanency has been
actually established the shares are a pure speculation.
This paper said in June last that a drop to 605.,
or thereabouts, in the grade of the ore treated at the
Associated Northern (W.A.) was inevitable before the end
of the year, and such a drop is about to take place. Later
on the grade will probably be brought down to about 505.,
and kept at that figure as long as the mine can stand it,
which, as far as can be judged at present, should be about
two years.
* * * »
Mararoa (W.A.) is still being kept well before
the public. Nearly every other day some newspaper
announcement is made that values are getting richer
in depth, or that the shares are bound to go
to a high premium before long. There is only
one way to get at the true value of this mine, and that is
to find out what grade can be kept up when the whole
plant is in operation, and what profit can be earned.
This piece of information would be worth more than all
the rest, and it is up to the management to supply it.
Westralia Mt. Morgans Co. (W.A. ) seems to
prefer darkness to light. No information is available as
to how much ore is going through the mill, what quantity
of gold is being won, or how much it costs to get the gold.
Anyone who wants to know these things must go to the
management and ask it as a favor, and the chances are
that he will not get it. As for newspaper reporters, they
are sooled off at sight. It is alleged that a monthly profit
of £3OOO or so is now being earned, but perhaps only
mining and milling costs are reckoned, the cost of the
development of the ore being ignored. This is quite a
common practice in the West.
* * * «
Ida H. (W.A.) shows improved prospects at
depth. Expenditure on new plant and development is
somewhat heavy, but, despite this burden, the co.
manages to show a monthly profit of £6OO to £9OO. The
new shoot met with some months ago at the No. 6 level
has opened up well, and preparations are now being made
to work it in deeper ground. The shares, however, still
rank as a gamble.
* • * *
Chairman Harper prepared shareholders for the
worst at the Golden Pole (W.A.) meeting. After making
the belated announcement that the north crosscourse
had shortened the shoot still further, he wound up by
stating that if values did not improve in depth (his
lugubrious air gave the impression that they wouldn’t)
the mine would be let on tribute. The meeting only
lasted a few minutes, and everyone wore a sad expression
at the finish. The co. has about £7OOO in hand, and a
tight hold will probably be kept on this sum in case a
suitable property is placed on offer.
* * * *
The Ora Banda district, near Broad Arrow
(W.A.), is again being worked. Some years ago it 'was
tried and found unprofitable, but latterly it has been,
making a bit of a name for itself as a gold-producer in
shallow ground. So far, nothing has been done in depth,
and, judging by appearances, that is where the pinch is
likely to be felt.
* * * *
The old trouble with regard to the powers of
mining wardens is again up for consideration in the West,
and it is proposed by the Government to make a warden’s
recommendation, which is now subject to veto by the
Minister, a decision equivalent to a verdict. It is in-
tended, however, to create an Appeal Board composed of,
the Minister, Under-Secretary, and a resident magistrate.
* * * *
This is the record of Barrambie Ranges (W.A.),
whose shares went to over 40s. on reports that the mine'
was as full of 3oz. dirt as a pawnshop’s window is full of
pledges : £4582 placed to credit of co. ; £12,000 raised
by sale of new and reserve shares; £4900 premium ’
obtained on reserve shares ; £12,900 gold won. Dividend,
£l3OO. Thus, after some £32,000 has gone into the mine’
shareholders have got a single tanner per share back.
There is about as much cash now in the safe as the co.
distributed in dividend. The co.’s crushing record for
six months is 2159 tons for 26240 z. The 3oz. dirt is thus
still missing. There is the same old story in the directors’
chatter about miners’ peculations. Because two men were
caught, that does not prove that the co.’s shortage in the
estimate was due to thieving. The directors should let
the past in respect to gold-stealing die ;if they don’t they
can’t expect the absurd 3oz. estimate will be forgotten.
It is whispered that the co.’s best development for the
half-year is the getting of water in the well. If the water
means extra crushing and regular divs., all right. Other-
wise the statement is an admission that mining work is
below expectations. Now the 200 ft. level has to demon-
strate that gold lives down, and that there is enough
stone in the mine to return even the present modest
market value of shares.
* * * * .
Ravensthorpe (W.A.) has been given a lift by
the local inspector of mines. He sees no justification for
the report that the leading mines have gone to pieces in
depth, and says that, as most of them are in the initial
stages of development, it is too early to give a
definite opinion as to how they are likely to turn
out in deeper ground. The Mt. Oatlin, which is the most
advanced of the lot, is, however, said to have opened up
well down to 300 ft., where an Bft. lode has been exposed
for a length of over 200 ft. The heavy fall in copper is the
reason assigned by the inspector for the heavy set-back
which the district has received, and in his opinion the
Kaufman and other crowds are doing the right thing in
curtailing operations pending railway communication
with the coast. Experiments in the concentration of
lower-grade sulphides are now being conducted by the
Kaufman people.
* * * *
J. Bull had better act cautiously with regard to
the Northampton lead mines (W.A.). Worked many
years ago—as far back as the ’so’s—these properties have
produced lead to the value of over half-a-million sterling,
but in the days when they did well, the standard of living
was poor, the rate of wages was low, and the wool ships
were glad to take the ore for “stiffening.” Things may,
it is true, be all right while the lead market is strong ;
but present prices can’t reasonably be expected to last.
And the mines can’t take advantage of the present prices
to any extent, because nearly all the mines are full of
water. J. W. Sutherland, of the Golden Horseshoe, spent
some time and money getting the water out of one pro-
perty, but after having a look below he allowed the work-
ings to flood again. This speaks for itself.
Now that the Melbourne crowd which took up
the Wheal Ellen (W.A.) has laid bare its innocence, it is
well to ask what purpose such people serve to mining.
They were a receptive lot of simpletons, who bought a
mine on a few specimens and the story of past rich-
ness. There was no prospectus, no preliminary report, no
inspection. All this is admitted, yet there is talk about
making a shilling call to raise more good monev to send
after bad by undertaking a lawsuit. What for?’CEn they
think that any jury will resist the thought that in the
hurry to get rich they neglected ordinary business pru-
dence, and so fell in ? The specimens shown told plainly
that the vendors were off the gold ; for if stone like that
was in the face, no miner would leave it to accept a
modest cash return out of the sale of the mine. The
best thing the shareholders can do is to write the loss off
with the determination to go slow when more specimens
are thrust under their nose.
* « * *
Mulrooney is a man of peace, so he did not accept
the invitation of his legal manager, Bell, and wade into
one Sharp for telling him, the other day at Ballarat, that
his sampling of the Nuggety Ajax had been lax, as well
as for inferentially suggesting worse things. As Mul-
rooney is a man of peace and a good fellow. The Bulletin
doesn’t mind also telling him that, while it cannot en-
dorse all the adverse criticism, still his management of
the mine deserved review. Mulrooney cannot be expected
to see beyond the fioint of the pick ; but the trouble is
that where the pick has been the stone has not
come up to what reports indicated. Therefore Mul-
rooney stands as a mark for those who took
shares up to 30s. and now see them at a crown.
There is no romance about the Nuggety Ajax now that
it has got down to hard battery work. Mulrooney says
that the gold did not get beyond the drives. If so, there is a
very stiff hill to climb, with a few calls by the wayside.
Ballarat meantime cannot make up its mind whether
Sharp, as a non-registered shareholder, had a right to
talk at the meeting. Legally he had no locus standi;
morally he had, and as he voiced a lot of what other
people have been thinking, he cannot be voted altogether
a public nuisance.

* * * „
A becoming air of caution pervades the remarks
of J. Pennington, manager of the Briseis Extended (Tas.),
when dealing with his somewhat dubious show. He
pledges himself to two more good runs from the present
cut in the shallow drifts, and then adds that “if
the lead continues towards the river, good re-
sults may continue for an indefinite period.”
The “if” show’s that previous limitations to this
property still exist. Pennington does not despise the
deep ground, as was the fashion the other day. He
wants facilities to handle it, and foretells twelve months’
profitable mining. There is workmanship in this state-
ment, for it looks as if the run of tin out of which the 18
tons came was the result of a back eddy close to the
bank, where a good deal of concentration had gone on.
This probably accounts for the tale told by the bores, the
details of which, by the way, are not shown in the half-
yearly reports. Co.’s financial position is—£ls3o cash :
book debts, £lO5O ; and stores and fuel worth £l6O. Not
much to come and go on with a claim like the Ex-
tended. A couple of good yields would therefore be verv
acceptable.
* * * *
Bischoff Extended, Waratah (Tas.), has had
8.,M. and Co.’s old engineer, P. Fitzgerald, inspecting.
He tots up tin ore in sight at 15,286 tons, worth some 3
per cent., which should be worth £42,500 gross. This
tonnage, he avows, will be largely increased by fossicking
in the old workings and by pushing on development work
from the bottom crosscut. Two dangers show up ahead.
One is that the old miner knew just as well as the
modern how to get tin ore out of a lode formation. The
other is that in tin mining you never know when the
ore will cut out. There is also the instability of the tin
market to be taken into account. It is in the co.’s favor
that it is strong financially, and so can put up the plant
Fitzgerald proposes. Still the mine, as it stands, is a
gamble both in the matter of its own future and on
account of the uncertain metal market.
• * * *
The first half-yearly report of the Mt. Cleveland
(Tas.) doesn’t add much to the ascertained facts. Its
supporters have put forward some very large, loose state-
ments about the property ; but that sort don’t count.
The co. is in 75,000 shares of 10s., of which 10,000 are in
reserve ; of the 65,000 issued, no less than 50,000 fully
paid went to the syndicate that sold the show to the
present co. ; so a poor little 15,000 contribs. have to
carry the whole burden of developing and equipping the
claim, in a district where costs run high. By September
30 the co. had already spent £2172, and it hasn’t even
begun to provide a mill or the trdmway that will be neces-
sary ; it hasn’t even determined on a process of the treat-
ment of its tin ore, though it has been making some
trials in Melbourne ; and how it is going to pull through,
even if it issues the reserved 10,000 shares, isn’t as yet
apparent.

Mt. Molloy (Q.) report and accounts for the
half-year to the end of June have only now reached
southern shareholders. The document will probably
perplex as much as it will help them. “Blister
copper realisations” for the half-year are said to
be £37,554. At the end of the previous half-
year “blister copper in process of realisation” was
down in the balance sheet for £16.582 in excess of drafts—
in other words, credit was taken for £16,532 as sur-
pluses expected. Whether more or less than this
£16,582 was actually received last half-year isn’t
mentioned; if more than that was actually re-
ceived, then the net profit on the past half-year’s
operations was less than the £8059 set down. On the
other hand, if less than £16,582 was actually realised on the
previous half-year’s shipments, then the past half-year’s
profit; was really more than £8059. The co., as at June
30, took credit for £8982 of copper in transit; if it didn’t
actually realise that much, then the half-year’s profit was
over-estimated. It all boils down, then, to this : that at
times when the copper market has the jumps, copper
mining cos.’ accounts are largely a matter merely of esti-
mates, so long as they take credit for unrealised stocks.
They can’t ignore those stocks altogether, either; the
‘accounts wouldn’t be true statements if they did. This
co., of course, doesn’t stand alone in the matter; it is
even said that such an old, conservative concern as Wal-
laroo and Moonta (W. A.) when it issued its last balance-
sheet put a higher value on its stocks than it would do if
it had to make up the accounts now.
* • * *
Ohillagoe is finding the job of getting the
Huntingdon-Heberlein process under way a tough one
up in remote North Queensland. The eight converters
are now preparing the ore for the blast furnace, and one
of the difficulties has been to break up sintered material,
and get it taken away. Men don’t rush such jobs, so
there is often a shortage of labor, as the railway and
cane-farms have absorbed so many hands lately. In the
long run, however, it isn’t the process so much as the
value of the ore that will decide this latest Ohillagoe
experiment, because the expense of grinding, roasting,
and converting the sulphides into a form suited to the
blast furnace must be heavy.
* * * *
North Queensland tin cos. are mostly sitting
down and thinking hard, as cos. are very likely to do that
have seen their product fall from £214 to £l4O in a few
months. The future, too, is uncertain ; the only thing
the tin-miners can find out for sure is that even £l4O is
higher than the metal ever averaged for a year before 1905 ;
and the £59 and £63 that were quoted ten years ago makes
them shudder. Several of the mines have also been
proving lately that tin lodes are as unreliable as tin
markets. The Endeavor began to have such a blue out-
look that the co. chased the Captain claim and caught it ;
it is a rich show on the Woolooman Creek. ' The
Dead Finish, a claim that the original prospectors
got out of for £6OOO at the top of the market,
is in trouble, and the co. is to be wound up—which is a
worse end than the show, an honest little one, deserved ;
so a new co. may be formed. The one concern that em-
phatically holds its end up is Gilmore’s. As for the old-
established Vulcan, the best thing that has happened
there lately is the tendency to replace good old patri-
archal ways by up-to-date methods.
* » , *. v v *
The Willcocks Cloneurry syndicate is in a bad
way, and reconstruction proposals are still being dis-
cussed. Meantime work almost everywhere has been
hung up, at. the Duck Creek smelter as well as at the
mines. The smelter, though practically ready, was never
warmed.
The survey of the route for the line to join the
Hampden and the Mt. Elliott mines with the Cloncurry
railway has been started. The line branches from the
Great Australia mine, which, by the way, has got its
smelter to work—the first on Cloncurry, if Aldis’s failure
is left out of the reckoning. At latest accounts the
Great Australia attempt was proving a thorough suocess ;
and the second furnace was being got ready. When that
is going the capacity will be 500 tons-a month.

When the Tasmanian Copper Co. suddenly
decided that it would have to th row up the sponge at the
Blinman and its other mines in northern S. Aus., the
Government agreed to protect its leases on condition that
it offered the mines on tribute. But the co., though it
agreed, is pottering about, and it doesn’t look as though
the men who want to tribute will be able to,get a look-in
before Christmas.
* * * *
An extraordinary yarn is running through the
papers about the West Arm tinfield (Northern Territory).
“ Between 3000 and 4000 men are making a good living,” it
is declared, “ and some are making more than that.” Bar-
rister Frank Lyons, who has been mixing himself up with
N.T. mining, has also been interviewed about the field, and
says “ it is the best situated tin field in either hemisphere
—water carriage right to the mines.” As a matter of
fact the population is about 150 Chinese and 50 Europeans,
and the Europeans are made to understand that
they are not wanted. It is quite true that there is
water right alongside the mines. It is just as true
that there are oceans of water in the mines, and the job
of sinking below water level (50ft. at the outside) will be
heart-breaking. No man or co. without plenty of
backing could look at the job. Wages are comparatively
poor (from 10s. to 12s. for Europeans), and the living con-
sists mainly of tinned dog and damper. It isn’t a cheer-
ful plaoe for a white working miner to turn his eyes to.


Education by Post.— Until recently young
people in the country who desired to better their "finan-
cial condition by the pursuit of knowledge have been
handicapped by the difficulty of obtaining expert instruc-
tion in general educational, business or technical
subjects. This state of things has now been remedied,
and the facilities of the greatest private educational in-
stitution in Australia may now be brought to their very
doors. Stott and Hoare’s Business College has elaborated
a system of practical and comprehensive postal instruc-
tion in Bookkeeping, Shorthand, Typewriting, Penman-
ship, Arithmetic, Correspondence, Electrical and Mechan-
ical Engineering, Architecture and Building Construction,
Mechanical and Architectural Drawing, Pen and Ink
Sketching, etc., and effective methods are employed to
prepare by post candidates for all public examinations.
The Principal will forward a beautifully printed descrip-
tive booklet, entitled “ Home Study,” on applicCfS'on to
Dept. 8., Stott and Hoare’s Business College, Moore
Street, Sydney.*

Surely a record shearing tally, that of John
Fry, at Tawalla Station (Vic.) October, 1907 —828
sheep in one week. It was worth £9 2s. 3d.
* * * w
Atherton, in the Far North of Queens-
land (where it is much too hot for white men to
work), had a £lOO wood-chopping contest the
other day. And (as it is much too hot for white
men to work up there) they chose crow’s-foot
elm for their gentle little recreation—logs, 2ft.
through, of tough, fine-grained stuff that, under
pressure, you could bend into a cart-wheel.
Mackay, of Mossman, was the winner, Mossman
being a coastal place hotter than most of the rest
of the North—the very place, in fact, where no
white man could possibly live and have any
stamina. He belted through his log in 9in. 35sec.

“ The Prooshan ” : What the servant pro-
blem is to the female employer of domestic ser-
vice as a topic of conversation, so is the virtue
of her favorite fuel to the backblox lady who toils
for herself. Mrs. Johnnycake begins with re-
marking what “ nice bright erackly wood stringy-
bark makes when real dry.” “ Lord, Mrs. J ,
surely you’d never stick a bit of that awful black-
burrtin’ rubbish in a stove,” says Mrs. Sandsoap.
“Give me appletree,” says Mrs. Tart; “it’s
lovely for the camp-oven, and the bark”
“What!” sneers a fourth, “that ’orrid, slow-
burnin’ ash-makin’ stuff ! I’d just like to catch
my ole man tryin’ to lob a load near my kitchen.”
Then all speak at once, and, above the din, one
distinguishes little tail-ends of contention like
these: “Nice dry mossy limbs,” “Light up in a
second,” “Go as black as yer hat,” and, “ Not a
bit er last in the stuff,” by which time the fire is
mostly out or nearly so. And the ladies repair to
the wood-heap to pick up a few chips to “ give it
a bit of a start again.”
* * * *
“Agricola”: Consider the contemptible-
ness of the newly-shorn ram. Before the opera-
tion he is a thing of joy and imposing symmetry.
His massive horns set off his thick-set woody
figure. He walks in seemly pride, an object for
respect, even reverence. Then of a sudden he is
seized and thrown on a board. He is pushed,
pulled, mauled, and smacked on the nose with the
shears because he won’t lie still. And, as a final
degradation, his outrager urges him from the
tally-pen with the business end of a large blucher.
And how changed a ram he is now. The lordly
head and massive horns are still there, but they
are attached to a body which looks like that of a
mere goat. It is made plain that the blocky
figure was a sham, being only woollen padding
after all—a thing the dejected creature had for-
gotten during the year which elapsed since he
saw himself last. If there are any other rams,
he seeks their sympathy and companionship.
Naked and ashamed, they abjure the society of
the gentler sex, and withdraw into the remote
corners of the paddock, until their need for fig-
leaves shall become less apparent. Hogan, J.P.,
consuming the bitter news of his shire council de-
feat, is a circumstance of self satisfaction beside
the same Hogan’s just-shorn ram.

Three teams lately sold to mining cos. at
Cloncurry (Q.) realised £450, £525 and £550 re-
spectively. Evidently a Cloncurry team is fre-

“X.P.D.”: Here in Chillagoe (N.Q.),
where half the cooks are yellow, the boarders at
pubs and accommodation houses have never made
a real effort to oust them. The white suits which
the co.’s officers (i.e., persons who work in an
office) wear, are mostly “ done-up” at the Japan-
ese laundries, where much of the work of those
whose wages are not salaries is also done. On the
other hand, the copper-mining township of O.K.
dealt suddenly with the alien question a few
weeks back, simply by a notification from the
boarders at the various pubs that white cooks
would have to be employed. But the O.K. crowd
are mostly miners, while Chillagoe’s population
consists of all sorts. One instance of insult to a
white waitress occurred at 0.K., but men were
present, and the Monkey still feels where the boot
was placed.

“Jimmie Pannikin”: “G.S.K.” writes
re various modes of travelling in whathelibel-
lously labels the ultra-Sheol country. The
multiplicity of methods of washing one’s
body in tiie Outer Land is also worthy of
record. Have swum in the Darling and its
anabranches, winter and summer. Had a
bath with a bucket at Gibberburra. Did a
drybiow many a time during the liver-rousing
shanghai trips. Tubbed with a sponge and
jugful of tank water. Revelled restrictedly
in a dish of discolored water. The mouth-
spray makes a good substitute for the
satisfying and sustaining shower in the
drought time. Once the District Works
Officer and I barracked for a bath at Fowler’s
Gap, but water was terrible scarce, so we
went down to the “ crik ”at daylight. Atfer
quite an exploring feat, we found a pool
fully 2ft. deep. I stood in the muddy middle
while the D.W.O. poured bucket after
bucket of yellow water over expansive me.
Then I returned the compliment. “Well,
it was worth the walk and the search,” said
the D.W.O. ; “it would have been just the
very thing the doctor ordered, but for the
queer smell of the water.” As we scrambled
up the bank we met a drove of pigs, making
for our polluted pool, the only open water
within miles of the pub. They wallowed
and swallowed and played water-polo for
quite half an hour before they were satisfied.
Then we could account for the perfume.
* * * *
“Aimee”: The soldier bird is the
finest actor in the bush. The manner in which
he leads you from his home and family is a
piece of artistic deception. Flitting from tree
to tree, each one a little further from his
address, he will utter the most heart-rending
cries. I have seen a Chinaman watching his
uninsured premises and stock going heaven-
wards in smoke, and the soldier bird gives a
first-class reproduction of that Chow. Approach
his domicile, and he maintains a discreet silence
and assumes an air of careless indifference, which
is merely bluff.
“Lancelot” (and about 15 others) : The
insect that “Elmore” in B. 24/10/’O7 does not
know the name of is the caterpillar of the cup or
slug moth (doratifera vulncrans). It receives
its name “ cup moth ” from the cup-like shape of
its cocoon—made of a bark-parchment-looking
substance found on twigs of saplings, etc. The
spines that were the cause of all the bad language
are hollow and poisonous—the stings causing
swellings.

“Hamer”: “You might not think it,
lookin’ at my clothes,” he remarked, “ but I’m
not used to city life. I haven’t been near a big
town for 30 years.” He also confided that he
didn’t want to smoke, but he had got into a
smoking compartment because the others were
full of : women. He reluctantly produced a pipe,
and started to smoke. When we threw our
smokes away he gazed at us with admiration, but
continued to smoke. He said he noticed we didn’t
smoke between stations, but he wasn’t going to
run the risk of a fine himself. One of us asked
sleepily what he thought he would be fined for,
and he winked sagaciously. We were finally dis-
turbed by the noise he made as he departed.
“I’ve had enough,” he said. “It’s a bit’ot to
expect a man to smoke all the way.”

IN THE NORTH.—AN UNPLEASANT PROSPECT.
“Agricola”: Agree with “ Namoi ” (B.
24/10/’O7) that the bush girl in Cow-infested dis-
tricts wears bluchers. Would he have her wade
through the slush of a cow-yard in dancing shoes ?
Likewise she wears a man’s old felt hat in the
yard pretty often. But “Namoi” omits to mention
the fact that the bush girl sheds her bluchers and
old felt hat after business, and sets-to to make her-
self, from footgear to headpiece, as attractive as
any city maiden —and mostly succeeds. Again, the
bush girl undoubtedly swears—that is, one breed
of her does. This is the breed that corresponds
to Liz from Woolloomooloo. But the ordinary
bush girl swears as little as the ordinary anywhere
girl. “ Namoi’s ” trouble is a tendency to generali-
sation, and he who thus generalises is blind to the
merits of the thing which is, and horse sense is
absent from him.
* * * *
“Quart”: Is the raising of a drought-
proof breed of sheep possible? Idea is suggested
by the fact that a stud ram, lost about eighteen
months ago, in the “desert” country near
Jericho (W. Queensland), was found t’other day
fit and well. Beyond the fact that he sported a
2-year-old woollen overcoat, and wasn’t offensively
“fat,” the salvaged one was the same old ram as
of yore.
* * * *
“Squeaker” : Notice some of your con-
tributors have been making a fuss about a few
sheep being left in a yard and going without a
drink until they were let out. Those sheep went
without a drink simply because they couldn’t get
one. Same as I and a lot more have to do. Now,
I know a drover who brought 3000 fat sheep, in
the wool, from the nor’-west of W.A. to the
goldfields, and they never tasted water from the
Gascoyne to Leonora, although they passed
plenty on the road. They wouldn’t drink. Why?
Because they could get plenty of “junga.” In
anything like a good season there is abundance of
this herbage growing in the country north of
Kookynie. Also here are two more sheep yarns.
(1) On a certain station known to me, a ewe, in
lamb, was left in a dry paddock for three years.
At the end of that time, a station hand discovered
a mob of 21, being the old ewe, her progeny, and
the progeny of her offspring, all very much alive.
(2) During the big Queensland drought, a small
mob of sheep were left in a very dry and bare
paddock for two years. At the end of that time
they were found in the corner nearest to where
the water used to be, all very much dead. They
had been dead about a year and 51 weeks. There-
fore my theory is that the sheep goes on no fixed
rule; that it lives without water out of cussed-
ness, and that it can do without it as long as its
cussedness lasts; that it dies for want of water
out of cussedness ; and that its whole life is regu-
lated by cussedness and nothing else.
* * *
“Australian” (Casino, N.S.W.): A timber-
getter hereabouts has a cute method of avoiding
waste of time and labor on trees which, when felled,
turn out to be “ short.” He owns a (more or less)
tame gohanna. When a likely tree is encountered,
the ’go is shooed up with a string measure lashed
to its tail. The distance from the first limb to
the ground is noted by the saurian’s master, and,
the go. having been hauled back, the axe expert
either falls to or passes on, just as the gohanna
says.
“Paul Cupid”: To “ Gip’s ” query (B.
17/10/’O7), “Should we eat pig?” I reply, cer-
tainly. The edible value of an animal mustn’t be
gauged by the food it eats. The barnyard chook
gobbles filth with gusto; and as for the duck, we 11...
The most delicate fish are generally the dirtiest
feeders. Consider the habits of the gentle harbor
black bream. The lobster’s favorite dish is corpse
—fresh or otherwise. There’s only one doctrine
. for the person who dogmatises against eating
unclean feeders—and that’s vegetarianism.
“Punter”: Am convinced that the quarter-
deckhabit, like Freetradeand all other notable fool-
ishnesses, becomes strengthened and aggravated
in extreme eld. Near me there lives in St. Kilda
(Vic.) an old retired sea-captain, who has in his
ashphalted back-yard a revolving clothes-dryer
fashioned in imitation of a full-rigged barque or
brigantine. When washing-day comes round, the
strenuous life is lived to the uttermost on the aged
cap.’s demesne. He has shirts on the mizzen,
socks on the spinnaker boom, unmentionable
female contraptions which produce balloon-jib
effects to windward of the port booby-hatch, and
sundry other soft goods depending from the fore
and main masts respectively. Bach morning
(washing day or no) he rises sharp at the stroke of
four bells (6 a.m.), and directs a brace of subordi-
nates in the work of swabbing down the hurricane
deck — viz., the back-yard aforesaid. The while
these operations are in progress he paces the
bridge {viz., the back verandah) Jawbone-Neild
like, with a massive telescope beneath his arm, and
directs how the yard is to be steered. As a study,
he’s not too bad ; on the other hand, as neighbor
to a habitual late-riser, he’s a nuisance.
* * * *
“ Yarrum ” : Supposed by some that
snakes suck eggs, but they do nothing of the
kind. A snake couldn’t break an egg if he tried,
and, anyhow, he don’t “’anker after ’em.” It's
the chickens he wants, and he scoffs them as soon
as they come out. A snake will hover about a
setting hen from the time the eggs are put down
until the chicks come out, when he takes charge.
Mrs. Rooster is charmed, and that accounts for
her never leaving the nest in quest of tucker. If
the hen pecks at the snake —they are mostly peck-
ing at something—the snake strikes, and then
there are no chickens. Lately a Kiama man,
next door to me, found a dead hen on a setting of
eggs. He got another hen, and next morning she
was cold poultry. A third one was laid out in the
same way. Then he sat over those eggs for three
whole nights with a light, which they say will
attract a snake, and a big stick, but the snake
didn’t arrive. The comb of a snake-bitten fowl
goes black as ink.
* * * *
“ Spaceband ” : Your correspondent
“ Hamer” (B. 3/10/’O7) states that the champions
of Maori nobility come from the Poverty Bay
district, from which I gather that “Hamer” has
not rambled round Temuka, Kaiapoi, Puketeraki,
or Akaroa, in the South Island. Had he done so
he would have known that the South Island
Maori is a better worker than his northern
brother. Those around Temuka and Kaiapoi are
really hard grafters. I have seen them all, and
after living among the Hawkes Bay tribes give the
latter the palm for being the dirtiest and laziest in
the Dominion, even though they have produced a
Maoriland golf champion.

“ Kink ” goes to town :
There are swifter changes in the country byways of
trade than in the city’s. I have just revisited Melbourne
after al5 years’ absence. Round the suburbs where I
used to roam in the boom time the same businesses are
still eking out an existence. The little shops where I
bought my peppermints, my cigarettes, and my boot-
protectors are still open, and still needing a coat of paint.
I see the largest changes at my erstwhile fruit mer-
chants’; the stands are now larger, brighter, and, alas!
run by Dagoes from Athens and vicinity. My old washer-
lady :has disappeared, and Sam Buck irons collars and
cuffs for thg Richmond and Oollingwood democracy.
One thing I do like about Melbourne, and that is the
sleekness of the horses. The city horse-owner is more
merciful to his beast than Cocky and his crew. The city
horse-owner hasn’t got horse-feed to sell—hence he
doesn’t stint his steed, like the hungry grower who has
got it to sell. The city lass fills my eye, too, though pale
of face and skimpy shankwards. Though poverty stares,
and anaemia is a guest, the Melbourne lass is as
jaunty as a godfather gohanna, and jauntiness covers a
multitude of shortcomings in the eye of the drought-
stricken trader of Outback.
THE ROAD INTO THE BUSH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beyond the confines of the town, the place of many
changes,
There is a road for you and me, to lead us through the
ranges;
It is the Road into the Bush—the lone, the great, the
splendid,
Wherein there is a home for us when all the turmoil’s
ended.
I’ve traversed it in times of sleep, through deserts bare
and blinding,
Or followed it by crag and steep, about the foothills
winding;
Fenced either side, or through the trees a path that
someone blazes —
A slender thread to lead you through the depths of forest
mazes.
And so it leads you, over hills, and round the rifts and
ridges ;
Through valleys cool with mountain streams, across the
batiks and bridges ;
Until you reach the furthest farms and station-steads are
rarer ;
Until, in unfrequented tracks, you meet no fellow farer.
Then all around you lies the Bush —the Bush of countless
ages ;
The Bush unmarred by human hands, nor hemmed and
shut in cages ;
The silent slopes of many hills ; the cataract that dances ;
The gullies bright with flower and fern, the weird and
vast expanses.
You’ll hear the old bush sounds once more, and tread the
ancient covers ;
You’ll hear the quaint, familiar calls, the notes of swans
and plovers,
You’ll see the movement in the fern where some marauder
passes,
And hear again the long-drawn sigh of wind in tall plain-
grasses.
I know a road outside the town that ever draws my
fancies —
It speaks to me of further lands, it tells of great
expanses;
It is the Road into the Bush ; and, after many changes,
I’ll travel by it once again to lands beyond the ranges.
VIC.
O. G. A. COLLER.

“Batcher”: To “Barb,” “Camp-Oven,”
and others. The hall-mark of the bushie is his
unkempt wire-whiskered beard. (Approximate
length, 2ft.) In it you will find entangled a with-
ered gum-leaf or two and a few burrs. If the
above conditions are fulfilled, and a doubt still
lingers, reverse the way back suspect (taking care
not to be maimed by his spurs), lift up his coat-
tails, and behold the classic beauty of your counte-
nance in the sheen of his pants.
“Snell ” : My inquiry re the most marked
characteristics of bushie in town does not produce
proofs that the “ Aboriginality ” experts are close
watchers and trackers of Bill and Jim. After
close and patient observation of bushie far from his
native wilds, I have come to the conclusion that
he is to be identified in town by his marked de-
votion to his hat. There are other pronounced
peculiarities that help to distinguish Bill and Jim
in Bourke-street—their quaint passion for having
their boots cleaned, for instance, also their tena-
acious grip on the hotel in which they reside
(when James and William are not leaning in
clumps hard up against the door jambs, the front
windows or the verandah posts of their hotel,
they are busily engaged having their boots cleaned
by a public practitioner). But the one unfailing
symptom that distinguishes Jimbill from the town
dweller is his painful concern about his old brown
hat. As a rule it is not much of a hat; at any
rate is not the kind of hat the average citizen of
no mean country would want to steal; but
Billjim can’t be convinced on that point,
and the precautions he takes for the preserva-
tion of his tile spoil his holiday. At restaurants
he will never trust his hat to the common rack or
any convenient hook, but carries it to the table
with him. Very often, when his concern is most
acute, he will not take the hat off at all; if he does,
he deposits it where he can keep a foot on it, and
he pauses between bites to dive down and satisfy
himself that no flash thief has stolen in and
pinched it. At the theatre his concern about his
“old brown” is even more agonised. Even if
crowded out of popular parts he carries his hat
into the circle with him, and wears it with an
ostentatious air of being too cute for these
miserable town spielers, till the curtain goes up.
Then he hangs on to his headgear all through the
act, losing the thread of the play by reason of his
nervous dread that the solid gentleman on his
right is hatching plots to abduct his hat. When
the lights come up again, Billjim wedges his
battered felt on his head, and breathes freely.
This process is repeated through the play. At
Melbourne Majesty’s one night last Show week a
Jimbill near me was politely requested to remove
his hat. He regarded the usher with startled
suspicion for half a minute, then took off his tile,
rolled it up, and stuffed it under his vest. The
usher withdrew foiled. I am satisfied that Jim-
bill’s horror of losing his hat is akin to the terror
of losing his trousers that plagues the dreams of
the respectable churchman.
* * * *
“Brad”: I have, or rather had, until
just recently a pet “triantelope,” which spun its
web in a corner of my window. This spider was an
expert at catching flies, but preferred the juice of
the succulent “ blowie ” to that of the common
house-fly. One day a bee had become entangled
in his net, and to see the Squires-like rush of that
spider was a revelation. They clinched, the
“ triantelope” grabbing the bee round the waist;
but the honey-gatherer managed to free himself,
and his footwork was simply brilliant. Then,
turning suddenly on his adversary, he struck him
heavily in the juice-basket with his sting. The
“tri” dropped to the floor of the hut, and I
counted him out—for ever.
DEATH BEFORE DISHONOR.
MINOGUE : “Have yez fired the shot yet, Patsy?”
PATSY : “Sure, haul me up loike lightnin’.”
MINOGUE: “Oi will. Besides, the missus is
waiting for yez.”
PATSY: “Good-bye, Minogue, Oim going back.”
Periwinkle” : I’ve known a few snake-
charmers in my time—Squires, Davis and others ;
and these men were not the fools the public took
them to be—in public! When the charmers go
forth to bag their snakes, they also take an old
woollen stocking, or a bit of blanket. Locating
the snake is an acquired knack. A mouth-organ
is produced, and a few dreamy staves are softly
breathed over. Snake feels less anxious to find
his hole. Then the charmer creeps on his prey,
and fixes a forked stick over the wriggler’s neck,
lhe cessation of the music and the fixture at the
neck are calculated to make any self-respecting
snake “snakey.” Then the “rag” is produced,
and, as the snake strikes at it, the fangs get
caught. A smart, upward tug, and the fangs
lemain in the soft material. Then the snake is
transferred to the bag. In public, the snake
handled keeps darting at the crowd and
charmer. Taunted, it fastens its fangless mouth
to the charmer’s hand, arm, or what-not. Then
thrill fills the crowd, especially if the charmer
is a good actor, as he generally is. The so-called
snake-bite antidotes are part of the game.

“Micawber” : I enter the foreman of a
suburban boot factory as a candidate for the Mean
Man Stakes. He never brings any dinner, but
walks around the floor during the luncheon hour,
seeking all lie may devour. His method is some-
thing like this : “ By cripes, Jimmy, eatiri’ agen ?
No wonder you’ve got indigestion. What is it to-
day ? Egg sandwiches! My favorite, too. Told
th’ missus t’ put some in me bag, but she muster
forgot. Stuck in corned-horse sandwiches, an’ I
can’t stand ’em on Mondays.” At the fifth stop
he is replete, and on the modest outlay of nix
precisely.

“Phil Random”: He holding a snake
spellbound with a hat—or placing a spinning bike-
wheel in front of the serpent to
arrest his attention while you search
for a stick. I was crossing the Gun-
ninbill Plain once accompanied by a
mate, and carrying a respectable,
stout female called Matilda. We
saw a tiger-snake on the side of the
track, and my mate wanted to kill
it. As there wasn’t a stick within
miles, I suggested leaving it to enjoy
its sun bath in peace. “ Strike me
fat!” said my mate, “d’yon think
I’m goin’ ter let that reptile go ? ”
I kept on walking, but my chum
dropped his bundle, and, picking up
a hard clod from a wheel-rut, threw
it at the snake. That snake stood
on the tip of its dignity for some-
thing less than a second, and then
did a rapid serpentine sprint in the
direction of the clod-heaver. The
heaver passed me at the rate of GO
minutes per hour, or thereabouts.
The snake pulled up in the vicinity
of my mate’s swag, which it eyed
with unrelenting scrutiny, whilst w.e
held a consultation some consider-
able distance away. After holding
possession of the wardrobe for about
40 minutes, the long one glided off
leisurely in the direction of China.
It bit the swag several times ere it
took its departure. Matilda became
very much swollen, and burst all
her strappings, and had to undergo
a surgical operation and be recon-
structed ere we again pursued our
journey. Yes —snakes were very bad
that year.
“F.H.R.” : “ Broadaxe ” (B. 31/10/’O7)
is wrong when he says it is only a fad of engineers
to favor backed railway sleepers. We handled
half-a-million sleepers on the N. S. Wales rail-
ways, and the sleeper that is cut with the grain is
useless for railway purposes. When boring a
sleeper for fastening, a three-quarter bit is used,
and a seven-eighth dog is driven into this hole.
The idea, of course, is to have everything tight,
and a free sleeper, even when cut on the “cross,”
will often split, and once a sleeper splits
towards the ends it is valueless. Another thing.
A sawn sleeper absorbs water much more quickly
than the polished axe-cut article. Now the more
water a sleeper absorbs the quicker it will rot.
* * * *
“ Nulla” : “There are no woodpeckers in
Australia. The nearest approach is the tree-
creeper, and as for calling that bird’s shrill, piping
notes musical—well, I must have an unmusical
ear,” writes “Chink” (B. 10/10/’O7). Well, I
enthusiastically agree with “Chink’s” estimate
of his own ear, and more so. There are two
varieties of tree-creepers common to the hilly
country of N.S.W. In the beginning they were
possibly woodpeckers, but our ironbark and box
discouraged them in their pecking, and they build
their nests now under loose strips of bark. There
is one, of a rich brown color, prettily marked with
buff stripes; I have often watched him pros-
pecting his way up the trunks of our bush giants,
and the peal of silvery, bell-like notes he lets
loose as he comes to a new claim is one of the most
joyous sounds of the wilderness.
“P.D.” : I was one of an interested crowd
that watched a butcher collect a very doubtful
debt. It was on the (1 lad Stone-Rock hain pton
railway. The publican was throw-
ing us out one Sunday night about 11.30, when
the man that ate the beef he didn’t pay for came
along. He was on his way to the main camp to
collect the value of a couple of months’ mullock-
shifting, and then do a slope. The butcher inter-
cepted him with a demand for money or an order,
to which he replied that he would attend the
butcher s funeral first. Then a Homeric scrap
began. The butcher was on top one time, the bad
mark another. However, after they had fought
right across the road to the butcher’s shop, which
was opposite the pub., the man of beef prevailed.
He dragged his bad debt inside the shop,
locked the door, wrote out an order authorising
the contractor to pay, etc. ; and with the meat-
chopper in his hand he gave his bad debt the
option of signing it or being slain, and the evi-
dence of the crime disguised as sausages. He
looked drunk enough for any crime, and the debtor
signed the order with horror on his bad coun-
tenance.

Dear Bulletin, —Have been horror-
stricken at the debauchery of bees among
the willow trees. Young and old become
incapable of flying’ a chalk line or saying
buzz ’ with a clear enunciation, owing to
the intoxicating effects of the young sap. By
the way, why do cats bring rats and birds to
let their owners see them before they will eat
them ? Is it to have a blessing asked over them ?
Think it probable myself. There always appears
to me to be something Pecksnitfian about the airs
and graces of pussy. Or do they bring them to
have a percentage taken by way of rent for their
house accommodation ? And, talking about blow-
flies, is there a better way of killing them than
chasing them in a motor-car and breaking their
knees with a stockwhip ?—Yours etc., Kati-Kati.

. il •' Why can’t we get up an associa-
tion for the cultivation and preservation of the
Australian flowering and other plants ? Here in
the Hills, 20 miles from Adelaide, are orchids,
daisies, currant bushes (why didn’t some patriot
preserve us the native names of them ?)
gems of delight that would ornament any Euro-
pean hothouse—things I call honey-myrtles, mar-
tialettes, Dolly-Vardens, snowdrifts, May’s-eyes,
“ so-long-Marys,” and heaps of others! And
what a garden they make—until the Cow-man
comes along a,nd gets to them with his axe and
fire. Damn him 1 And never a one to preserve
small, even small, areas of this Eden for the chil-
dren of the next generation to know the real Aus-
tralian flora ! I asked a little girl on the roadside
what she called a lovely, five-petalled beauty that
looked like a diminutive apple tree in full bloom,
ohe said, “-Dunno 1 One of them kinds of ti-tree,
I think.” Then she went to help dad burn off.
We import “Johnson’s Curse” and stink-weed,
and other unmentionable things, and burn the
flowers of pre-Adamite Heaven.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
Protection : Yes, and when you’re forming your

protect Merely Angelina from herself. The innumerable
Merely Angelinas who “ engage ” and aren’t heard from
afterwards need to be spoken to just as severely as the
marms do. The Merely Angelina sisterhood is spoiling
many a good and once-sympathetic marm that way....

educated at Oxford. It is a great place for turning out
people with a comprehensive knowledge of the
conditions existing in Oxford and environs and an
all-embracing ignorance of those obtaining elsewhere....

“ Goril ” : A friend of mine residing up
the North Shore line had two tame kookaburras
on which he rather prided himself, he having
trained them to act as scavengers in keeping the
garden clear of grubs. But some wild koos in
the neighborhood seemed to resent the indignity
cast upon the honor of the koo family by this
servitude, and tried to induce the scavengers to
quit their tame ways and live the lives of self-
respecting koos. But all to no purpose. One
morning the lady of the house was astonished to
observe two of the wild koos range up one on
either side of one of the scavengers, and, by
manipulating their wings under his in some
peculiar manner, raise him bodily from the ground.
The lady succeeded in shooing off the would-be
kidnappers for the time being, but since then both
the scavengers have disappeared mysteriously,
and the sarcastic note in the laughter of those
wild koos when they now approach that garden
makes my friend regret that they are protected.
“ Hamer ”: An ability to swear is often an
inconvenience. It brings bad luck. The best
swearer I know is always meeting with accidents.
He is a carter as well as a curser. I meet this
accomplished linguist often, and almost every
time he is the centre of an admiring crowd
listening to his views on his latest disaster. He
has either run into something, or something has
run into him. There is such a fascination about
his flow of speech that I would be tempted to
drive a vehicle—if I had one—headlong into him,
just to hear what he has to say about it. But so
far this hasn’t been necessary. Someone else has
always done it. His grandest effort was when
his hind wheel came off, and let his load of wool
down softly on the tramline. Then the way he
leaped on his hat and objurgated was good to note.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Lily Fury was the name of a witness in a
Melbourne City Court the other day.
Ex-Labor leader Watson’s dissipation is
Bridge. IN ew Labor-leader Fisher’s is chess.
Monday, Nov. 4th, Julian E. Salomons,
K.C., and father of the N. S. Wales Bar, entered
his 72nd year; and on Tuesday (Guy Fawkes’
Day) N.S.W. Governor Rawson passed into his
64th.
When Dan O’Connor called on the Ameri-
can lions, he talked for an hour and a half to
President Roosevelt, and then Dan was put off on
to the head of Teddy’s Cabinet, and talked to him
for three hours. So the modern Daniel overcomes
lions—with Samson’s weapon.
William Sinclair, of Tyringham, N.S.W.,
(son by a former marriage of just-deceased Eliza-
beth Ologher), is the sort of citizen Australia has
need of. He is the father of 24 children, and has
adopted two others—the orphans of old mates.
All the pupils of the Tyringham school come
from his house.
Dan O’Connor, in an interview with
Sydney Telegraph, blackguards Coghlan and the
N.S.W. Agency-General vigorously. Which,
taken at its true value, is a high compliment to
Tim Coghlan. We all like the “genial Dan,”
but somehow would rather suspect the industry
of a public official who won his favor.
It was announced in London Times the
other day that a wedding was shortly to take
place between Alfred Dobson, Agent-General for
Tasmania, and Mary Alice, eldest surviving
daughter of the late General J. T. Walker, some-
time Surveyor-General of India. The bridegroom-
to-be will be 60 next August. He is Senator
Dobson’s little brother.
Rev. D. Telfer, of the Eaglehawk Pres-
byterian Church (Vic.), who received his last call
at Melbourne on Oct. 25, aged 49, was one of the
few parsons who bothered to think on social and
economic questions. He often used to make his
somewhat fashionable congregations have cold
shivers down their spines. He was a preacher of
Christianity, and not churchianity.
The Father of the Queensland House of
Awful Fossils, Johann Christian Heussler, met
Time out for business last week. Heussler hailed
from Germany, came to Australia as a young
man with shattered health, and his age of 87 is a
tribute to the climate of Brisbane, where he spent
over 50 years. Though all that spell in the
country, he could not master the English language
to the extent of intelligibility. Heussler did
good work for Ban an aland—though he was a hide-
bound Tory—in the earlier times, by inducing
many of his countrymen to become settlers in the
Commonwealth.
Sydney Telegraph the other day reported
the death, at an advanced age, of Miss Matilda
Jane Lowe, “daughter of the late Robert Lowe,
afterwards Lord Sherbrooke.” It was
news to a great many, especially the
Lowes of Mudgee, who were located
there years ago. Robert Lowe, of
Mudgee, was an enterprising and
successful grazier. He, in 1875, received
a gold medal from the Government for
the courage he displayed in shooting
the bushranger Heather, in a hand-to-
hand encounter, in 1863, near Slapdash.
“ Sherbrooke ” Lowe had no children by
»- either of his two marriages.
Mrs. Sarah Hudspeth, who died in
Bathurst last week, lived in the reign of five
British Sovereigns—George 111. and IV., William
IV.. Victoria, and Edward VII. She had been in
N.S.W. over half-a-century, and in Bathurst for
45 years. By this last she earned her rest.
A temperance enthusiast with the highly
appropriate name of Fountain has just died in
Adelaide at the respectable age of 92 years, built
up on a foundation of cold water and black-
smithing. He prided himself on having acquired
22 grandchildren and 27 “ great” ditto, none of
whom had any use for beer.
Died, at Warrnambool (Vic.), Louis
Bayer, aged 50. All over the Western dis-
trict his name was a household word. Bayer was
a German by birth, but came to this country when
only a lad. For a while he roughed it in the
Western district, and then he took up violin-
teaching and the writing and production of opera.
He claimed to have been the first to pay Ada
Crossley a fee.
There died recently, near Warrnambool
(Vic.), Mrs. William Coughlan, at the alleged age
of 107 years, 55 of which she had spent in the
Western District of Victoria. She and her hus-
band arrived in Sydney in 1838 from Ireland (these
centenarians all come from Ireland). The hus-
band died at the alleged age of 100 years, and Mrs.
Coughlan’s brother, Mr. Walsh, of Purnim, was
said to be 102 years old at his death.
Andy Fisher, the successor to Watson as
the pilot of the Federal Labor party, is not
aggressive until aroused, and then he strikes hard.
He, naturally affable, will go out of his way to
quell a fire—-and in this closely approximates
Watson. His sympathies are national. He is
not petty nor provincial. His two experiences of
office were short-lived—Secretary for Railways in
the week-old Dawson Ministry of Queensland,
and a member of the Watson Government.
Fisher began to earn his living a,t the age of 10,
and for years delved in the Gympie mines.
Died, at Macquarie, near Windsor
(N.S.W.), at the age of 83 years, one of the oldest
settlers on the Hawkesbury, Mr. Thomas
Arndell. The original Thomas Arndell came out
with Governor Phillip as assistant surgeon. He
was the medical officer on board The Friendship.
In 1790-2 he was in charge of the Parramatta
Hospital.
The Yarra Yarra station, the property of
the McLaurin brothers, in Southern Riverina
(N.S.W.), was originally taken up and stocked by
r ather Therry, of Sydney, who at that time was
head of the R. C. Church in Australia. He was
an excellent judge of cattle and his brand IJT
generally topped the market. He sold Yarra
Yarra for £20,000, most of which he devoted to
the building of St. Mary’s Cathedral.
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” supplies an epitaph :
As an Outback ex-exile, I desire to refer to the splendid
work done by the late and truly-lamented Dr. W. C.
Speece, of Coramba, in the interests of the West-o’-
Darling settlers. The dead man was particularly sympa-
thetic to the sufferings of the settlers’ children. Some-
times his anger interfered with the exact accuracy of his
press statements ; but only the cold-blooded, callous ice-
bergs are never white-angry-hot. Certainly the doctor’s
heart was always right. Occasionally he lacked patience ;
but, as he himself used to say with a semi-cynical, wholly -
humorous smile, “ Patience is often merely a form of
anaemia or utter indifference.” Those who feel warmly
often speak warmly. Absolute accuracy is a fine thing in
mere mathematics ; but sincere sympathy is a finer thing
for suffering humanity. Many an outbacker will mourn
“Medi-cuss.”
The death last week, at Melbourne, of
Hubert de Castella, aged 83, recalls the initial
sprouting of the Australian vine. In 1811, John
Macarthur went off to England to make an ex-
planation concerning the way Governor Bligh
lost his job. Macarthur was away six years
or so, and, in the interval, put his son to “fag-
up” things at a school at Vevey (Switzerland),
and have a look at the vineyards and the yodels and
other local products. When that son came home
he planted the first Australian vineyard at Camden
(N.S. W.) —from which William Ryrie got cuttings
and planted them out on his Yering (Vic.) cattle
station. In 1849, Paul de Castella came to Victoria
from Switzerland, and bought the Yering station
(now Lilydale), and kept the vine flourishing.
Hubert arrived in 1854 ; kept the eye of a prac-
tical vigneron around for a while, and then took
some samples of Yering wine to Switzerland. The
Swiss folk said the refreshment was good, and
Hubert came back about 1864. He bought 3000
acres near Yering, and founded the well-known
St Hubert’s vineyard. De Castella married, in
1865, a daughter of old-time N.S.W. Legislative
Councillor Robert Pitt Jenkins, and N.S.W.
viticultural expert, Francois de C., is a son of the
marriage.
A flourishing man in South Africa these
days is Mr. C. Knox, who makes good his claim
to have done work at the Eureka Stockade. He
went to S’Africa over a Quarter of a century ago.
and was successful
both as storekeeper
and miner. Now he
has built for himself
a beautiful home in
an ideal spot at the
foot of the Ingeli
Mountains in south-
ern Natal, where he
showers hospitality
on all who hail from
Australia.

About some religious, or semi-religious
folk. Bishop Stone-Wigg, of Papua, resting in
Queensland with his bride, is announced to be “a

of Rockhampton, is voyaging to England for
rest and a holiday. Archbishop Carr intends
visiting Rome when the bother about Roman
Catholic State school teachers is satisfactorily
settled. Mr. Judkins will tour Fiji and Maori-
land in search of health and heart, his admirers
having presented him with £l2O for that purpose.
Laboring in the Vineyard is evidently a strenuous
and health-attacking occupation.
Australia’s Postmaster-General Mauger
bates two things like poison : (1) to have to carry
Ikey’s disguised betting-price list through the
post; (2) to be called “Morger” instead of
“Major.” A deputation waited on him t'other
day from Way-Out, and a local councillor was
wiring in with the vehemence of a rescued
Salv’army recruit, demanding that a telephone
should be laid on to some cow-yards ; but, to the
consternation of his local member, he kept on
referring to the P.M.-G. as “Mr. Morger. ’
When Cow-yard paused to get a breather,, the
local member whispered—“ Call him Major.”
The amateur log-roller winked and looked wise,
and, resuming his remarks, dragged in a chance
to promptly refer to “my worthy friend ‘Major
Morger’.” In the explosion that followed, Cow-
yard said that he had finished.
James Watson, M.L.C., who answered the
quorum bell last week, was a native of Ireland, and
came to Sydney in 1856. Later, he ran a business
with his brother, at Lambing Flat (Young,
N.S.W.), where the Chows used to lose their pig-
tails; and in 1869 he joined the Sydney firm of
John Frazer and Co. In the same year he became
M.L.A. for Lachlan (N.S.W.), and Parkes made
him Treasurer in his 78 Ministry. Then he fell
into the Upper House, and stayed there till he
died, aged 70.
