Our utmost hope would be that in the far future there should be a Jewish province in Australia, perhaps
even a Jewish State, as not the least valuable member of the Australian Federation.— ISRAEL ZANOWILL.
The walls of the Promised Land.
Baptist Gardens, the site of the £35,000
worth of accommodation for a Sydney police
depAt now nearing completion, was one of
the best laid out and prettiest flower-plots in early
Sydney. John Thomas Baptist, who cultivated
what before his time was rocky and sandy ground,
was the first to set up the gardening standards of
the South of Europe in Australia. He was a
Portuguese, and arriving with a young family in the
’twenties of last century, addressed himself at once
to the soil, and in a few years was in a position to
supply most of the city with all tropical and semi-
tropical plants and flowers. Wealth literally
rolled in upon him. Much of his landed property
is still in the hands of descendants. The well-
known fountain in Hyde Park is a tribute to that
lucky old Baptist.
Died suddenly, last week, Henry James
Byrnes, P.M., of Cowra. He was son of the
James Byrnes who helped Martin and Parkes
financially and politically at many junctures, and
was on two occasions Works Minister. The
Australian head of the Byrnes family accom-
panied Captain Phillip as sergeant of marines,
and was thus to the front at the very
beginning of things. He took up his quar-
ters at Parramatta, and soon the Byrnes’
influence dominated that settlement. His sons
were in time owners of most of the place. They
ran stores, steamers, ’buses and mills. The
Byrnes’ Parramatta woollen mills were once a
flourishing Australian industry. Then they went
for politics, James in the Lower House and
brother William in the Upper, both living to a
great age, and spending on that hobby a lot of the
cash they had heaped up in their other pursuits.
Adelaide’s star “ Lucknow relief ” veteran
is George Thompson, who was a full cor-
poral at the time of the row, and did
something conspicuous at a, bridge 50
years ago. He did something just as
valuable when, a few years later, he
and half-a-dozen others established the
Adelaide Co-operative Stores on half a bag
of potatoes or thereabouts, and nursed it during
the next 35 years into a big, benevolent con-
cern. He retired from the general manager’s
job a couple of years ago.
By way of cultivating a hobby, W. M.
King, of Clayfield (Brisbane), has put in most
of his spare time during the last two years
in making a pipe organ, said to be the best
and largest ever made in Queensland by an
amateur. It contains 320 pipes, and has
nine stops. And, by the way, it was
made by an amateur in a country
where, according to the importer, the
trained workman can’t make a clothes
peg.
Burnand, ex-editor of Lon-
don Punch, has taken to judging
what the Daily Chronicle (Lon-
don) calls “Lipton Limericks.”
By the rules of this pastime,
which is carried on broadcast in
the Ma land just now, the first
four lines of the Limerick are
supDlied by the prize-offerer ; the
forwarder of the most blitheringly
idiotic fifth line collects the reward—
which latter runs sometimes as high as
7s. 6d. and £250. Adjudicating Limericks
is held to be an almost ideal pursuit for an ex-
editor of Punch.
“Jimmy” Graham, member for Surry
Hills (N.S.W.), takes pride in being familiarly
addressed by his constituents as “Doc.”
Yet a standing injunction in the medical
schools is “Don’t be a ‘Doc’.” The National
Medical Review printed an article on the
theme once, and heaped up instances showing
that the man who accepted the familiar address of
“Doc” invariably went to the bad in England
and elsewhere. “ A medical man may be called
a thief, a liar, and a deadbeat, and yet may
prosper and live on the fat of the land. But once
let him be called ‘Doc,’ and his professional
success is at an end.” By the way, they had just
begun to call the member for Middle Harbor
“Doc” at the immigration meeting, last week,
when he rose and resigned his position of president
of the society.
The repudiated remarks of the Victorian
Presbyterian parson, who, according to all the
papers, counselled Australia to shoo Byron, Burns
and Bacon off the premises on the ground of their
impropriety, recall the efforts of another “good”
man. His name was Randell, a ’Groper M.L.C.,
rising 80. Forrest put him into his Cabinet as
Minister for Education, on the principle, no
doubt, that a man is never too old to learn.
Randell, however, was too old to learn. But
he wasn’t too old to teach. At the annual prize-
giving of the chief State school in Perth, or there-
abouts, the head of the Department rose up and
denounced Shakspeare. He admitted that there
were two or three ideas of William S. that were
worth preserving, but he “ought to be re-
written.” People met him in the street after
that and assured him that he ought to push on
with the job, and that there were plenty of junior
clerks hanging round his Department doing
nothing.

Artist Arthur Streeton sailed from Mel-
bourne by the Everton Grange on October 20.
His destination is first the Cold Country, after-
wards Paris and Venice. He contemplates being
away about three years. Just before he left he
spent a week in Queenscliff (Vic.), fulfilling a com-
mission for his old schoolmate, W. L. Baillieu,
who desired a picture of the ’umble cottage on the
beach in which all the Baillieus were born and
reared.
Alfred Dreyfus retired from the French
army last month on a pension. He was con-
demned 13, and again eight, years ago on the
ground mainly that he was a Jew. He is an
acquitted man to-day (the Supreme Court of
France declared him “entirely innocent” in
July, last year), for the same reason. “ J’Accuse,”
the work of the Jew Zola did much, and Dreyfus’
own characteristically Hebrew endurance under
persecution, the rest.
Cow item from an employer’s evidence
before the N.S.W. Arbitration Court: —
John Hogan, dairyman, stated that he had seven wages
hands. His son rose at 5 a.m., worked until school time,
and resumed work after school. His wife and daughter
also worked, but none of them got any wages. His men
worked seven daj'S a week, and llj hours a day, and
received up to £1 per week.
After recording which, the Foreign-trade papers
tell Australia to stick to the Cow and other great
natural industries, and have nothing to do with
“ the wretched drudgery of factory life.”
“Gum”: Re new-chum Chinamen in
Queensland. A relation of mine has a large
garden in Victoria, in which he employs a number
of Chows. [Better change your relations.—Ed.
B.] Occasionally it is necessary to renew the
“ stock,” and a Melbourne Chinese firm in the
banana trade is always able to supply the required
Chow on a couple of weeks’ notice. When the
new chum arrives the head Chow takes him in
charge, and sets him to work for a while in the
least-frequented part of the Thus he
loses his freshness in the secluded country air,
gains a knowledge of English from his country-
men, and avoids the rude Customs officials, who
might ask awkward questions.

The existence of an Aliens Exclusion Act
on the statute book of Japan will, no doubt, come
as a shock to Spruce Myth and others of his
school. Certain railway contractors and mine
managers in Japan—discovering that the Chinese
coolie in Japan asked for only 20 cents (lOd.) per
day, and the Korean would work for even less, while
the Jap coolie demanded twice the wage—began
to import Chinese and Koreans. Just as in this
part of the world, they explained that it was not
because the foreign labor was cheaper, but
because it was more manageable. The local
labor market was disorganised, collisions be-
came frequent, racial troubles broke up the
peace of whole districts, and brought about
something like a state of war. The Jap
Government remembered that there was an Aliens
Exclusion Act, and, with the prompt methods of
a practical despotism, the law was set working
immediately. All Chinese and Koreans were
ordered to be dismissed and deported. Shipments
that arrived after the issue of the Imperial
Ordinance were taken in hand by the police and
sent back at the cost of the owners of the ships
that brought them. That Japan has the right to
exclude and deport them is unquestionable, but
the curious part of the business is that. Japan
wants to prevent other countries exercising the
same right.

YANKEE DOODLE.


A little lady you all know in this city was
once very unhappy. Everyone wondered why.
She had money—piles of it—a figure that men
used to fairly rave about—hosts of admirers—in
fact, was quite ripping. Only her complexion !
Blotchy and rough, tanned by the sun—it was
wretched. That was her sorrow. Then a change
came about—everyone noticed it. Her skin lost
all its blemishes—it became soft, and smooth, and
beautiful, with that clear satiny look you see in
the skins of little girlies. And the lady is no
longer sad. She is the admired of all. Would
you like to know how the change was brought
about. Listen, while I whisper it. Valaze.
That’s all. Valaze. Has the little fable any
meaning to you ?*

FACILE PRINCEPS
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 1907.
My Dear Arini,—
The Hartwell lady, who was Miss Georgette
Fleury, daughter of a worthy French-Algerian
trader, has secured her divorce from the faithless
Sir Brodrick, Bart., who arrived here on a yacht
with someone else’s matrimonial encumbrance
t’other day. Mrs. Joan Chamberlain, the flighty
aristocrat’s companion, is a tall, statuesque, albeit
somewhat vapid, type of beauty. If it is ever her
painful lot to lose Brodrick, she can always earn
an honest crust as dressmaker’s model. The lady
owns a figure that would “ set off ” a soogee bag.
The engagement is whispered about of the
charming Mrs. W. B. Dailey to a young official of
a Sydney shipping firm.
Anna Gould, the Yank “multi,” who divorced
her awful spouse, Boni de Castellane, the other
day, on about 120 good and sufficient grounds, is
going to face the matrimonial altar again. Her
new venture is, according to N. Y. World, a
prince who is “the most fascinating rakein Paris.”
In support of the latter contention the paper gives
a picture of him in the very act and fact of
alluring a lone female on a verandah. He does
his deadly work wearing an immense hat, cylin-
drical as any drainpipe, and with a per-
fectly straight brim. Around his neck is
wound, in about 1300 convolutions, something
that might either be a giant hunting stock or a
Bashi-Bazouk’s turban. His sprigged waistcoat
is double-breasted, and as huge as it is
gorgeous. Gigantic likewise are the trousers
which swathe his august legs, and the frock-coat
which droops about him elsewhere. Chains,
charms and monocles bestud his breast, and he
owns a moustache beside which Jawbone Neild’s
appears a mild and inoffensive decoration. The
paper has no opinion whatever of the prince’s
code of ethics. It delves into remote history
to his discredit. All his dead-and-gone rela-
tives sustain rough handling. But he remains
the absolute boshter of a peculiarly bonzer
family of moral horrors. “His admirers call
him the modern Alcibiades”; “havingexhausted
every form of vice, he has been attacked by
paralysis,” and, again, “ he has no spinal marrow
left.” He “ maintains 12 separate establishments
at once in Paris and other places.” Stanford
White’s Hamland retreats were mere feeble imi-
tations of this man’s Gallic ones. Ever since reach-
ing the age of indiscretion the nobleman has been
fighting duels. Whenever he gets bored, he goes
out, insults someone, and then there is a duel.
(This benefits the local ammunition factories, and
no one, unless lamentable carelessness is shown,
ever gets hurt.) This particular Alcibiades has
been engaged more times than he can count. Two
engagements to one duel is a rough estimate ; but
since the last fragment of spinal marrow left he
has gone slow. Abel Hermant, the Palais Royal
playwright, has put him into a piece in the guise
of a sort of Cyrano de Bergerac, who jests about
his skill in taking down the rich vulgarian, and
at the same time challenges anyone who hints
that he is “hot stuff” in this regard to acute
double pneumonia—the certain result of an early
morning duelling scrap in the chill, dank Bois de
Boulogne. “It would be impossible,” concludes
the World firmly, amid the edified plaudits of
every Yank, from saintly old Pittsburg to the
New York Tenderloin quarter, “to find any
man in the world more utterly unfit to become
the husband of an American woman.”
That gentle champion of Christianity, the
London Saturday Review, deals thus charitably
with the Deceased Wife’s Sister marriage, now at
last legalised in England :
The Church has no option but to make it quite clear
that it will not recognise these marriages. If the Govern-
ment has any sense of fairness, they will restore a clause
relieving an incumbent of the obligation to allow his
Church to be used for the celebration of these marriages,
if a clergyman can be found abandoned enough to offi-
ciate. If this is not done, the bishops must deal with the
situation by an authoritative pronouncement that they
will not recognise or have anything to do with any clerk
who marries parties under this Act. He will be a moral
and spiritual outlaw. . . Social ostracism is a powerful
deterrent. Strong measures are necessary.
Meanwhile London gossip in general, caring not
twopence for the matter either way, speaks
pleasantly of it as “ an Act for the Abolition of
Aunts.”
Hardly a day has passed within the last fort-
night without one of the Sydney seaside mayors
explaining the reasons why he didn’t want surf
bathers to wear a skirt, and how nobody had ever
suggested such a thing. Mayor McDougall, of
Rand wick, has just given the thousand-and-oneth
explanation, in which he said that he had never con-
sidered the skirt for men at all, but was deter-
mined that women should be compelled to wear
them in the water. As the principal ground of
complaint against the skirt is its danger, the
average person, not a seaside mayor, fails to see
any more reason why women should be drowned
than men. If the skirt is intended to conceal
the female form, it is as well to know
that it would utterly fail in this object,
seeing that the moment a skirted woman-bather
gets into the water the skirt is converted to a
cumbersome belt about her waist, conveying the
impression that she is in ordinary walking cos-
tume, which has become seriously disarranged.
The municipality of Manly, a year ago, made
skirts for women compulsory, but, after a trial,
so many women persisted in entering the water in
the old one-piece costumes that the authorities
gave up the attempt to enforce the by-law.
Anyhow, it is hard to tell who is offended
by the sight of the lady whose costume suggests
her figure. It can’t be the other women, for the
matter is of no interest to them, and it doesn’t
seem to be the men, and if you exclude the women
and the men from the population, there are only
the mayors left.
From Palings’ comes a patriotic song, “ For the
Honor of the Flag,” by May Pettifer. Its senti-
ment is culled from the policies of all right-think-
ing wimmen’s leagues, and the rather tame verse
apostrophises king, country and flag. It has a
simple, tuneful swing, and offers little difficulty
to the vocalists who like to advertise their
patriotism by inoffensive ditties.
From Brisbane :
The Governor returned to the State on Saturday, and
sheds a pale light upon the proceedings of the Austral
Festival at Toowoomba on Monday.
There is said to be a rift in the Metropolitan Anglican
See that threatens to engulf Bishopsbourne itself. The
Archbishop goes about with a look of worry that even the
recent revelations over the Infants’ Home failed to pro-
duce. The trouble appears to be centred around the
Bible in State schools question.
The Clara Butt-Kumford party had a great audience at
the Exhibition Hall. But Brisbane is bad at enthusing,
and there was a considerable dearth of flowers, and people
got up and hurried on with their cloaks ere the last note had
died away. If the Angel Gabriel and his best trump band
performed here, I don’t believe he could keep people from
leaving before the end of the world.
The great profession of tooth-pulling is about the most
paying of specs, in Brisbane. The beer-engine isn’t in it
with the dental grinder. Was told yesterday that our
most fashionable lady-dentist has just purchased a car,
and will shortly whirl through the city. As this young
lady has not been in business a year, some idea may be
arrived at of the rate at which the shekels are raked in.
The engagement is announced of pretty Ethel Murray
Prior, daughter of the late T. de Murray Prior, of Maroon,
and Roy Butler, of Kilcoy stud estate.
From “Rosna,” in Adelaide :
Our Residence is in mourning, so we go without the
one little festivity, a garden party, that was to have
broken the monotony of Cup month in Adelaide.
Nothing but the Butt visit remains to cheer us up, after
which we shall put up the shutters on our town mansion,
and betake us to the drowsy kine and the frisky lambkin.
Little Stella Hall gave an invitation piano recital in
the Town Hall t’other evening, and the Salt was
there. The pianist certainly prodded the instru-
ment somewhat forcefully, and showed promise ; but
her work is, as yet, unequal and immature. Some
judicious person should also see to her frocking. Frocks
are half the battle in this wicked world—as the cute
people who stood at the elbow of little Leila Doubleday
realised. The vocal interjections were provided by two
back-from-Britain-ers, Frank Robertson and Elsie Jones ;
the latter of these two is being heaped with engagements
and sweet-smelling vegetables.
After all, Adelaide missed a view of Beatrice Irwin in
the Julius Knight co., because abrother or some other
inconsiderate relative died at the wrong moment. How-
ever, Miss Elbertorton was new to Adelaide, so we bore
up. The lady struck us frockily. In “ Rattles” she wore
a wicked green-eyed garment, and a snaky velvet hat
proclaimed a vivid Past. Now she is frocking the naive
Woodland maiden in “ Robin Hood” with equal splendor
and thought.
Apropos, an Adelaide damsel who has figured largely
here during the last year or two, has gone on the stage
by way of retrieving "the family fortunes. ’Tis said she’s
to have a speaking part in the first production of the
“Scarlet Pimpernel.”
The ’Varsity undergrads, are to hire the Theatre Royal
for their December revelries this year. They no longer
relieve the dry ceremonial of Commemoration with their
mad hilarities, but stay firmly away and disport them-
selves in some public place, where Faculties don’t shake a
reproving finger. Last year, as an experiment, they hired
the Tivoli and packed it at 3s. a head. And though age
find respectability are supposed to be agin’ it, I don’t
believe it would take much to make the Council itself
drop its dignity and fight for seats with the rest of the
world.
From London :
To-day’s charmer of the Big Smoke wears a gold whistle
on her muff-chain or her chatelaine or her cable bangle.
This is not for summoning a cab, but for collecting Spot
or Towzer or any other similarly named yelper. The pet
learns the key or tone of his lady’s whistle, and comes
hurriedly out of the gutter to leap at her clothes with all
his four muddy feet. The dog craze is the strongest
passion the Englishwoman admits, or at least publicly
demonstrates. The coldest type and variety of her hugs
the soiled yelper to her proud bosom in the public eye,
and kisses his dirty little nose before the street
gaper in almost unconscious ecstasy. There are
motor-goggles for Spot now, and a whole petrol-
car outfit; and the brute sits on a little cushioned
perch behind the steerer, thrusting an inquisi-
tive nose out at every sign-post or halt op the road.
Their own mistresses put up with looks of boredom and
satiety from these pampered little beasts which they
would not suffer from a rich godfather or a wealthy
eligible. “My dog’s a perfect tyrant,” admitted an
Honorable somebody to me the other day; and she told
the truth. (A woman often does by accident.)
Melbourne, November 4, 1907.
It was no use staying in Sydney. Everyone
was going to Melbourne. So I went too. Mel-
bourne is a much better dressed city to-day
than when I left it six years ago. I don’t
refer to the frocking of its women the
instinct towards that urges like gravitation
—but to its municipal dressing of trees and
shrubs and plants that climb and cling. It is
dressed like a salad, especially Collins-street,
where the elms have added many cubits to their
stature, and where these spring days old birds are
teaching baby birds to fly. And under the green
and leafy dusk of the “fair, brave trees” walks
as of yore the ever-silvered Reggie Bright, on his
way to or from his club. There’s a legend that
Reggie came up the Yarra with Batman. Be that
as it may, his silver top still wins the benedictions
of debutantes to-day, who wreathe with their
fluffy compliments the old buck. Another road,
made marvellously leafy and spacious, grown out
of all recollection —finished with a pencil instead
of a spade, so to speak—is that from Prince’s
Bridge to St. Kilda. In Sydney we don’t muffle
our public buildings in green, as is done in Mel-
bourne, to Melbourne’s advantage. The barracks
on St. Kilda Road have hidden their stony face
behind the green hair of the Virginia creeper.
Three men-of-war disturb the summer’s doze
in the barracks garden where the strong marigolds
feast on the sun. Colonel Stanley, with the bend
in his back unimpaired, squares his shoulders
under the burden of Commandantship, and says
the diplomatic words that leave the visitor in
doubt as to whether he would rather be looking
from behind the unveiled face of the barracks at
Paddington or standing here backgrounded by
the white lauristine that smells of honey and the
sea. The second man is one of our own Sydney-
siders, Major Legge, and both he and the
Colonel look like smart school-boys beside
the third person, the leviathan Colonel Hopton,
the English visitor of rifle-shot fame. Goliath
Hopton towers to the low-lying thunder-clouds,
and impinges unduly east, west, north and south
beyond his place in the Cosmos. And when he
gets on to the road, and Melba’s motor-brougham
—a huge touring-car—whizzes by him, one thinks
that, if there was a collision, it would be the
motor, not the man, who would suffer.
Melba’s motor goes several times a day on this
road, trailing clouds of glory and of dust. The
a
prima donna, who is living Malvern way, is
little ruddier than on her last visit, but her eye s
burn more fiercely bright. Astute men of affair s
who meet her talk quite reverently of her com'
prehensive grasp of problems with a big P. One
of them said she should have been sent to the
Peace Conference at the Hague. Another told
me he had seen her four times in the same gown
—a chiffon with blue and black in it. She
wore it on Derby Day. A constant squire
of the Chief Skylark is the MaeCulloch, a
member of a well-known Victorian family.
He is a gallant widower, with the face
of a well-groomed war charger. His late
wife was a sister of that ill-starred genius,
“ Jack ” Wagner, whom many Sydney-
siders remember with affectionate regret.
Melba has only to be seen in public with a Some-
body bachelor, like Sir Harry Rawson or the
horse-faced gentleman, for people to say she is to
be married to him.
At the first night of Meynelland Gunn’s “Mid-
night Wedding,” Melba, with her son and
daughter-in-law and the charger-faced gentleman
in a box, drew the concerted gaze of the house.
It seemed to amuse her, and she gazed back
somewhat through her jewelled lens. Daughter-
-in-law Ruby is a short person, of just
nineteen years, with star-bright dark eyes,
the loveliest of complexions, and a small, delicate,
parrot-beaked nose. She rides astride when in
the country, and lets her hair blow loose. But to
hear her conversing in French and German (in a
drawing-room where there were some foreigners
who knew no English) is to believe that she has
had unusual advantages. She seems to have read
everything, and been everywhere, and the over-
whelming Melba hasn’t even begun to whelm
Colonel Jocelyn’s one ewe lamb.
That Melba will give some concerts during her
stay in Australia is now generally known. She
is too much alive to put her vocal chords out of
business for many months. Papa Mitchell, boss-
ing his big contract for Buckley and Nunn’s new
premises and going strong on his other concerns,
wool and wine ones included, also proves his
vitality.
MISS CARRIE LANCELY, a bright young
Australian soprano, who went recently to
London to study under Charles Santley.
Cables tell of her success on London concert
platforms.
Melbournians seem to take their play-acting
more seriously than Sydneysiders. In “The
Midnight Wedding ” Gaston Mervale, as the
wicked officer, was enthusiastically hooted and
groaned at by the gallery for his attempted damage
to the spotless reputation of Princess Astrea.
When Princess Astrea (Madge Mclntosh) takes
her riding-whip to the intruder she was entreated
by a galleryite to “give him the father and
mother of a beating,” and Hareourt Beatty had
compliments and offers of drinks for himself and
collars for his dog showered upon him. The King
Charles doglet that played a dozing part in ‘ ‘ Sweet
Nell,” is stdl with the actor, and “ Mr. Beatty has
gone to take the dog for a walk ” is a frequent
answer given to callers at his hotel.
By their ties ye shall know them. Poseidon’s
male friends and backers are wearing violet ties.
The large Foy clan has some stern, wild, kilted
porridge ties. As for the Albury Mitchells —one
member of whom owns Dyed Garments —they
don't dress down to their nag’s name lest people
say they got their clothes from a pop-shop. At a
gold-dusted cafe on Derby night after the theatres
there was a “Poseidon table.” His owner, Mr.
Denison and Mrs., beautifully gowned in white
and silver, the W. Camerons (her lace gowns and
§ olden head were not small potatoes at our recent
ydney Spring meet), and the Benjamins occupied
it. A Napoleonic-looking party named Poolman,
recently bought out (from Port Melbourne) by the
Colonial Sugar Refining Co. for a sum resembling a
war indemnity, had another glittering party.
One lady—don’t know if she was tagged on to
Poolman’s party or not —had a small peacock in
her hair. Every “eye” on the tail was an
emerald. Its head eyes were rubies. All it
wanted, to be the real thing, was life and a
raucous voice.
Bookmakers’ wives are the most expensively
dressed Cuppers in Melbourne. But that doesn’t
mean that they are not sometimes dressed with
good taste as well. One lady, whose diamonds are
blinding Melbourne dress-circle audiences, has
nine pairs of evening shoes to match her nine new
evening frocks, golded and silvered footwear in-
cluded, and her monogram, a detachable device in
blue and silver thread, is put on to the instep of
the particular daisy-breaker she selects.
On an afternoon this week three Sydneysiders
met in Collins-street, each bursting to tell the
others that the Melbourne hansom man ought to
be thrown down a sewer oii something vicious.
Each had been most humorously overcharged
by cabby. My complaint was that cabby had
made me pay three shillings for driving me from
George and George’s to Government House and
bank to Allan’s. The animal driving walked
the animal in the shafts, despite the fact
that I alternately admonished him with a
few words, or a red sunshade poked through
his porthole. From start to finish, morgue
pace notwithstanding, the journey was 20
minutes. The god on the vehicle explained that
“three shillings was the Government House fare.”
I forget the cost of the other woman’s grievance ;
but the third complainant was a man, and his
cabby asked him for three half-crowns for driving
him to Colonel Stanley’s house, although the
journey was done in fifteen minutes. He reck-
lessly gave the money. On the day of the Govern-
ment House garden party, quite sixteen hundred
persons, the majority of them wearing costly,
perishable, fragile, and immediate frocks, trammed
as far as Government House gates, and walked
the remainder; find the healthy exercise is quite
understandable if the Melbournians are mal-
treated by their cabbies as are the visiting
miserables.
Melbourne has the most gorgeous drove of
motor cars. For one you’ll see in Sydney there
are 20 here. We keep motor launches in-
stead, and take our pleasure on the blown wet
face of the sea. And all the Melbourne petrol
carts are closed, for the dust is as exasperating
as it is constant. For dusty reasons also the
Melbourne girl wears gossamer veilings 29 days
in the month. She’s a friendly puss, the Mel-
bourne girl. The first stranger damsel I met
showed me her new powder puff, a square of
chamois leather with about a teaspoonful of
powder permanently ambushed under an oblong
strip of chamois. This strip is perforated with
tiny holes, and just enough fine flour filters
through. When not in active service the puff is
rolled into an atomic roly-poly pudding shape, and
tied with white satin ribbon.
Doubling back to the Garden Party: The lawn,
being as big as Monaco or Garden Island, held all
“ those present.” At four o’clock a wireless
message to come to tea must have reached the
“ brilliant assemblage, ” for it let down its train,
turned its collective eye and feet towards the
House, and slowly centipeded to where the
browse was laid. The point of this observation
sticks into the trailing trains. The simultaneous
sweep of a thousand or so silky fabrics over the
grass was audible below the crash of the band
and the gas and giggle of conversation. It
was on one note, even, insistent, penetratingly
sweet, and lasting for quite five minutes. I don’t
recollect noticing anything of the kind before,
unless it were a baby wind running through green
barley, and that didn’t travel on one note.
A hive of Sydney people at the G. party : the
Clibborns, Mrs. Oscar Backhouse, and Mrs. John
Williamson. Mister John is known here as “Tariff”
Williamson. No man could be as innocent as Mr.
Williamson looks (that’s a quote, of course, but
it fits “Tariff” like his coat). He has christened
Bill Lyne, Beale Lyne. But William’s troubles !
Mrs. J. T. Toohey is a Cupper, and Mrs. and Miss
Sparke, and Miss Pearl Faithfull, whose painted
chiffon, shaded like the Rainbow Falls in the
Yosemite, was one of the gowns on Derby Day;
and the Matthew Harrises, some Chisholm and
Marks branches. Mrs. Maclurcanand pretty Jean
and Mr. Egan, the fiance of the last, are here
taking bites of the capricious weather.
I arrived this day week, and was told that
the day was an exquisite one. As my intellect
was tottering after the malignant conditions of
the rail journey (yet our bunch was as tenderly
cared for as if we were eggshell china), I don’t
remember the quality of that day, but the next
one was above 90° in the shade, with an able-
bodied dust-storm doing business in partnership
with a screaming wind. The third day one
couldn’t see out of doors for the torrential down-
pour of rain. Derby Day, more heat and dust.
Sunday, more lances of rain. To-day (Monday)
is cold, and so threatening that I fear Sir
Harry Rawson will lose his biton Poseidon. Our
State Governor dreamt Poseidon, you must know;
and report says that the Pos. horse won’t win if
he runs in mud.
Talking of horse. A great bunch of men were
standing in the vestibule of the hostel that I
pervade, when someone said that the distinguished
mare Solution was about to be sold up at Some-
thing rooms. The bursting of a bomb couldn’t
have cleared out those men faster. Solution
fetched ten thousand and odd guineas. (Yet she
wears but one race coat of bay pelt.)
Dr. Arthur Weston, of Point Piper, and Mrs.
are here for the opening of the new Dental Hospi-
tal. Mr. Marich, of Paling’s, is trying to look
ill-humored over something, and failing signally.
Major Lenehan motored from Sydney with the
Rose Bay Smiths. They were so black with dirt
on arrival in the city that the police were going
to do something to them as colored and undesir-
able aliens. The Majah has Ballaratitis badly,
as the party stopped at a convent there
and an adorable Reverent Mother, eighty years
young, admired the soldier and wanted him to
stay and become a monk or a nun or something.
The Windeyers, of Newcastle, were at the garden-
party, daughter Daisy doing the coaly city credit.
The item that took my eye on Derby Day was
a white silk parasol embroidered with three raised
wreaths of strawberries and leaves. The paths,
so to speak, between the strawberry-beds were
filled with frills of narrow lace. And there was a
gown of primary-hues chiffon with a plain over-
skirt of pure white chiffon. That was an unusual
touch.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

WORLDS
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, November 4, 1907.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Marvellous Melba has bought a basinet— the
basinet which adorns a section of the Women’s
Exhibition. This surprising purchase is stirring
the imagination of gossip-gadders, who have
come to the disappointing conclusion that the
future occupant won’t have the chance of a
hereditary fortune in the shape of a Voice. In
fact, they’ve decided that the imminent infant
will merely be a fairy god-child or something of
the sort. After Melba’s performances in many
parts, it would be rather interesting to see her
play grandmother. This basinet, by the way,
arouses the envy of every woman who has even
seriously considered matrimony.
Melba has moved into “ Flete,” the house where
the Hartley Williams family once bided. “ Ereil-
doun,” meanwhile, will see the diva and her
retinue only during hot weather spells, or when
Toorackety diversions become too warm to be
tolerable. The Voice candidly confesses that
rural retirement sometimes seems as exhausting
as an opera season.
The Cats’ Conference at Cliveden was a joyous
farce. Delegates from every flourishing women’s
league in Australia have made laborious pil-
grimages that they might loose their eloquence
under the nose of Janet Mrs. Clarke, and show
local vote deflectors what sort of wonders are
grown in other States. Addresses were limited to
a quarter-hour, but debates had neither beginning
nor end. Apparently, if a fat matron chose to
vocalise the middle and end of an independent
trail of thought, nobody cared. Or, if a sparse
spinster opened up the subject of conscription,
while the Conference was considering the suit-
ability of Miss Smitb-Jones’ hose to the shanks
of a respectable never-to-be-married woman, and
the desirability of a White Australia, it didn’t
seem to matter much. The chief thing was
Cliveden, and the dear Dowager who’d been so
politically generous. Really she did do the
delegates well. Afternoon tea was invariably
faultless, and some of the Naicest leaguers have
been bidden to Cliveden festivities, in their un-
official capacity. _ What more could political
petticoats desire in their most aspiring moments
of patriotic fervor and national concernment?
By-the-way, a Sydney suffragette repeated her
famous bon-mot, to the effect that, if men were our
masters, it needed only singleness of purpose and
untiring energy (she didn’t mention on whose
part!) to make us their mistresses. Can such
humor be unintentional, one wonders.
Sometimes I am inclined to doubt it. Are we
not all familiar with the lady who wilfully says
something capable of a giddy meaning so that she
may show her savage virtue by snubbing the
victim who giggles? Perhaps the lady on the
Elatform always blunders innocently ; I’ll try and
elieve so.
Those tableaux in the big concert hall at the
Women’s Exhibition were watched by a packed
squash of folk, and at the end of each tableau
be-rouged and made-up maidens would flutter
round and join their doting parents, and smile
delightedly through sooty eyelashes at the attend-
ant Johnnydom. It has been The Thing, with
a capital “ T,” to pose in these pictures.
Foremost among the spectators, the hard-
worked Madden and Forrest Dames folded their
hands in the bliss of perfect triumph. It was
really “worth the whipping.” The Woman’s
Orchestra and an occasional warbless assisted the
much-rehearsed effects with sweet music. Burne-
Jones’s picture, “ Dante’s Dream,” was faithfully
copied. The extreme neckiness of that neck of
the bier-resting maiden had always appealed to
me as unnatural in the 8.-J. work, but pretty
Mrs. Raleigh stretched her pretty column
to quite the pictured length, and the re-
sult was good. Mrs. Raleigh’s face is rather
thin, but, like Trilby, she has “beautiful bones.”
Dame Forrest’s masterpiece, “The Sultan’s
Daughter,” was finely posed, with pretty Miss
Eileen Whiting in the name part. After all the
talk there had been about the trousers of the
Eastern beauty, the long-expected garments re-
solved themselves into quite flowing draperies,
and the drooped posing of the tired attendants
was good. In a following tableau a tall maiden in
abbreviated white tunic and lengthy brown-tights
legs called forth an amazed remark from a mere
man. “By Jove! Is that the girl who refused
to wear ’em.” He had picked up some odd scraps
of the early “trousers” discussion, and was re-
garding the brown understandings as further
proofs of inconsistency in the sex that runs the
show.
Musical competitors are emitting a long and
plaintive wail about Exhibition arrangements.
The trouble surrounds “The Spinning Chorus”
from Wagner’s “ Flying Dutchman.” There are
four sets of competitors, I think. The University
Conservatorium crowd, the Marshall Hall folk, a
bevy from Hobart, and warblers led by Mrs. Garew,
of Melbourne. The judge is being kept a deeply
dark and mysterious secret. Each bevy, of course,
will do “ The Spinning Chorus.” Chief wail is
that each performance is to be separated by two
and sometimes three weeks, an arrangement
which is pleasing to the tribunal, but hard on the
competitors. For how can a mortal judge com-
pare the points with the softening lapse of time
between? “Also,” wail the First Comers, “the
Others will come and grab all our little tricky
ideas and innovations—criticise our failures, and
improve on obvious mistakes.” Melbourne Uni-
versity Conservatorium come first in the list.
Their wail is feline. They have been pre-dated
fully three weeks, and fur is flying. For one
thing, Frocks are not ready. And that is deadly.
To be caught, like a foolish virgin, without a suit-
able frock when the conductor’s baton taps is a
depth of disgrace not tolerable.
Mrs. Sydney-Carpet Edwardes—over here with
Exhibition enthusiasms —broke into one of the
huge jovial all-embracing Teas that the Exhibition
Madames are wallowing in. This spread of ices
and bun took up almost all the platform room of
Paris Cafe, and the chatter was like a flutter-fight
in an aviary. Just-returned Madame Slapoffski
starred as guest. She is in Melbourne, waiting
for Hubby. Then they jaunt to Sydney. “ We’ll
furnish and settle there,” says Mrs. Slap.
On Friday, at the Women’s Work Exposition,
Mrs. Janson arranged a programme of Scan-
, dinavian music. It was a real privilege to music
lovers. Mrs. Christeson was chief coadjutor
to the plump contralto, and sang some songs of
wonderful power, by the Finnish composer
Sinding. And the Whitley string quartette made
strange, sweet music.
The Naval and Military Ball, the night before
the Derby, was a brilliant event. The affair was
more in the hands of Military officers than Naval,
and several spoilt darlings of the Navy stood out
in a pet. Heard someone murmur : “ Anyhow, the
Ball’s a success—even if Tracy would not come.”
I heard one languid Sydney dame put quite a
swing into her voice in praise of it. “ We’d never
have such a scene in Sydney,” she declared. “ No-
body’d have enough enthusiasmand energy to work
it up.” It was a disappointment that the war boats
had not got round; it left out a great and
adored splash of officers. Nevertheless, the girls
had a good time. The entertainment scheme had
been carefully thought out—the numbers were so
large. Every colonel and captain made himself
personally responsible for the owners of his wedge
of invitations, and everything went smoothly.
The only mistake was that no one in particular
was told off for A.D.C. duty for the fringes of the
various Governor’s encircling suites. Heard some
of the hospitable officers concernedly discussing
the matter next day. “ We’ve never had so many
Governors to deal with at once in our lives,” de-
clared one, amazedly. “ We should have told-off
a special escort for each exhibit.” “ A Governor-
General and a Lieutenant-Governor bracketed, is
no small matter; but Governors by the gross need
thinking out.”
The Northcote Lady looked fine in black Spanish
lace, draped over her white and silver frock. She
drew a court train magnificently after her, and
wore a tiara. The Northcote Lady owns beauti-
ful feet and shoulders. The feet are always
beautifully clad, and the shoulders generally
gracefully unclad. All the other Governor ladies
were dressed in silks and glitter, but the Chelms-
ford lady wore black, and muffled herself, even
while dancing, in a funereal-looking black feather
boa. The magnificent-looking stranger man, in
gorgeous uniform, who had roused much com-
ment, resolved into Count Morner, the new
Swedish Consul for Sydney, or somewhere.
German opera again, and the same old crowd at
the Princess’s. There was quite a re-union ; and
the thick, soft sauerkrauty accent was like a
blanket folding everyone in a big warm embrace.
Mrs. Carlyle Smythe (who has wandered
over Maoriland with hubby and the com-
pany) leaned back in her seat and gloated,
“ This is Home. Give me Melbourne and
my Princess’s after Maoriland mummer halls.”
Pretty little Miss Mohwinkel—niece (who has
also wandered with Uncle)—was just as gloating
(even though Melbourne is not Yarmany). Her
moan of Maoriland is summed up in one wail,
Invercargill. “We arrived there at eleven one
night—raining steadily—a dark railway platform,
no porters, no cabs. We walked in a mournful
procession along streets pitch dark (lights put
out at ten), and tried to find our hotel.
There was no hotel, so we went to a coffee
palace. I rang for a syphon of soda water. They
wouldn’t bring it —said Invercargill was a Pro-
hibition Town. I convinced them that soda
water held no alcohol and they promised to
obtain it ‘ in a day or two ’ —Oh In-ver-car-gill! ”
Folk had forgotten in the softening interim
the awful implacability of the management as
far as door-closing was concerned. “Lohengrin ”
was allowed to begin at eight, but a
happy Derby crowd who had backed Mountain
King did not worry about little things like
that, and motored along after the fateful fan-
fare. The lockout was quite real, nevertheless,
and they had to sustain themselves with coffee
and chatter for a solid hour —what time wild
shrieks came from the prima donna within, and an
occasional blare of thunderous orchestra. When
there is not a race meeting the awful lockout
will begin at 7, 7 30, and 7.40. On Saturday the
half-hour intervals were chopped down to ten
minutes, and the comfy lounge, chatter, and
stretch was quite cruelly cut out. Flowers by the
cartload were shovelled up to Heinze and Mia
Barcow during a pell-mell rush of the audience
for their last train.
First day at Flemington—Derby Day—was a
shout of jolly color and well-groomed form. There
was the row of Governors and Governoresses that
we are getting so used to (what a blank there’ll be
after the races, when we are reduced to a mere
G.-G. and a G.-lieutenant!) sitting in their gor-
geous blue and gold pen, with luscious roses to
help on the color. Our Governor Lady was in
blue, and the visiting Ladies flaunted many
plumes in soft grey and fawn color. One Madden ■
maiden carried a country hedge of roses on her
head.
Frocking is wonderfully pretty nowadays—but
it seems to be the Hat that counts; the modish
style of dress is simple and quiet, so the ultra-
smart let their fancy cry out loudly in headgear.
Recipe : Get the biggest all-round straw hat pro-
curable at the moment (the kind fruit-pickers
wear near-by the Murray and Goulburn), pile on
all the fruit, flowers, plumes, wings and ribbon
you can afford, and be sure to let some tumble
over your left ear ; do your hair on a cart-wheel
frame to make it fill the inside vacuum, and pin
odd curls to the brim with pins the size of primi-
tive daggers. Then you’re “all there.”
Melba was better dressed than she has
appeared before in the Glaring Eye (and that says
much—her frocking is a symphony). Very soft
grey was the toning, with cool touches of white —
a shady, plumed white hat, and a duck of a lace
parasol. A pink-clad daughter-in-law was
with her. Saw Heinze, of the opera com-
pany, sailing about in all-black. The Von
Pinschof maidens wore their gorgeous ama-
teur-theatrical frocks (the frocks were de-
cidedly not amateur). Demure Miss Phyllis
of the Clarkes chummed about with Mrs. Pinky-
Frocky Raleigh; so did the little Fenner girls.
Dame Forrest seemed to enjoy herself with a
crowd of Westralians and Tasmanians; her frock
looked like strawberries-and-cream. Mrs. Nor-
man Menzies buried her energetic little self under
her hat—there were huge pink roses tumbling
over her white-clad shoulder. Mrs. George
Chirnside was at her Frenchiest point—pale
mauve hat, gown, gloves, everything ; purple
peonies pinned in her dress. Saw one
frock that stood out by itself (Mrs. Peters was
within it). It was crepe, the tint of old ivory and
lilac tumbled round the foot, drooping and crush-
ing over a black crepe hem and a vivid green
border.
The George Chirnsides had a swagger lunch
party under the vines, and the Pinschof maidens
spread themselves in a Strawberry Tea. The
L. L. Smiths’ lunch party entertained the gaud
Mayor and his Lady, Mr. Bent and other notables.
The luncheon parties were quite happy between
wind-gusts; poor John and Adolphe had the
worry—the wind lifted the whole cloth and
all the table furnishings in playful pleasantry
at unexpected moments, and the salt tipped into
the charlotte russe, and the strawberries rolled
into the salad.
Garden-party, ball, races, theatre—that is the
alternate-day programme nowatimes. One poor,
gold-laced atom moaned at the last garden-party,
“ This is the third time I’ve dressed to-day, and
I’m due for three more dressings before I blessedly
disrobe.” This is certainly a giddy race season.
On Friday night, the “ Como ” Armytages gave
a big dance, and there was a flutter of carriages
going on to “ Como,” or varying the pro-
gramme by finishing with the gay, latter part of
the “Naval and Military.”
Private dances are the order of the night, and
increase the whirligig gaiety. Admiral Bridges
and his wife gave one on Wednesday night.
Their own ballroom would not meet the demands
of the hordes of extra inter-State visitors, so the
Pr’an Town Hall was hired. It was a most
swagger dance; the Northcotes guested, which
is an unusual favor with private dances. A
florist flew into dreams and fancies to deck the
hall suitably. The ceiling is domed and very
lofty. There was plane after plane of soft-
colored electrics, making misty stars with dim,
purply blue—like gentians—in the far back-
ground, and the walls were hedged in with roses.
The Weatherleys, of “ Belillah,” at Brighton,
also broke out into a dance that would not fit into
the home ballroom. Brighton Town Hall had to
be annexed.
Messrs. Meynell and Gunn gave an At Home
at Menzies’ on Thursday to meet the warm-haired
Miss Madge Mclntosh. Rupert Clarke was
much in evidence, and Daughter Phyllis fluffed
quietly in a blue and feathery hat and dress that
snowed up the milk-and-roses of her complexion.
Hasn’t Phyllis got just papa’s eyebrows and nose?
The Cummings maiden and sister Mrs. Robertson
aired their lately-arrived selves, and little Mrs.
A’Beckett had hubby in tow. It seems a good
wicket to marry a settled bachelor ; they are so
tame to handle. Little Mrs. Willie has no
end of a good time. The musical programme
was just right. There was an unusual item.
It sounded like a violin, then a ’cello. It was the
auburn-haired Lioness of the At Home playing
the concertina —most beautifully. I don’t know if
the instrument has a different name in polite
society, but it was the musical box that ’Arriet
plays driving ’ome in the moonlight, with the
same sentimental wavings and undulations.
The most gorgeous gowns—flocks and bundles
and hundreds of them—trailed the lawns at
Government House garden-party on Friday. It
was a record party for numbers, and as for
plumage, it might have been a congress of all the
birds of the Tropics. The Lady Northcote trailed
silk of pinky lilac, and her parasol and gloves and
veil were all of the same soft, cool color, with a
daring, but effective, contrast hit out in a small
toque of misty blue-and-pink. The Madden Lady
was perfectly gowned in white lace, filmy beauti-
ful lace (and the lawns were so green). Ruby
Madden looked very tired in a most wide-awake
green.
There were many men at this lawn party; quite
an innovation. Many came without their frilly-
frocked halves (who were Exhibitioning), and
exploded the idea men are wont to moan, that
their wives drag them to garden shivoos.
Visitors from other States are here in myriads.
N. S. Wales folk simply bank up the streets and
trams and and hotels ; and as for Queens-
landers ! At a recent At Home there were two
hundred Queensland visitors alone. The whole
island of Tasmania is deserted, and Westralians
are as numerous almost as the sands of their State.
The engagement is proclaimed of Geva Stanley,
daughter of Mrs. Riddell Stanley, of “Cabers
Carre,” Elsternwick, Melbourne (and daughter of
the late Commander H. J. Stanley, R.N.), to
Colonel S. W. Dashwood, sth Fusiliers (North-
umberland), son of the late Rev. S. V. Dashwood,
of “ Loughborough,” Notts, England. The com-
bination of Army, Navy, and Church in the
announcement is as very English and respectable
as the names of the contracting parties.
Miss Phyllis Clarke has been flourishing a
busy pen. An invasion of MSS., bearing the
brands of Victoria’s only baronetcy, threatens to
disturb the calm of several paper offices.
Sydney artists did well at the Women’s
Work Exhibition. Florence Rodway gains first
prize for a charcoal head, and second for figure
painting ; Ethel Stephens first for flower paint-
ing ; Dagmar Ross second for animal painting;
and Bernice Edwell first for miniature painting.
The Syd. Secessionists have reaped a fine
harvest. Both attendance and sales have been
healthily large. Norman Lindsay has established
perhaps a world’s record amongst contemporary
black-and-white men by his “Pollice Verso,”
bought for 150 guineas by the National Gallery.
The others, too, have done well. As usual, the
chief investors in artistic assets were A.
Douglas Hart, Prof. Spencer, rotund Theo.
Fink, and that wise man, L. Abrahams.
Yours affectionately,
J OHANNA.

Radyard Kipling marle a powerful plen at the Canadian
Club Ottawa, against the exclusion of Asiatics.
“ Ladies and gentlemen, as you all seem, to have
forgotten my poetry, let me introduce to you
two new little items which I think,” etc., etc.
Owing to the “iniquitous tariff,” Lipton,
the big British tea man, intends to establish a
tea-packing house in Australia.
Wade’s proposed remissions of taxation in
N.S.W. all relieve the richer classes, who are
a ready more lightly taxed in Australia than in
almost any other part of the world.
“Micawber”: Witnessed a pathetic in-
cident last week—am ex-M.L.A. paying his
tram-fare for the first time in seventeen vears.
the look of dumb hound-like appeal o'n the
unfortunate fellow s face I shall never forget.
Premier Wade promises to “ consider the
advisability” of the imposition of an Absentee-
tax. it will -if Ins purpose is carried through
somewhat mitigate the dishonesty of the proposed
income-tax remission.
Mr. Fisher is to be “ Chairman,” and not
leader, of the Australian Labor party, which sug’-
gests that, at least, there is no intention of ex-
tending the power of the position. Said by one
paper that in the event of the Labor party Chair-
man being “sent for” to form a Ministry he will
have to delegate the choice of his colleagues to
the caucus.
Wade, neatly, on Dan O’Connor’s wail re
not being able to “ see ” N.S.W. Agent-General
Loghlan, the other day.: “When a man is ‘out’
it may mean that he isn’t ‘in’ to irresponsible
callers from all parts of the world.”

“In Switzerland disqualification from
military service is a matter for shame, and those
who are unable to pass the examinations for the
armed services, seek to gain entrance to one
branch or another of the various assistant troops
—the sanitary, supply, transport, intelligence, or
bureau departments.”—London Tribune, 17/9/’O7.

Justice Higgins, in the Federal Arbitration
Court lately:—
The demand for working people in Collingwood is in-
creasing by reason of increased manufactures. The more
work the more demand for houses, and the more demand
for houses the more rent.
.Tust so ! The landowner grabs the unearned
increment as usual. But a progressive tax on land
values would restore some at least of it to the
people.
Mr, Justice Higgins smote the Victorian
Wages Boards a heavy blow last week. When his
Honor denounced six shillings a day as a starva-
tion wage, counsel immediately interrupted that
some of the Wages Boards had fixed minimum
rates of pay for persons above 21 years of age at
from 30s. to 365. per week. The Judge’s reply was
a crusher: “ Wages Boards are not told to define
fair and reasonable conditions. lam!” And the
indictment is true.
The lot of the livery stable employee in
Melbourne is not a cheerful one. A statement
has been presented to the Government showing
that some of these men work from 16 to 20 hours
a day. One man is said to have put in 112 hours
in one week, which, at seven days a week, works
out at 16 hours a day. There is talk of a Wages
Board for the calling.
A recent House of Commons’ report on
shops and factories employment pathetically
states that, the keenness of modern industrial
competition demanding absolute cheapness in
production, the growing tendency in Great
Britain, where Freetrader so beloved a thing is
for the mother to oust the father as the family
breadwinner. Under these savage conditions
examples are cited where the family poverty com-
pelled mothers to return to the factories two or
three days after child-birth.
Local weepers over “Government interfer-
ence with Private Enterprise’' should cast a
thoughtful eye on Italy. Manv of the stone-broke
ancient families try to replenish the larder by
disposing of the surplus ancestor as painted by
an ‘‘old master.” The Government forbids
the export of ancient works of art, but
kindly offers, as a last resource, to take
them over at its own price. This is
generally about a quarter (or less) of
what a Yankee millionaire would
gladly pay, but the owner has either
to accept, or keep the picture to mind
the premises while he goes out and looks
for the indignity of work. The Govern-
ment gives itself power to search any-
body’s premises and catalogue his ancient
works of art, and if it comes again and
finds that some are missing it wants to
know where they have gone. Some time
ago, one family sold a masterpiece secretly
for £14,000, and the purchaser smuggled it
out of the country. Whereupon, the seller
was dragged to Court and fined exactly £14,000 !
That Tory woman’s convention has broken
the record for disgraceful lying. The S. M.
Herald of last Wednesday reports quite gravely :
The deportation of the Kanaka has, they said thrown
many hardships upon the women and children of the
North. The white man, who has partly filled the place of
the kanaka, is a menace to society, and no woman in
places where he is employed dares go out after dark.
The husbands of the women responsible for
this wicked slander on White Australians really
should lock them up, or smack them soundly. If
men talked like that in decent company, they’d
certainly be horsewhipped.

Petrie Watson, in “ The Future of Japan,”
points out that the circumstances helping the
erection of the Japanese navy are just the circum-
stances that will help Australia to build a navy
able to oppose the other navy :
The Japanese reform leaders were assisted rather than
embarrassed by the circumstance that there was neither
an army nor a navy when the powers of State came into
their hands. . . . They were able to begin at the be<dn-
mng ; almost unhampered by tradition, they were able to
fashion the country’s fighting machine, and to equip it
hi harmony with the surest principles deduced from
European experience.
The Age comments scathingly on the
strenuous efforts of the Conservatives to protect
Trusts and combines, foreign and local, from hos-
tile legislation, and expresses wonder at this atti-
tude of the Tories. But why should David be sur-
prised? Was Conservatism ever particular how
it robbed the people ? Has it ever failed to fight
strenuously for the preservation of ways and
means of robbing the people? Did it ever care
what company or companies it kept in the work
of spoliation ? Did it ever, even in its most
benevolent moments, cease to regard the masses
as the legitimate prey of the classes? Victorian
Conservatives battled for “thrift” a few years
ago because you can’t rob the improvident man he
having nothing to steal. They battle for Trusts
because even roguery is “cornered” nowadays
and a I rust is a rogues’ corner.

P. W. Crowe, of Brisbane,
who has been writing some things
for the local Courier, travelled
up from Gladstone to Townsville
with a man who, judging by his
complexion, he took to be one of
the new English immigrants.
Wanting to do the amiable, and
welcome the stranger, he got into
holts, and then ascertained that
the pink-cheeked, fleshy immi-
grant, presumably from Somerset
or Devon, had been for the
past 14 years farming in one
of the North Queensland dis-
tricts where “ the white man
can’t live.”
“Agricola Be “ San
Remo ” (B. 10/10/’O7) and
the Imported Shoddy Hat
Curse. A couple of
weeks ago I was dis-
cussing the tariff with
our storekeeper. Taking
me to his stock room he
displayed a consign-
ment of hats justarrived
from Italy. To all ap-
pearances they were a
first-rate article, but
our local Anthony
Hordern smiled when
he spoke of the effect
of a shower of rain on
those hats. They were
not made of felt at all,
but of wool prepared
in some manner that
made it look like felt.
We calculated that the
freight and duty handicapped those hats to the
extent of 57 per cent. Then he showed ine his in-
voices, and proved that they cost 8 per cent, less
to land here than the price of the Sydney-made
hat. The invoiced price of that imported head-
gear was los. 6d. per dozen. Obviously they were
the result of sweated labor somewhere, and the
only effective duty against this sort of rubbish is
about 300 per cent, ad valorem , or ss. per hat, or
total exclusion.

Actor Macready to Frederick Pollock, in
1857:
I hope you have seen the account of the working of the
ballot in Australia. It has always been a persuasion of
mine that we shall learn the practical lessons of political
economy from our offshoots—from those who are not
afraid to experimentalise, and who have no clogs of
antique prejudice on their movements.
But. alas ! some of the clogs and the antique
prejudices and a whole lot of the rabbits were im-
ported, so things are not all they might be.
“ Michael Carr ” about boots :
“Stony” (B. 26/9/07), in referring to his Victorian-
made boots touches on p rimps the most progressive
industry built up by the late Victorian tariff. Twenty-
five years ago hardly any lines were made in Victoria
excepting what is known in the trade as “ strong work ”
that is, pegged and riveted kip-work for men’s work-
ing boots, and women’s and children’s strong boots. Now
every variety, of the finest materials, is manufactured.
Protection induced the strong-work manufacturers to
taite on light work, and in time each manufacturer
specialised his particular make, till this market is now
supplied at most reasonable prices with goods that would
be a credit to the oldest manufacturing countries.
CANADA AND JAPAN.
Japan is pleased with the attitude of the Canadian
authorities regarding the anti-Japanese outrages, but op-
poses the convention restricting the movements of
Japanese laborers. —TELEGRAPH.
THE JAP : “ Well, I must say that's a nice
respectful attitude, but just more your feet
away from that hole in the wall."
Aunty Sosh in Melbourne resolved that—
While this conference approves of the principle of
White Australia from the racial point of view, it con-
siders the importation of colored labor necessary for the
development of the Tropical Territory.
In other words, Aunty Sosh believes in keeping
the race pure, but the purse heavy ; and, on con-
sideration, it decides that the purse is of more
consequence than the race. Presumably, Aunty
wouldn’t mind marrying a Kanaka if he had
£5OOO a year—that, at any rate, is her own
argument.
The reason Australia has heard so little or
Anti-Sosh of late is now revealed. The Scarecrow
has gone to Britain. And, according to the cables,
Conservatives and Liberals are uniting to destroy
Socialism, and 70 societies have been formed to
combat it. The average Australian with the civic
records of Glasgow, Birmingham, Leeds and
London before him, was under the impression
that Britain understood Socialism fairly well, and
had got it so tamed down that it would feed out
of the hand. It was so till quite recently, and then
a doolc, or somethin’ like that, awoke, yawned,
sprang to his feet with a yell about robbery, spoli-
ation, and the break-up of the family. The papers
found that Socialism meant starvation and
national bankruptcy, because under it the country
wouldn’t produce enough food for its population,
and advocated that the two great parties should
refer the fiscal question to a Royal Commission,
and unite to destroy Socialism. Evidently Anti-
Sosh is something like measles or influenza—very
catching.
HE FELL IN.
MERCHANT: “ Are your habits all right?”
APPLICANT FOR POSITION: “ Yes, sir.”
MERCHANT (after a pause): “Do you drink?”
APPLICANT (absently): "Thanks, don't care if
I do.”
Last week when Justice Higgins, in the
Australian Arbitration Court, asked the repre-
sentative of Hugh McKay what he considered
should be the rate per day for unskilled labor,
Mr. Schutt promptly replied six shillings. Mr. Jus-
tice Higgins replied that he was sorry to hear it. So
is The Bulletin ; and so must every Australian
patriot be. What earthly chance has the laborer of
marrying and rearing a strong, vigorous family on
six shillings per day? The thing is impossible!
An industry must pay better wages than that to
be worth having. As Mr. Justice Higgins said,
men receiving six shillings per day are always
just on the brink of starvation. And a country
that keeps its working classes just on the brink
of starvation is always just on the brink of ruin.

The Lion called a meeting- of his brother beasts of Prey—
“’Tis time,” lie said, “we animals put wicked War
away.
And in response the Tiger came in state ; and from the
Nile
(A peaceful smile about his jaws) there came the Croco-
• due.
The Wolf, the Fox, the Jaguar—they likewise mustered
And from his rugged, rocky haunts there came the
Grizzly Bear ;
The anear' a £ lode in hooded state—the Eagle swooped
The hungry Jackal dogged his King with many a cun-
ning leer.
This meeting’s called,” the Lion said, “to help the
Cause of Peace, p
That bloody strife may leave the land, and wicked War
may cease ;
O, brethren mine, give heed to me, and bid your passions
pause,
And let the Surgeon take his tools and draw your teeth
and claws.
“I cannot let him nip my nails—my health will not
allow ;
Btttl wUI always keep them sheathed in velvet soft—as
And as to teeth—the ones I wear are little more than
sham ;
They’d never hurt a sucking-pig, or prick a baby lamb.”
The Tiger said—“ I’m all for Peace—l hate the sight of
Blood ; h
But draw my teeth ! ’ —l promised Ma I never, never
would ! ”
The Jackal and the Jaguar they wept their eyelids raw,
But said that Peace was best maintained by them that
wore a claw.
The Cobra swore his venom-bag was but a sac of bile ;
“My jaws are just for ornament! ” exclaimed the Croco-
dile ;
The Eagle cried—“ My curling beak must always go with
me &
That I may guard from Wicked Birds my little Familee.’’
And then they sang together—“ What a cruel thing is
War ! °
It makes our little bosoms lust for other creatures’ gore ;
But we must hang- to every fang till other creatures cease
To cart around such horrid things and break the blessed
Peace ;
For, sure as Fate, there’ll be no end to ravin foul and red,
Lnless we keep our claws on hand and teeth within our
head ! ”

NOTIONS
Apologue ended the long discussion as to
the merits of the stable companions bv winning’
the Melbourne Cup, with Mooltan and Mountain King
second and third respectively. He won with7st. 91b up
while Mooltan carried an extra couple of pounds and
Mountain King 31b. less. As far as is possible to judge
the result is a great and overwhelming victory for Ikev
O’Flaherty, who used to be known as Christian Moses.
* * * *
Saturday’s racing at Flemington was unusually
tame for a Derby day, and it left the public with no new
subject of disputation for the Melbourne Cup. Not that
Mountain King didn’t make friends by his apparently
easy win in the Derby. There were some who thought
the big son of Wallace could have won in record
time if he had been pushed. On the other hand
it was argued that a Derby run in 23sec’
slower time than was credited to little F.J.A. four
years ago did not put the staying power of Mountain
King to the test. A finely-proportioned giant, with anv
amount of breathing-room, the “King” is perhaps the
most awkward galloper (viewed from the rear) on the list
of Derby winners. To win the Cup, it was evident he
would need to have more stamina than nags of the
Poseidon stamp, who make no trouble of getting over the
ground. The difficulty with Mountain King 011”, Saturday
was to straighten him up for an effort, but as soon as
Hickey had got the colt in hand, he “ larruped ” along
in commanding style.
Nevertheless, there was still the natural sus-
picion that he might have “gone to pieces ” in a bump-
ing race if the opposition had been stronger The
favorite was neither bumped nor bustled. His most
likely opponent, Welcome Trist, came with a belated
run, and finished at the neck of Peru, who was a length
behind the favorite, but the effort was too late to give
the winning jockey any concern. So the doubt as to
Mountain King’s stamina remained to be settled in the
Cup.
Melbourne Stakes has foreshadowed the Cup
result in many previous years. This year the stable com-
panions, Poseidon and Apologue, had the finish to them-
selves, with the “ champion ” winning as easily as was
expected, considering- that he was meeting Apologue on
351 b. better than their Cup terms. As for Tartan the
old hero gave no comfort to his backers. He appeared
to be outpaced all the time. But on this occasion
Apologue certainly showed in front at the turn for home
which caused punters to remark that he had never
carried his 30-odd pounds lighter weight in front of
Poseidon in the Caulfield Cup race.

THE MALIGNING OF MALONEY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Decent fellow, Dan Maloney
(To a cobber and a crony),
With a tendency to women, wine and horses !
But he’d recently been fretting
None the better of the betting,
And was fairly at the end of his resources.
Not a Chinaman had struck
Such a rotten run o’ luck ;
Every “ certainty ’’had finished, with the rotters, in the
ruck.
They had broken Dan Maloney ;
Made him absolutely “ stony ”;
And he cursed the day he ever saw the courses.
Yet, though luck be most infernal,
There’s a “ hope that springs eternal,”
And that fills the punter’s bosom with elation.
He was broken altogether,
But began to wonder whether
He could possibly relieve the situation.
He was fairly stony-broke,
And it wasn’t any joke
If he couldn’t chance to hit upon a bright and happv
stroke; l
But it came, and seemed supernal,
As he read The Punters’ Journal,
And related to a Eagsall Consultation.
He decided, for a start, he
Would contrive to form a party
That would speculate in fashion systematic ;
Pool a tenner or a pony,
And then leave it to Maloney,
Who’d the wisdom of the wily Asiatic !
He considered it was best
(That is what he told the rest)
In the smaller consultations, not the larger, to invest.
The approval of the party—
Or the syndicate—was hearty ;
And its confidence decidedly emphatic 1
When the members had invested
As Malonej' had suggested
He was good enough to let ’em know the numbers ;
But he didn’t show the tickets.
If they thought it was a trick, it’s
Pretty sure it didn’t hurt Maloney’s slumbers !
Let me tell you with regret
Which I’m not quite over yet
That Maloney used the money of the syndicate to bet.
And the cash was not invested
In the way he had suggested,
And he’d given mere imaginary numbers 1
’Tis the habit of the Devil
When he lures us on to evil
And from paths of righteous rectitude would turn us
To make sinfulness look easy !
Very slippery and greasy
Is the sloping path descending to Avernus !
And Maloney by his guile
Carried on in splendid style,
But at last a bogus number was the same that won the
pile !
And the Club went off their level
In a wild unholy revel —
They were joyous, very joyous, for a very little while !
Not a cobber nor a crony
E’er again saw Dan Maloney !
He vanished like the mist before the morning!
And his name they are maligning
As a villain most designing,
And his memory his fellows join in scorning !
They believe, of course, that Dan
Has become a wealthy man ;
That the published ticket-holder was a party to the plan !
But they all malign Maloney,
For he once again is “ stony,”
Not, as they suppose, some foreign Court adorning !
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
BROOK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
And so, it seems, the burbling Brook
The sinful dust of cycle racing
Prom off his pedal-pushers shook,
And takes instead to Bethel-gracing.
To higher things does he aspire
Than cycling, with its perils dire.
When mighty winners give a pledge
That on the track temptations revel,
Perdition must the losers hedge,
For with the hindmost sprints the Devil!
But, though his help they most require,
Brook looks—and leaves them in the mire.
N.S.W.

THE ORIGIN OF A NAME.
A CURIOUS FACT IN NATURAL
HISTORY.
One of our contemporaries brought, a few days
ago, a notice of a very interesting kind in his
columns as to how names are discovered. In con-
sequence of more exact information which we
were fortunate enough to obtain from a most
reliable source in London, we are able to declare
that the name Odol is not derived from the
formation of the craters in the moon, but has been
seen for the first time on the skin of a tiger in the
jungles of British India.
An eminent naturalist has drawn attention to
this very peculiar feature in the markings upon
the tiger’s fur, which affords another remarkable
evidence of the proverb that there is “ nothing
new under the sun.” While scientists were busy
with the important investigations which resulted
in the discovery of the famous dentrifice, Odol,
and centuries before the name was thought of, it
would appear that tigers were roaming the
jungles of India bearing upon their coats the
impress of the very word. That this powerful
and beautiful habitant of tropical forests —per-
haps the animal of all others with the best dental
equipment—should be, as it were, the actual fore-
runner of Odol, is one of those coincidences which
are nothing short of marvellous. It provides
food for thought, and Odol is fairly entitled to
whatever advantage may accrue from the dis*
covery of the coincidence.

STARTLING DISCOVERY of the CAUSE of
APPENDICITIS !
According to an authoritative report it is stated that Dr. Blanchard
has placed before the world his startling discovery as to the cause of
Appendicitis. It is asserted, moreover, that his opinion is endorsed by
Professor Metchnikoff, the celebrated scientist of the Pasteur Institute.
These eminent research workers, the report states, have discovered certain
parasites in the appendix of every case which they have examined. It is
further stated that there are three kinds of these parasites or intestinal
worms, the most dangerous of which is known as the trygocephal, and it is
this which causes the acute pain. Appendicitis is neither contagious nor
infectious, but it frequently becomes epidemic in character, more particu-
larly in hot weather. According to Professor Blanchard, the common vege-
tables of everyday use may contain parasites which set up Appendicitis.
Fortunately, however, there is a certain preventive of this dire scourge,
Appendicitis, which, if regularly adopted, will render us immune against its
attacks. This remedy is Kutnow’s Powder, which, when taken before
breakfast in a glass of water, quickly removes all parasites, foreign bodies,
and poisonous matter. It cleanses the entire system and gives renewed
activity to the digestive organs so that any waste matter instead of remain-
ing in the body and forming an irritant poison is regularly removed each day.

The police authorities in N.S.W. seem to
be possessed with a fierce desire to prosecute
everybody possible under the Secondhand
Dealers Act, recently passed. This Act was
avowedly passed to grapple with the bottle-o and
“ dealer ” of his class, who were more than sus-
pected of sneak-thieving. But the last effort of
the police was to arrest a country cycle-seller
under the Act, his crime being that he accepted an
old bike from a customer in part payment for a new
one, furbished up the old one, and sold it again!
A foolish magistrate convicted him. The bicycle
trade took the matter up, and appealed against
the decision. The Crown Law people fought
strenuously for the conviction, but the Judge had
no hesitation in quashing the verdict. Supposing
the Crown Law Office to be genuine in thinking
that an offence had been committed, why did the
police pick on a luckless up-country trader for a
prosecution, and put all parties to the expense of
coming to Sydney for the appeal ? All the big
musical establishments in the metropolis call
loudly every day in their advertisements for old
instruments in part payment for new ones. The
practice of part payment is common in many
lines —pianos, sewing machines, phonographs, and
bicycles are all openly trafficked in by the biggest
people. Why, therefore, did the police go to
Molong for a case ?
The guileless Chinaman continues to
smuggle opium into North Australia, and finds
the business pays, despite occasional fines and
confiscations. At the Cairns (Q.) Court recently,
Lun Pan pleaded guilty to illegally having opium
in his possession. The Chow, dressed as though
he were going to the Cup, and carrying a port-
manteau, had been noticed constantly travelling
to and from the coast. An inquisitive policeman
looked inside the portmanteau, and, instead of
pyjamas and socks, discovered opium. Lun
Pan’s bank-book was also in the bag, and it
showed that he had paid in £400 since the begin-
ning of the year. The P.M. remarked £50 or six
months. Lun Pan, without a murmur, seated
himself at the Court table and scribbled a cheque
for the amount. Minus his parcel of opium, and
with only £350 in the bank, the Chinaman left
the court, and is probably now busy studying out
a new method of carrying on the old business.
N.S.W. Local Government Act, which
comes into full force in January, has struck a
hard, and presumably uncontemplated, blow at
the far-seeing husband who makes the family
humpy over to the “ole ’oman.” In terms of the
Act the present municipalities fall down dead
next year, and a voter or candidate for the new
councils must be “an owner, occupier, or lodger.”
A man who lives with his wife in his wife’s house
isn’t any of these things; and, in consequence,
quite a number of aldermen are preparing for the
Simple Life without ’possum robes. Among
them report places the Mayor and three aldermen
of one Sydney suburb, the Mayor of another,
and sundry other aldermen in divers places. Mrs.
Alderman, it seems, is quite a numerous house-
owner. Sydney solicitors, also, are finding the new
Local Government Act something of a snag. It
makes elaborate (and much-needed) rules as to the
cutting up of estates for building allotments.
Plans of the streets, etc., must be approved by
the_ local authority. In one or two cases of sub-
divisions, where the fact of the new law s existence
was overlooked, there has been trouble.
A steam launch recently blew up at Cir-
cular Quay, Sydney, with startling suddenness.
A theory was put forward that some explosive had
found its way from the coal-mine into the bunkers
of the steamer. This seemed a far-fetched notion,
but it received startling support when, during
last week, the steamer Dilkera reached Albany,
(W.A.), with a coal cargo from Port Kembla
(N.S.W.). While discharging at the Westralian
port, a trimmer dug up a shovelful of dynamite.
Altogether, 14 plugs'were found.

The stimulus imparted to the body is most
pleasant, soothing, and health-giving. It removes
