The Bulletin Post Cards
NEW SERIES, IV., V., VI.
FORWARDED POST PREE TO ANT ADDRESS. Packet of TWELVE, Is. Packet of TEN, lOd
The Inky Way.
There are seven men in the Queensland Government
Printing- Office who have been there 40 years or more,
Frederick Spence being top score with 45. The gods may
die young ; but all the devils don’t.
* * * *
Maoriland has two long-service reporters. George R.
Hart, leader of the reporting staff of Christchurch Weekly
Press, completed 37 years’ service on that paper last
month (October); and John R. Gibbons passed the 25
years’ graft-stone on the Wellington Evening Post during
September.
* * * *
Victoria’s new Gaming Act doesn’t allow the papers to
report ante-post betting, but the little ’Erald worked its
dodge by remarking, re Caulfield Cup, that “ Ike Earn-
shaw is training the two first favorites, Apologue and
Poseidon; Ebullition is a strong public fancy, while
lolaire has many friends,” etc. It named the whole of
the candidates in this fashion, and then wound up by re-
marking—“ The order in which the horses stand in public
favor is that in which we have referred to them.”
* * * *■
The S. A. Register runs a literary page in its Saturday
issue. This is mostly filled with snips, but there is a
sprinkling of original matter. An aspiring poet sent the
old paper some decent verse, which was printed in this
famous page ; but in reply to a request for payment he
was informed that, though the Register was always
willing to encourage amateur writers by printing their
efforts if the same were meritorious, there was no pay-
ment in such cases. And yet, if a man encourages the
landlord by taking his house (it being a meritorious domi-
cile) without payment, a bailiff is put in.
* * * *
“ U.” : I feel inclined to ask The Bulletin to print my
items in full italics, so as to kind of balance things with
the other papers on my visiting list. There is a shocking
tendency nowadays to keep press utterances to a deadly
monotonous linotype level, with nary an inflection, as
far as the type is concerned. I expect to see capital
letters abolished soon, so as to save the trouble of reach-
ing out for them. Then we will be informed that “the
rev. henry worrall said beelzebub bossed bendigo,” and
“ mary lady smith addressed the Sydney women’s national
league in a stirring speech and a gown direct from paris.”
* * * «
The Dominion, the latest organ of Toryism, made its
first appearance at Wellington (M.L.) on Dominion Day.
The squattah has lately been having nasty dreams of
possibilities in the way of subdivision—“confiscation,”
he calls it—when Lands Minister McNab and Premier
Ward get their Land Bill and Graduated Tax going ; so he
has bought himself another newspaper. The Freetrade
Conservative doesn’t do things by halves when it is a
question of protecting himself, and the Dominion is
backed up with a capital of something in the vicinity of
£lOO,OOO. Nearly a year was spent in getting ready and
talking about the great show that was to be made. A
mighty staff (numerically) was engaged, purporting to
comprise all the best of Maoriland journalism. But when
the organ played its first tune it was discovered that the
mountain had brought forth a mouse, and not a very
robust mouse at that. The Dominion’s first article that
wasn’t about itself was an attack on the State-owned
railway service. Next day the paper gave up politics for
the time being and tried its youthful hand with a long
leader on astronomy. After working off three parts of a
column the writer became as befogged as his readers, and
gave up explaining the mysteries of the solar system in
order to ask questions. Thus :
“ Whence and whither is Arcturus speedingat 370 miles
a second? What energy propels him, or controls him?
Whither is the sun and his train of worlds speeding at
nineteen miles a second? He is heading for the bright
star, Vega, seen low in the north, but is his path straight?
Do other stars accompany him ? Is he one of a group ?
What is the meaning of the varied colors and spectra of
stars ? What are ‘ blaze stars ’ and what are variables ?
What is the genesis of a star ? Has collision anything to
do with it? Does a nebula condense into a sun?' Is
there a temperature at which atoms dissolve and all
elements are reduced to one? Do suns die by cooling?
And is ours dying, or is the Cosmos immortal ?’”
Everybody will agree that these are questions which
demand an immediate answer. But in the meantime, if
the sun is not going straight, what is the matter with
calling a meeting of the stewards, and having it dis-
qualified?
THE WAR-GOD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I met a burly editor,
And joy was in his eye.
He gazed upon me merrilee
The while he hurried by.
With cash his purse did overflow,
His hair was all a-curl ;
His lovely show of dentistry
Would shame a gate of pearl!
O ! tell me, Mister Editor,
Why is thy smile so gay ?
Thy raiment, too, so superfine—
So sweet thy new array ?
Hast tumbled on a ruby mine,
Or got Dorado’s gold,
Or found the stone philosophers
Did seek in days of old ?
He turned —the Golden Editor—
And laughed for very glee :
“ Dost thou not know the Buss is out
To slay the Japanee ?
And /—the gentle Journalist—
I plan their little game,
And tell them in the Daily Bag
How they must run the same ;
“ And people in this simple town,
From tinker up to toff,
They mostly think I run the show
For Jap and Bomanoff ;
And so they send a steady stream
Of coppers to my door,
And thank the Lord they own a man
Who’s fit to wage a war.
“ If Dalny town is up in flames,
Or Yalu’s running red,
I work the next manoeuvre out
While folks are all abed ;
And when we meet the morrow morn,
’Tis good to see them stare—
‘ Last night he cue a corps to bits
And never turned a hair.’ ”
“ If that be so—Sir Editor—
O, bid the war to cease !
Let Jap and Eussian join their hands
And rest again in peace ! ”
“ Not so —not so—Sir Craven Heart;
The Public longs for blood,
And now I couldn’t stop the war
No matter if I would 1 ”
For though the jolly Journalist,
May fan a spark to flame,
He hasn’t got the slightest power
To check the ruddy game ;
And when he’s found a fount of pelf
In public thirst for blood,
Why—as to stopping Wicked War,
Hu wouldn’t if he could !
He turned —the jolly Journalist—
And sought his jolly den ;
He drew his warlike scissors forth,
And wet his plucky pen ;
He sent the Czar the word along
To force ahead the fray,
For he was doomed to plot by night
That blood might run by day.
QUEENSLAND.

THE CLAVIOLA
Is the only Piano Player that can interpret all the works of the
great masters as written, it having a full compass of 72 NOTES.
This extended range does away with the necessity of mutilation required
for players with a shorter compass.
The Claviola Artist Music Rolls are absolutely unique and without
parallel —they are electric records, note by note, of the ACTUAL
PLAYING of the World’s Greatest Pianists, and are perfect renditions
of the classics.
Other important features of the Claviola are:
Special Levers for Phrasing and Melody Accentuation.
Brass Tubing and Leather Bellows throughout.
No Perishable Rubber used.
Our Library, comprising thousands of rolls of music, is available to
purchasers on payment of a small annual subscription.
' The Claviola can be bought on easy terms, and we issue a special
invitation to any lady or gentleman interested to call and play their
favorite composition —we promise them a revelation.

will not Crack, Scale or Blister. Used on Metal it con-
tracts and expands with the painted surface under all
atmospheric changes.
It is not affected by acids, alkalies or sulphurous
gases. It is impervious to water and effectually with-
stands the severest action of the elements. It is fireproof
and guaranteed to last for at least Five Years.

The British Trade Union Congress has
heard an explanation from a Salvation Army
officer of the charge that the Army pays joiners
in its workshops 12s. a week, and charges them
9s. a week for board. Answer was: —
The skilled workman comes to us as a place of refuge.
In many cases he comes without heart,. stomach, or
stamina, a skeleton of his former trade-union days. In
time he earns, perhaps, more than he receives, but the
surplus goes to assist the man at his side, who is a loss
to us.
Then, having sweated the good workman to make
him pay for the one without a stomach, the Army
sends round the hat to get money for doing the
same thing. In other words, it sweats the work-
man and the public for the same purpose, and it
is about time the Army explained where the
second lot of sweated money goes.
“ Silica,” from Kidston’s country :
If the future Japanese historian or publicist, securely
domiciled in Australia and protected by the bayonets of
the Japanese warriors, should write learnedly re the in-
fluence of low tariffs on the decline and fall of Australia,
it is to be hoped he will devote a chapter to the multi-
tudinous, selfish little insects who undermined the tariff
wall. Take the case of Queensland. Here is a State
so thoroughly nurtured and educated to casual Pro-
tection that it has forgotten that Oobden ever ranted, or
the Manchester League everpamphleted. All it recollects
of alleged Preetrade in N. S. Wales is a “ Hop” portrait
of a globular person and his dog. It laughed consutnedly
at the dog', and wrote letters to The Bulletin asking par-
ticulars of the breed, and inquiring if Reid kept it chained
to the barristers’ table in the Supreme Court. It blinked
sleepily when the old, ineffective Federal tariff wiped out
the fairly efficient old Queensland tariff, and made wild
and crudely ignorant guesses at the reasons why the Ips-
wich Woollen Mills, and Walkers, Ltd., Maryborough, were
steadily declining. In short, Queensland was, and is,
wretchedly ignorant of the principles of fiscalism. It sub-
scribed itself to Protection, much in the same way as
Billjim writes himself a “ Roming Carflick ” in the census
return—because it has a dim recollection of having been
brought up in that faith. Then the new Australian tariff
happened along, and offered prosperity, good wages, and
protection against foreign invasion to Queensland. But
the fiscally illiterate State is suspicious, and the deadly
work against the Australian tariff is being done, not by
the bold and piratical importer (there are few of him in
Queensland), but by the ignorant little insect all over the
State. The temporary rise in prices (inevitable till the
new industries get going, and carried to needless lengths
by the huckstering insect) is bitterly resented 'by the
buying insect. The latter, in the depths of his ignorance,
mouths blasphemy against Federation and the Federal
Parliament —against everybody, in fact, but his fellow
creeping thing. He doesn’t understand; he doesn’t
reason ; he is ready to sell Australia to the Asiatic and
himself to the Foreign-trader, because he pays sjd. more
out of a sweated wage than he did before. Therefore, I
trust, when the Japanese historian aforesaid sits down to
his task, he will devote the eleventh volume of his work,
with an illuminating preface and a voluminous index, to
the creeping thing in Queensland, who will then be hew-
ing wood and drawing water for the Asiatic garrison.

The Case of an Upright Judge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To-day at Samarai I met a score of miners of the
Gira and the Yodda. We knew old Westralian
rushes, and talked early Coolgardie and the Zeehan
that went before the West, and the Broken Hill
that went before Tassy.
The miners of Papua have headed a fat sub-
scription list for the building of a memorial to
Judge Christopher Robinson, a good man if a
hasty, and a fine Australian. Courage and sin-
cerity most appeal to a man, and so within a
month or two the strand at Samarai will bear the
monument to honesty and misfortune.
Back of the Mission at Port Moresby is a grave
without a sign—a mound of baked earth, and of
perished grasses, sapless as dead hair. In the
grave is the last of Christopher Robinson, Judge
and some-time Governor. The baked earth and
the dead grass cover the memory of a crime, and
the crime was not his who lies below.
On Bth April, 1001, two missionaries of the
London Mission JSuciety, named Chalmers and
lompkins, foolishly ventured on the island of
Goaribari, at the head of the Papuan Gulf,
despite the fact that on the previous day the
natives had insulted them on their own ship,
u ith a Papuan an insult never stops at an insult.
Generally speaking, if he is not quite respectful
and quite friendly to a stranger, then that
stranger he means to kill. Chalmers and Tomp-
kins went ashore in the face of all warning and at
the sacrifice of all prudence, and Chalmers and
lompkins were duly killed, with their nine native
followers, and the eleven bodies were cooked with
sago and eaten.
Goaribari is an island of 3500 inhabitants, all
cruel cannibals and men of war. On the main-
land and around the deltas are thousands of fight-
ing men, cannibals all, The punishment of
natives for a crime against a white mati is more
tor the safety of white men who come after than
in revenge of the actual death. Governor Le
JJunte arrived at Goaribari on a punitive expedi-
tion on 2nd May. The natives shot arrows at the
party; an answering volley killed some natives,
estimated on the spot to be 34, but afterwards
found to be six, on the evidence of the natives
themselves. This spirit of exaggeration was to
indirectly cause the death of Christopher Robin-
son. Le Hunte burned the dubus—long houses for
warriors and destroyed 120 canoes, and then he
sailed away. The nor’-west monsoon intervened,
and no further communication with the murder-
ing tribes was possible until the following year,
ihen a second visit was made for the purpose of
securing the actual murderers and the skulls of the
murdered. Le Hunte had told the chiefs that
the first time he came in peace, but
that next time he should come in war—
that sooner or later the murderers must be given
up, and that he demanded the immediate sur-
lender of the white men’s skulls. The possession
of a white man’s skull gives high prestige to a
A apuan. Ihe delivery of the skulls was a great
blow to the cannibals’ pride, but at last they gave
up Chalmers’ skull, though not the other. Le
AAunte concludes the journal of this expedition
. A S m~ far, then, as the villages of Dopima
and Aurotere are concerned, they have an un-
purged offence yet to account for.”
The London Mission Society had its usual im-
pudent interference with the course of justice,
a mg from London to the Government to ask
for leniency to the murderers. For the protec-
ho,L°! H vm £’ the matter of the dead men
to the law ; and the Mission cable was
an unwarrantable attempt to modify the law.
Governor Le Hunte, then, threatened to return
in war; and although he left the Possession before
out >. aric l never returned to Goaribari,
his intention and his threat on record for
f it-il benefit 0f hls success or. The legacy was
In dune, 1903, Chief Justice Robinsott Was
appointed Acting-Administrator, but too late in the
monsoon season to proceed to Goaribari that year.
On sth March, 1904, he arrived in the Merrie
-England at Goaribari. He was attended by Mr
Bruce, Commandant of Police j Mr, Jiear, the
Resident Magistrate of the local division: Mr.
Jewell, his acting secretary; and 39 native police,
armed, as usual, with rifles. Mr. Jiear Was
given charge of the arrangements for the
arrest of the murderers, as being the magis-
trate of the division, Against the usual
procedure, Judge Robinson decided to entice
the men on to the Merrie England, and to take
them prisoners. One “ usual ” plan is to give the
native twenty-four hours’ notice of intention to
arrest, and to be exceedingly surprised and
grieved when the native fails to get hitnself
arrested atthe time appointed. The better
method is to boldly surround a village, baring
nothing for numbers, and so bluffing the blacks
mto surrender, Acting on the white-police
method his lawyer’s training would naturally
suggest, the Judge, who was determined to finish
the work his predecessor had initiated, got the
natives aboard the ship, and arrested one of the
actual murderers and ten semi-prinbipals, Ho hie
ot the 1 apuan police had a private feud to
avenge, and When the hundreds of natives arouhd
and on the ship drew their boWs to shoot, a black
policeman fired and killed his than, who was aim-
ing at Captain Harvey, Commander of the Merrie
England. The officers and Judge Robinson then
ln the shooting and killed sOrrtC natives,
lhe natives immediately retreated and the firing
stopped. It had lasted Only a feW rrtiniites.
I he Judge certainly lost his head-, hilt he lbst it
bravely. As Governor, he might have directed
operations from his cabin ; but he insisted on per-
s?,na' leadership, and chivalrously took all respon-
sibility, before and after the event. The number
of killed was only eight, but hysteria made it first
200 and then 80. The excitement lasted some
days, and when calm came again the mischief was
complete.
The Merrie England proceeded to Hall Sound,
and found there the London Missionary Society
steamer, the John Williams. The Rev. 0. Abel
was on board. The Rev. 0. Abel is a missionary
of great commercial ability, as evidenced by his
mission saw-mill and boat-building ventures at
Kwato. On the 31st December, 1903, at Samarai,
i e had tried a South Sea teacher of the Lon-
don Missionary Society for perjury, arising out of
a charge preferred against him of intimacy with a
native s wife. The Judge found the mission
teacher, guilty, and sentenced him to twelve
months hard labor. The missionaries complained
bitterly of the sentence, and because of it frankly
disliked the Judge.
Rumor waxed shrill over the shooting, and the
Rev. C. Abel got a hearsay account of the affair.
.Then the Lev. C. Abel went to Australia, whither
Captain Harvey had preceded him, and on 22nd

Meantime the little bureaucracy that wished to
rid itself of Judge Robinson as Administrator, but
was incapable of using such means as these to gain
its ends, Watched with something horribly like
complacency the turn given to affairs.
Judge Robinson, already weakened by fever
practically accused of murder, a sulky officialism
around him, feeling every man’s hand against
him, knowing by his knowledge of at least one of
his subordinates the evidence some of them were
capable of giving, took the way of the faint-
hearted, and shot himself. He had acted fear-
lessly in his attempts to improve the public
service, and with more than his usual energy at
(Joaribari, because two years had elapsed since
Governor Be Hunte had promised punishment.
But Bobinson know that a promise—even a wrong
promise of punishment—to a tlative mast hfcifc bt
broken if the native’s respebt for the white ttuth
is to be preserved.

RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
This prayer, which the £ig Landlord has
strangely forgotten, appeared in the sixth
Edward’s second liturgy of 1552
We heartily pray Thee to send Thy Holy Spirit into the
hearts of them that possess the grounds and pastures df
the earth; that they, remembering themselves to be Thy
tenants, may rtot rack Or Stretch out the rents of -theft
houses Or iatids, nor yet take unreasonable fines or moneVs;
after the manrier of cOv'etous worldlings but so let
them out that the inhabitants thereof may be able to pay
the rents and to ivri arid assist their families, and remem-
ber the poor. Give them grace also to consider that they
are but strangers and pilgrims in this world, having here
no dwelling-place, but seeking one to come; that'they
remembering the short continuance of this life, may be
content with that which is sufficient, and not join house
to house and land to land to the impoverishment of
others, but so behave themselves in letting their lands
tenements and pastures that after this life they may be
leceived into everlasting habitation. J

The Secret.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
INHERE were two men who had slain a man,
And hidden the corpse away ;
They left no trace of its hiding place
Disclosed to the light of day.
His bones were cunningly hid away
By a shrewd and a skilful plan.
Each knew that alone the secret lay
In the heart of the other man.
One was a bold and reckless wight;
And one was a slinking cur
Who sweated his fears in the dead of night,
And longed for the morning’s stir.
But time went on, and the world forgot;
Men put the tale aside ;
The corpse rose not from its lonely grot,
And the nine-days’ wonder died.
Nor came there ghost or spectre pale
As the course of the seasons ran ;
Each grew aware he had but to share
The fact with the other man.
The qther man ! But would he be true,
And hold to the secret fast ?
Though safe with one, was it safe with 1
Would he yield to some voice at last ?
So asked in the night the craven’s heart,
And ever he looked askance ;
And, urged and led by a growing dread,
He waited his evil chance.
At last it came. In a lonely place,
Where a deep, swift river ran,
From a hidden nook, with a hand that shook,
He shot the other man.
Down in the waters went the corpse
With a weight to the ankles slung;
And only with him was the secret grim—
He had stilled the other tongue 1
And time went on, and the world forgot,
And the nine-days’ wonder passed;
For out of the deeps it lifted not,
But held to its secret fast.
There’s a bent old man with a haggard face,
His glance has a furtive leer;
From the tail of his eye when he’s slinking by
There’s the glint of a craven fear;
Though he knows his secret’s safe in his breast,
And the story’s laid on the shelf,
Shall he forget what the world forgets ?
He asks himself in his grim night-sweats:
Shall a man be sure of himself i
VIC.
A.B.C.

NICHOLAS DON AND THE MEEK ALMIRA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
NICHOLAS DON was a gay deceiver.
His attitude towards womankind was con-
sistently frivolous, and his opinion of the
sex was small but indulgent. “ They’re all
erlike,” he said, and it must be admitted that his
experience was extensive. He devoted the greater
part of his leisure to the pursuit of it, and his
audacity in “wording” cold, proud young per-
sons in the grand march past in the city on Sun-
day evenings, and his success in attaching himself
to girls in whom his first advances had seemed to
provoke unutterable loathing and contempt were
the wonder, the admiration, and the envy of
comparatively timid spirits like Benno and the
printers.
On these occasions the Don always indulged a
harmless and pleasing imposition, and presented
himself to the little lady of his fancy—or the little
ladies, for Nicholas did not hesitate in the face of
numbers—as a person of consequence. He
adopted the name of some singer or actor con-
spicuous for the moment, some well-known man
about town, a titled visitor, or a local celebrity of
great wealth, and he acted the part. Young
devils from the factories and warehouses generally
assume fictitious names when plunging into adven-
tures of this kind.
Mr. Nicholas Don did not always expect the
young lady to be deceived when he introduced
himself as Lord Savens or Walter Baker. Often
the fiction was accepted with open glee, as the
mere manifestation of a cheerful spirit. But if
the damsel were sufficiently ingenuous, and seemed
prepared to take Nicholas at his word, the carter
presumed upon her innocent, trusting nature.
Then the eye-glass appeared, the drawl became
very, marked, and the toothpick was gracefully
manipulated. The Don had carried through
many a successful impersonation of a wicked
young English aristocrat with these properties
the quill toothpick. Nicholas’s
iaith in a toothpick was profound, “Er bloke
with er bit iv say ’n’ er toothpick kin trade him-
self off ez er haide-de-kong ’r somethin’jist ez
large ez life, though he’s wearin’ trowsis that look
. he’d bin tryin’t’ blast ’em off with dinnymite,”
said the Don.
Spats’ carter admitted that his attention was
first drawn to the meek Almira by an advertise-
ment in the “Missing Friends” column of a
morning paper. Therein Almira described her-
self as a young widow and a lonely soul, and
craved the sympathy of “ middle-aged gentleman
similarly situated.”
‘ Thinks I, ‘ Wha’s ther matter with me ? ’
said the Don, long after it was all over. “I
T>n j m *ddle-aged, but I’m er symperthetic soul.
11 do er duck in, ’n’ mingle tears with Almira.
1 put on me bes’ pretties, ’n’ took me brass ring all
covered with di’monds, ’n’ me card-case, ’n’ went
orth ez ther Hon. Horace Badminton-Carte t’
yearn ’long with me Almira by happointment.
■trust me when I say she wiz jist ther best piece,
tr llttle f at widder risin’ 30, er sad, sentimental,
ush-a-bye kind, but with er savin’ bias towards
n ' s * ;ou * : " She *-°^ e me ’ er ole pot-’ri’-pan
ed dodged under ther daisies through sittin’ out-
side , ther draught in ’otel bars year in ’n’ year
out, n took me to ’er ’umble ’ome, where she
earned a onest livin’, givin’ man ’n’ his young
boxm lessons on ther pianner. I sighed over ’er
unhappy lot, ’n’ she tumbled that we was symper-
thetic souls orl right.
Iher Hon. Horace Badminton-Carte was er
young English gentleman, out ’ere fer change iv
scene ’n’ what not. I gave it to Almira strong,
she lookin’ ’er soft ’n’ simple baby mine. ‘ It’s
dem hard,’ I sez, ‘fer ah fellah to be penned in
this beastly country, but what’s ah fellah to do,
bai Jove, when the old gentleman says, “Go
there, sir, and stay there, demmy, till you’re sent
for ? That’s what ah fellah gets for being too
dooced rackety, and serve him dooced well right,
of course. By gad, it’s sobered me, by gad it has.
JJy f’d have been down to it on my bendeds
if Sir Cbawles Jradn't dropped a wemittance now
and again. As it Bye had to take a dem
billet, by gad, and Wgr.k like tally slave seven
hours a day. Bai Jpye, yes. I’m ashamed to
say where I am, positively.' By Jove, yes, by
gad.’ ”
Ihe Don, who was a close student of the drama,
got his idea of the English younger son largely
from certain familiar comedy types, but he had a
talent for dissimulation, and it must not be ima-
gined that meek Almira was necessarily a fool in
reposing some little confidence in Horace Bad-
minton-Carte, and believing in his reformation
and in his expected remittances. The idea was
that dear Horace was to call on Almira frequently,
“ with a view to the above ” when eventually the
remittances became regular, and the black sheep
was restored to parental favor. Meanwhile, they
were to regard themselves as each other’s, for
better or worse. Escapades of this kind were
meat and drink to Nicholas. He followed-up
the meek Almira with assiduity. He devoted
evenings, Sundays, and Saturday afternoons to
the quest. Twice during off-days in the city she
excused him while he darted into a bank to see
the manager respecting an idea he had that “ Sir
Chawles ” might be placing a thousand or two to
his credit. Once he detained her while he held a
cordial conversation wfith the chauffeur of a grand
motor, whom he afterwards described to her as
‘ ‘ dear old Fitzie, you know. Dooced good chap.
His people know my people at home. Beastly
rich—wanted to lend me fifty. Couldn’t think of
it, you know ; couldn’t think of it.”
The little widow found this all very convincing.
She thought Horace’s scruples in refusing to take
the fifty from dear old Fitzie a trifle strained, but
there was certainly wisdom in his heroic determina-
tion to let the governor see he could do without
that sort of thing. The Don’s only doubts were
inspired by Almira’s maid, a pert young thing of
16, who giggled foolishly at him. She also called
him “Percy” when she caught him alone, winked
with insolent familiarity, and said, “Oh, what
sort! ” in a tone that almost made Horace Bad-
minton-Carte forget himself. Horace feared that
he might have met her in some former existence.
It w'as the meek Almira who suggested the
evening at the theatre, and it was she who
financed the venture. She had a new evening
dress, and an early public display of it w'as essen-
tial to her happiness. Horace, fearing a financial
failure, raised objections. “ Fact is, me dear,”
he explained, ‘ ‘ ah fellah’s been peculiarly situ-*
ated. Dem awkward. Yes, by Jove. Fellah’s
had reverses and that sort of thing, by gad. Had
to sell his dwess clothes. Beastly vulgar thing to
do. By gad, yes. But ah fellah had no alterna-
tive—honest poverty, don’t you know.” Horace
seemed deeply moved by the contemplation of his
misfortunes, and the little fat widow was touched,
She shed two distinct tears, and strove to comfort
him with a kiss. She said she had a dress suit
that had been left by the late lamented Berryman.
It was in an excellent state of repair, and it was
at Horace’s service.
So Horace Badminton-Carte and Mrs. Almira
Berryman went to the theatre, Horace insisted
on paying the tram fare. The dress-suit was a
pretty good fit, but smelt suspiciously of pawn-
shops. It was a trifle long in the leg, and full in
the bust, but Horace had no difficulty in keeping
it on. In the coat room he brushed his hair very
flat, and arranged the parting and the curves with
artistic deftness. The effect w'as highly decora-
tive. Horace thrust an expensive red silk hand-
kerchief negligently into the front of his evening
vest. He followed Almira into the circle, with
the bland ease of a man to whom this was rather
a come-down after Vienna and Paris, but who was
prepared to be quite pleasant for all that. “The
pater always had his box, you know,” he told the
meek Almira.
In a conspicuous seat near the front Nicholas
Don flattered himself he was quite at home,
although hitherto when attending the theatre he
had been a god in the galleries. He bought
Almira a shilling box of chocolates from the boy.
He bowed graciously into the thick of the crowd
on the other side of the circle, and made some
show of being concerned on finding Sir John and
Lady Winterton present.
“Dooced annoying,” he said. “Met ’em at
Government House, you know, before the funds
gave out. Fussy old devil, Sir John. Hope he
won’t come dinning ah fellah with his demmed
invitations to dinner, by gad. ”
Almira was radiant to find there were those
present who appreciated Horace at his true social
value. She was a trifle florid in her present
setting, but the Don thought her a particularly
fine woman. It was a proud moment. He spread
himself. His attitudes were graceful. He made
excellent play with the brass ring “all covered
with diamonds,” which, he had explained,
belonged to the Badminton-Carte family jewels,
and was not to be parted with at any price. He
talked familiarly of the people in the boxes. Not
a doubt assailed him, until there came faintly to
his ear a familiar voice from the gallery, which
said:
“’Strewth, Donny, howjer do it?”
Then Horace Badminton-Carte went cold all
over, his flow of small talk was frozen under, and
suddenly the Don realised that his dress suit was
too big for him, that too much linen was falling
out in front, and that his awful night had come.
. “ Tuck in yer washin’, Nickie,” cried the voice,
in graceful allusion to Horace’s superabundance
of shirt. Something hit Nick on the head, and
bounced into the lap of the lady on his right. It
was a peanut.
There is nothing more humiliating to a young
gentleman enjoying a rare interval of high life
and superior refinement with the lady of his
heart than to become the object of loud, vulgar,
and familiar criticism. When discourteous ad-
dress is accompanied by a fusillade of peanuts, the
young gentleman fathoms the depths of human
anguish, and knows utter degradation. For a
moment the Don had a blind idea of springing
up, dashing over or through the intervening
audience, and flying from the scoffers, but pre-
sently his natural and acquired impudence came
to his aid, and he determined to see the matter
through. He leaned gracefully towards the meek
Almira, and entertained her with fluent small
talk. He flourished his toothpick. A ’cone
formed of a twisted programme, weighted with a
grape, shot like a dart from above, smote Don on
the head, and skidded into the corsage of a very
stout lady in front, where the grape remained,
A flush of rage spread all over the visible sur-
face of the stout lady; face, neck, and suburbs
glowed angrily, and she rounded on Horace

Badminton-Carte with an ejaculation far below
her apparent station in life. Horace was dis-
tracted at the unmerited accusation which the
address implied, and sat forward to expostulate.
He wore one of those steel-backed clip ties. The
tie jerked from its moorings, struck the stout
lady sharply on the nose, and fell into the depths,
whence it was hastily dug by the now funous
female, and flung aside. A young man recovered
the tie, and it was courteously returned to
Horace, passing from hand to hand. The inci-
dent was very popular ; everybody seemed de-
lighted.
“ What-o, Nickie, you won’t get nothin’ from
the missus Saturday,” said a voice above a voice
of friendliness touched with commiseration.
“ Doin’ in yer board on choc’lates,” added an-
other voice, in admonitory tones. )
The Don knew what was implied. The night’s
extravagance would mean a painful interval of
apologies and excuses re the overdue 13s- 6d. a
week with the frigid and suspicious landlady of
the home for single gentlemen where he lodged.
His wrath welled up. He was moved to rise and
respond in his own fierce, effective, aboriginal
speech, but at that moment the lights fell, the
curtain rolled up, and the situation was saved.
Horace Badminton-Carte did not enjoy that
first act, and the meek Almira seemed uneasy.
He was afraid her suspicions were aroused, and he
whispered something about the demned lower
orders always being stirred to malice at the sight
of wealth, breeding, and beauty. He wished to
convey the idea that the attack was the outcome
of class prejudice, and was general, not personal.
He thought of going out during the interval, but
feared that a movement on his part would pi ovoice
a demonstration that must identify him as the
boon companion of the ruffians aloft, and it was a
long way to the door. Meanwhile an occasional
peanut dropped on his head, and an occasional
one fell into the recesses of the vast female in
front.
The act was all too short, and when the curtain
came down and the lights shone out again only
two or three men moved for the door. The Don
sat very still, waiting anxiously. He forgot
Almira. The strain was awful, but didn’t last
long.
“Say, Nickie,” called a persuasive voice,
“ won’t yeh come ’n’ ’ave er beer—er long cool-
’n’-juicy?”
“ Garn, don’t lure ’im ! ” said a second, in mock
expostulation.
“ Rats ! ” cried a third. “ What’s beer t’ ther
Don after ther bar’ls iv champagne he’s used ter?”
Then the first voice again, pleadingly : “Nickie,
where did you get them round-the-’ouses ? ”
“ ’N’ that flogger?”
“ ’N’ that little dickie-shirt ? ”
Involuntarily the Don made an effort to tuck in
his shirt. The action called him to himself. He
became extremely attentive to the little widow,
and talked airily. The twenty-fifth peanut
bounced off his head. The Don was not quite
his suave, collected self. Some of his sentences
were in Horace’s speech, some in the rude utter-
ance of Nicholas Don, driver of Spats’ delivery-
van. He was perspiring a little. His hand
trembled. His ipoqo.cle refused to stick in. He
felt that every eye in the theatre was upon him.
Then came the awful voice again in anxious
warning,
“ Don, Don, yer clean fergot t’ hook ther ror
Hire’ docket off yer clobber.”
“ Wait till ther little tom from ther pieplant
gets on to this —false one ! ”
“ ’R Lizzie at ther pickle-mill.”
At this point, the specialist engaged to weed
disorderly persons out of the gallery arrived, and
expostulated with the Don’s friends up aloft. He
said he would bump them down the stairs, and
they said it was too big a trouble for a man with a
face like that, and, in the subsequent argument,
Horace Badminton-Carte escaped attention. But
the badinage was renewed in the second interval,
and the cheerful push above, having run out of
peanuts, collected orange-peel, and dropped it
into Horace’s clothes. Remarks referring to his
humble calling were exchanged in an exasperating
matter-of-fact way. He was cautioned not to
spill the devilled oysters in his lap, as the Hebrew
owner of the dress suit would certainly bankrupt
him with a claim for damages. _
“Wear ’em t’ ther two-up Friday, Ned,” said
Chiller Green.
The Don was wholly himself now, and a bottled
geyser of blazing invective and unholy wrath. A
section of orange fell on his head, and stuck there.
All was forgotten but his wrongs. He arose
before the whole house ; he shook a vengeful fist
aloft.
“Righto, Pinkie,” he cried. “I seen yeh !
I’ll put a skew on your chin Monday, see if I
don’t ! You” _
Plere Almira swung on Nick’s coat-tails, and
dragged him into his seat. The gallery had
broken into howls, shouts, laughter, stamping,
and a roar of barracking ; the dress circle was
horrified and delighted ; a small usher was tapping
at Nick’s shoulder, threatening to eject him ;
Nick was replying that if the minion didn’t get a
shift on, he’d kick the crupper off him. And then
came a violent diversion to the Don’s rescue.
Loud, angry voices sounded from the vestibule,
and suddenly a stoutish man fell into the circle
through the folding doors. The intruder was red,
dishevelled, and somewhat drunken. Pie was
uttering loud threats. Hands from outside clung
to him, other servile hands hastened to thrust him
back. He fell through the folding doors again
and disappeared.
At the sight of this man the meek Almira had
started up, and uttered a cry of distress. She sat
white and trembling while echoes of the argument
drifted in from the crush-room. She said she
was feeling ill. Then came the blessed darkness,
and the play was resumed. Happily, there were
only three acts, and when tlie curtain fell Don
and his little widow mingled with the escaping
crowd, yearning for the night air and the sweet
oblivion of back streets. But their trials were not
over. In the crush-room a large man, an angry man,
breathing whisky and bad words, arose from a
settee, and lurched at Nicholas Don. It was the
man who had fallen through the folding-doors
Almira screamed and buried herself in the press of
people.
“ Tha’s him,'’ cried the inebriate. “ Tha s the
chap ! He’s go’ my clo’s on. Gimme me clo s,
y’ scoundrel! ” , , , „„ A
The evening-dress crowd backed away, ancl
made a ring. Don was again the centre of interest.
The drunken stranger slid down, and hung on to
his trousers. He tried with violence to pull them
off. ‘ ‘ Gimme me clo’s !” he gurgled. hatcher
mean be stealin’ a man’s browsers?”
“ Who th'ell ’re you?” said Nicholas.
“ I’m ther man whosh clo’s yer wearin’, yeh
bla’guard!” gurgled the stranger. “Gimme me
There was laughter and excitement and talk of
police. The man had risen, and he barred the
way. Nicholas saw red. He drew his foe with
a feint, shot a left in on his neck, and the claimant
of the clothes went down hard, skidded on the
pile carpet, and shot under the settee. Then
Horace Badminton-Carte broke the ring and
bolted, hatless and without his overcoat. ihe
meek Almira had disappeared. Nicholas turned
to the left and ran for the slum streets, hunted by
the terror of the ignominy of being arrested in
another man’s clothes—if, indeed, it were true
that the lamented Berryman had resurrected.
In a frowsy but familiar bar parlor Don re-
viewed the situation over a consolatory beer. He
was abroad in the dyed garments of a total
stranger, pursued by the law perhaps ; his own
suit was in the little spare bedroom at the abode
of Almira ; his hat and coat were in the cloak-
room of the theatre. If the law were seeking him
the widow’s cottage was no safe place. He must
proceed with caution. He had another beer.
Three-quarters of an hour later Nicholas, wearing
a dress suit, a staring white shirt, and an old soft
felt hat—a villainous thing that had been at the
service of the cat for weeks, but the only one the
hotel wo»ld trust him with —stole down the dim,
suburban street to Almira’s garden gate.
A stout man was staggering about on the
verandah, brandishing his hands in the air, and
clamoring in drunken speech for his clothes. Ihe
house was in darkness, but curious neighbors were
hanging their heads out of adjacent houses oner-
in<>- loud advice, and all deeply interested in the
grievance of the stout man, who rattled at
Almira’s windows, pounded on her door, and
filled the night with piteous cries for his clothes.
Don ducked down a side street, found another
hotel, and stayed till kick-out, taking counsel
with long, strong drink. Then he stole back to
Almira’s cottage again. The heads were all gone,
but the stout man was sitting on the mat, with
his back to the front door, drowsing, and making
weak lamentation. Nicholas was wondering
whether it would be possible to steal by m the
gloom, and effect an entrance by the back door,
when the outcast on guard put up a howl of great
anguish, and started battering at the door again.
“ Lemme in !” he cried. “ Lemme get at the
villain who stole me clo’s ! ” Then his voice
broke, and he continued piteously, “ Oh, JYI ner
how could yeh—how could yeh ? ” He advanced
to the edge of the verandah, and addressed an
imaginary audience in tones of poignant sorrow.
“Lays ’n’ shennlemen, I’m a man o’ sorrer .
Englishman’s clo’s is his cashtle ”
Nicholas Don left him listening, and stole
away again, and talked in the cold, dark, damp
morning to his home in the distant suburb on t. e
other side of the city. He held long arguments
with policemen by the way—policemen whose
suspicions were aroused by the incongruity of his
hat and his costume. It is easy to arouse the
suspicions of policemen at 2 o’clock in the morn-
ing. On Sunday Nicholas could not venture out,
because he had only an old working spit, and the
evening clobber of a perfect stranger. On
Monday night he recovered his. overcoat and hat
at the theatre, and journeyed to Alffiira’s Ugam-.
From the door he heard the beery, melancholy
voice of the stout man within, and he was sttl|
uttering bitter reproaches about the absent clothes,
and so the person who had once been Horace
Badminton-Carte retired without knocking.
On Tuesday he discovered an advertisement ip
the “ Missing Friends” column, in which H- -R-:
C. was informed that if he appeared at go,on gt
the corner where he first met A. B. parcels might
be exchanged. Nick rolled the unfortuggte
evening suit in brown paper, and took it tq work
with him. He managed with some difficulty tq
be at the corner mentioned at noon, with tge
delivery van. Almira’s servant was waiting,
nursing a parcel.
“ What-o, Percy ! ” she cried, “. s that you ?
“ No,” growled Nicholas, “ but it s me nearest
’n’ dearest survivin’ relation. Them my soft-
goods, Sissie ? ”
Sissie nodded. “ But you gotter part up first.
I ain’t t’ be done in.” f
The Don threw down his parcel, and alter
Sissie had torn the paper and satisfied her nund
she tossed the other bundle into the van. “ Strike
me, you’re a pretty pair o’ take-downs,” she said,
“ you : n’ mar. ’
“Me ’n’ mar!” cried Don. “Gam, you
ain’t chattin’ that ther meek Almira’s your mar.
Well, I am! : N’ I s’pose ther bloke was ther
ole pot-’n’-pan ? ”
“ Yes—he’s dad. Mum’s got a legal separate,
but he comes round sometimes when ’e’s drunk
’n’ lovin’. An’ mar passed me off as the girl, for
fear your rich relations mightn’t like me. How s
Sir Charles?”
“’N’ you put the old man on t us at tner
theatre Saterdee ? ” , ,
Sissie grinned. “ Tor’ strike yeh cock-eyed or
it,” said Nicholas, bitterly.
That evening Nick encountered Pinkie in Fgg
Lane, and they fought three rounds, to their
great mutual disadvantage. The conclusion was
postponed. Nicholas did not open his parcel till
he returned home, and then he found, to his
astonishment, that it contained some old rags, a
note, and a pawn ticket for his best suit. 1 ne
note said : — T t a ,
“This is the best I can do for you. Had to
pawn your clothes to keep George drunk till I got
his own back. You are an impudent impostor.
Farewell.— Almira.”
Nicholas sat down. “’Smith!' he gasped.
“I do like that. Swelp me Jimmy Gee, 1 do
like that 1 ” And then for five minutes Nick sat in
a rigid attitude, staring blankly at the pawn ticket.
